Mishneh Torah, Tefillin, Mezuzah and the Torah Scroll



Chapter 1

Four passages [of the Torah]:<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">contain references to the mitzvah of <i>tefillin</i>.</i> <i>Kadesh Li</i> and <i>V'hayah ki y'viacha Ado-nai</i> in the book of Exodus  13:1-10 and<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">The commentaries suggest that, in this instance, the Rambam cited the source for these passages because there is also a passage in the book of Deuteronomy that begins <i>V'hayah Ki Y'viacha</i>.</i> 13:11-16) and <i>Shema</i> and <i>V'hayah im shamo'a</i> (Deuteronomy 6:4-9) and<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sefer HaChinuch</i> (Mitzvah 422) explains that these four passages were chosen because they include the concepts of the oneness of God, the acceptance of His yoke and the yoke of His commandments, and the description of the exodus from Egypt. These are fundamental principles of the Jewish faith.</i> 11:13-21) should be written separately<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">on parchment</i> and covered with leather.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">as described in Chapter 3.</i> They are called <i>tefillin</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Tosafot, Menachot</i> 34b states the word is related to the root ללפ, which means "dispute." The <i>Tur</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 25) inteprets the word as meaning "sign." <i>Tefillot Yisrael</i> associates the term with the power of thought, citing Genesis 48:11. The <i>Pri Megadim</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 25:20) understands the term as meaning, "mark of distinction."</i><br>They are placed on the head and tied<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">The commentaries note the difference between the two verbs, "placed" and "tied." See also our commentary on Chapter 4, Halachah 4. It is, nevertheless, worth noting that in the listing of the mitzvot in the introduction to the <i>Mishneh Torah</i>, the Rambam mentions "tying the <i>tefillin</i> on the head."</i> on the arm.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sefer HaMitzvot</i> (Positive Commandments 12 and 13) and <i>Sefer HaChinuch</i> (Mitzvot 421 and 422) consider these obligations to be two of the Torah's 613 mitzvot. (See <i>Menachot</i> 44a.) Since they are two separate mitzvot, the performance of one is not dependent on the performance of the other, as stated in Chapter 4, Halachah 4.</i> According to Torah law, even a mere point of one of the letters<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">for example, the <i>yud</i> has a short foot in its lower right-hand corner. If that foot is missing, it is considered to have been improperly formed. Therefore, not only it, but the entire passage is not acceptable.</i> from these four passages prevents all of them from being acceptable. All four must be written in the proper manner.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">If even one of the passages is not written in the proper manner, the person is not considered to have fulfilled the mitzvah at all. (See also the Rambam's Commentary on the Mishnah, <i>Menachot</i> 3:7.)<br>Accordingly, it is proper to have one's <i>tefillin</i> checked from time to time to make sure that none of the letters have faded. (See the commentary on Chapter 2, Halachah 11.)</i>
Similarly, if even one letter of the two passages<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">which mention the mitzvah and, hence, are</i> contained in the <i>mezuzah</i>, <i>Shema</i> and <i>V'hayah im shamo'a</i> (Deuteronomy 6:4-9 and 11:13-21), is lacking a point,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">from its proper shape,</i> it is not acceptable according to Torah law, which requires that they [each] be written in a perfect manner.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">In this instance, as well, a disqualifying factor in a single point of a single letter prevents the entire mitzvah from being fulfilled.</i> Similarly, a Torah scroll which is lacking even one letter<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Ben Yedid</i> explains that the difference in the phraseology used by the Rambam with regard to <i>tefillin</i> and mezuzot ("lacking a point") and a Torah scroll ("lacking even one letter") alludes to the difference in the phraseology used by the sources for these laws (<i>Menachot</i> 34a and <i>Bava Batra</i> 15a).</i> is unacceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Though Rabbenu Nissim (<i>Megillah</i>, Chapter 2) appears to contest the Rambam's statements on this issue, the Rambam's view is accepted by most authorities. (See also Chapter 7, Halachah 9.)</i>
There are ten requirements for <i>tefillin</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Though all of the ten principles mentioned by the Rambam have their source in the Talmud, their organization into a list of ten is original.</i> All of them are halachot transmitted to Moses on Mount Sinai.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yad Malachi</i> interprets the latter expression as a law which has all the authority of a Torah commandment, even though there is no allusion to it in the Written Law. (See also the Rambam's Introduction to his Commentary on the Mishnah, Chapter 4.)</i> It is necessary to fulfill them all. Therefore, if one made any changes with regard to them, the <i>tefillin</i> are not fit for use:<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">A person who wears <i>tefillin</i> which do not fulfill these requirements is not considered to have fulfilled the mitzvah at all.</i> Two of them involve their composition,<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">These are listed below and discussed in the following halachot.</i> and eight involve the coverings [placed around the passages] and the tying of their straps.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">These eight conditions are mentioned in Chapter 3, Halachah 1, and discussed in the subsequent halachot of that chapter.</i><br>These are the two that involve their composition:<br>a) They must be written in ink;<br>b)<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">as opposed to other tints</i><sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">This translation is not precise. Note Halachah 7 for a more specific definition of the term ףלק.</i> They must be written on parchment.
How is ink prepared? One collects the vapor of oils,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Shabbat</i> 23a states that it is preferable to use olive oil.</i> of tar, of wax, or the like, [causes it to condense,]<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Shabbat</i>, ibid., and commentaries, where it is explained that they would heat the above substances and hold a glass above, upon which the vapors would condense. Afterwards, the soot would be collected.</i> and kneads it together with sap from a tree<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Shabbat</i>, ibid., states that it is preferable to use balsam sap.</i> and a drop of honey. It is moistened extensively,<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">In one of his responsa, the Rambam writes that it is customarily moistened with oil.</i> crushed until it is formed into flat cakes, dried, and then stored.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Niddah</i> 20a teaches us that their ink was stored while dry.</i><br>When one desires to write with it, one soaks [the cakes of ink]<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">From the statement in <i>Shabbat</i> forbidding ink to be soaked on the Sabbath, we can assume that the normal process is to soak it before using it.</i> in gallnut juice<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">which endows the ink with a lasting quality. In contrast, the Rambam writes in one of his responsa that if one were to soak the ink in water, it would fade rapidly.<br>In the same responsum, he states that a liquid possessing qualities similar to gallnut juice can be obtained from pomegranate shells or the outer shells of other nuts.</i> or the like and writes with it. Thus, if one attempts to rub it out, he would be able to.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sotah</i> 20a derives this concept from Numbers 5:23, which states, "He shall write and he shall blot out." Proper ink should be able to be blotted out after writing.</i><br>This is the ink with which it is most preferable to write scrolls, <i>tefillin</i>, and mezuzot.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Note the <i>Zohar</i> (Vol. II, p. 159a), which requires that ink be made from substances that are derived from the plant kingdom.</i> If, however, one wrote any of the three<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Torah scrolls, <i>tefillin</i>, and mezuzot.</i> with gallnut juice<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">itself, and not in a mixture with other substances</i> or vitriol,<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">a substance produced by the rusting of metal. Our translation is based on Rav Kapach's commentary which has its source in the Rambam's responsa. Others render this phrase "with gallnut juice and vitriol," implying that the two substances should be combined.</i> which remains without being rubbed out, it is acceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">after the fact (בדיעבד).</i>
If so,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., if there are no specific substances which are excluded, as stated above</i> what was excluded by the halachah conveyed to Moses on Mount Sinai, which stated that it be written in ink?<br>It excludes tints of other colors, such as red, green,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Megillah</i> 17a explicitly disqualifies a <i>megillah</i> written in red ink. From that and other sources, it appears that such ink was frequently used at that time, but was deemed unacceptable for use for a Torah scroll.</i> and the like.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Note the <i>Bi'ur Halachah</i> (32), which states that even blue ink is unacceptable.</i> If even one letter of a Torah scroll, <i>tefillin</i>, or mezuzot is in another tint or in gold,<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Soferim</i> 1:9 relates that a Torah scroll was written for Alexander with every one of the names of God written in gold. When the Sages heard about this, they said that the scroll should be entombed.<br>Besides excluding the use of gold-colored ink, the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 32:3) explains that covering the ink with gold dust is not acceptable.</i> they are invalid.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">From this, we learn that all black inks are acceptable, regardless of their makeup. Though scribes continue to make ink in the traditional fashion, there is no prohibition against using commercially produced black ink.</i>
There are three types of parchment: <i>g'vil</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">whose preparation is described in this halachah.</i> <i>k'laf</i>, and <i>duchsustos</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">whose preparation is described in the following halachah.</i><br>What is implied? The hide of a domesticated or wild animal is taken.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">As mentioned in Halachah 10, the animal must be kosher.</i> First, the hair is removed from it.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Shulchan Aruch HaRav</i> (32:10) states that if the hair is not entirely removed, the parchment is not fit to be used. Even after the fact, it is unacceptable. The <i>Mishnah Berurah</i> quotes this opinion as well, but also mentions other opinions, which do not invalidate the parchment if some hair remains, as long as it had been placed in lime for a long enough time for thehair to fall off.</i> Afterwards, it is salted and then prepared with flour. Then resin and other substances which cause the skin to contract and become harder<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 32:8) mentions that lime is customarily used for this purpose.</i> are applied to it. In this state, it is called <i>g'vil</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">In one of his responsa, the Rambam explains that the term <i>g'vil</i> is used to describe any coarse, uneven surface. Note a parallel usage in <i>Bava Batra</i> 3a.<br>If the hide is not prepared in this fashion, it is referred to as <i>diftera</i>, and a Torah scroll, <i>tefillin</i>, or <i>mezuzah</i>, written upon it is invalid. Even בדיעבד (after the fact), such parchment is not acceptable, as stated in Halachah 14.</i>
After the hair is removed, the hide may be taken and divided in half in the manner known to the parchment processors.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">There is, however, no obligation to divide the parchment. If it has been processed correctly, it may be used for a Torah scroll without being separated, as stated in the following halachah.</i> Thus, there are two pieces of parchment: a thin one, which is on the side where the hair<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">or wool of the animal</i> grew, and a thicker one, on the side of the flesh.<br>After it has been processed using salt, then flour, and then resin and the like,<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Several of the Rambam's responsa were addressed to communities where it was not customary to prepare hides in this manner. In these letters, the Rambam stresses that even after the hair is removed from the hide, it must be processed in this manner. Otherwise, it may not be used for either a Torah scroll, <i>tefillin</i>, or <i>mezuzah</i>.</i> the portion on the side where the hair grew is called <i>k'laf</i><sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">which means "peel." It is given this name because it is a thin layer of flesh which is peeled easily.</i> and the portion on the side of the flesh is called <i>duchsustos</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Duch</i> is Aramaic for "place," and <i>sustos</i> is Greek (Median in other texts) for "meat." Thus, the word means "the place of the meat" (<i>Aruch</i>).<br>Our translation and commentary follows the standard published text of the <i>Mishneh Torah</i>, which is quoted in the <i>Shulchan Aruch, Orach Chayim</i> 32:7. It must, nevertheless, be emphasized that the authoritative Yemenite manuscripts of the <i>Mishneh Torah</i> reverse the interpretations of <i>k'laf</i> and <i>duchsustos</i>. The latter interpretation is reinforced by several responsa of the Rambam on the subject. It also is supported by <i>Hilchot Shabbat</i> 11:6. The Rashba's text of the <i>Mishneh Torah</i> also contained this version, as is obvious from his responsa (Vol. 1, Responsum 579).</i>
It is a halachah transmitted to Moses on Mount Sinai that a Torah scroll should be written on <i>g'vil</i><sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">From <i>Bava Batra</i> 14b, it appears that Moses wrote the Torah scroll which he placed in the holy ark on <i>g'vil</i>. Similarly, <i>Gittin</i> 54b mentions that Torah scrolls would be written on <i>g'vil</i>.</i> on the side on which the hair had grown.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">This side is smoother and better to write on.</i> When <i>tefillin</i> are written on <i>k'laf</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam's expression is somewhat inexact, because it is permissible to write <i>tefillin</i> only on <i>k'laf</i>. If they are written on <i>g'vil</i> or <i>duchsustos</i>, they are unacceptable (<i>Shabbat</i> 79b).</i> they should be written on the side of the flesh.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., on the portion of the <i>k'laf</i> where it is separated from the <i>duchsustos</i>.</i> When a <i>mezuzah</i> is written on<i>duchsustos</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">As stated in the following halachah, it is preferable - but not obligatory - to write a <i>mezuzah</i> on this type of parchment.</i> it should be written on the side of the hair.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., on the portion of the <i>duchsustos</i> where it is separated from the <i>k'laf</i>.</i><br>Whenever one writes on <i>k'laf</i> on the side of the hair<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">the epidermis</i> or on <i>g'vil</i> or <i>duchsustos</i> on the side of the flesh,<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">the side facing the inside of the body</i> it is unacceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Aruch</i> states that this practice is alluded to by Proverbs 25:2: "The glory of God is in the concealment of a matter." Only "the concealment of a matter," the sides of the parchment which are naturally concealed, are fit to be used for "the glory of God," the fulfillment of mitzvot.<br>It must be emphasized that the Yemenite manuscripts mentioned in the previous halachah, which reverse the definitions of <i>k'laf</i> and <i>duchsustos</i>, do not change the text of this halachah. Thus, according to these texts, when writing on the thin upper parchment, one should write on the epidermis, and when writing on the thick lower parchment, one should write on the part facing the inside of the body.<br>There is an advantage to this version. According to the standard texts, there is an apparent contradiction. Although one should write on the epidermis when writing on <i>g'vil</i>, it is improper to do so when writing on <i>k'laf</i>.</i>
Although it is a halachah which was transmitted to Moses on Mount Sinai, that if one wrote a Torah scroll on <i>k'laf</i>, it is acceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">The entire scroll, however, must be written on one or the other of these types of parchment. If half was written on <i>k'laf</i> and half on <i>g'vil</i>, it is unacceptable (Chapter 7, Halachah 4; <i>Shulchan Aruch, Yoreh De'ah</i> 271:4).</i> <i>G'vil</i> was mentioned only to exclude <i>duchsustos</i>. If a Torah scroll was written on the latter, it is not acceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Though this concept is not mentioned explicitly in the Talmud, the rabbis derive it from their understanding of <i>Bava Batra</i> 14b.</i><br>Similarly, if a <i>mezuzah</i> was written on <i>k'laf</i> or on <i>g'vil</i>, it is acceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Even in Talmudic times, <i>Shabbat</i> 79b mentions that on occasion it was preferable to write a <i>mezuzah</i> on <i>k'laf</i>, because the <i>mezuzah</i> would be preserved better than if it were written on <i>duchsustos</i>.</i> <i>Duchsustos</i> was mentioned only as a mitzvah.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., it is preferable to do it in this manner.<br>It must be noted that at present, a single type of parchment is used for all three sacred objects. The parchment is not separated in two. A thin portion is removed from the upper layer, and the majority of the - if not the entire - lower layer is rubbed off. The parchment produced in this manner is more attractive and also lighter (a factor significant with regard to a Torah scroll. (See <i>Shulchan Aruch</i>, <i>Orach Chayim</i> 32:7; Ramah, <i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 271:3, 288:6; <i>Siftei Cohen</i>, <i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 271:9.)</i>
[Torah] scrolls, <i>tefillin</i>, and mezuzot<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Shulchan Aruch HaRav</i> 32:14 expands the scope of this law, stating that it is not befitting to write the name of God on parchment from a non-kosher animal. This applies even when the intent is not to use the parchment for a sacred purpose.</i> may not be written on hide from a non-kosher animal, fowl, or wild animal.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Shabbat</i> 108a derives this concept from the exegesis of Exodus 13:9, "So that the Torah of God will be in your mouths." They conclude: Only what is permitted to be "in your mouths" - i.e., species which are kosher - is acceptable for a Torah scroll. Even בדיעבד (after the fact), such parchment is not acceptable, as stated in Halachah 14.<br>As explained in Chapter 3, this requirement applies not only to the parchment, but also to all the other elements involved in making these sacred articles.</i> One may write on the hides of [all] kosher animals, wild beasts, and fowl.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">See Halachah 20 in regard to writing on parchment made from the skin of birds.</i> This applies even when these animals died without being ritually slaughtered or when they were killed by wild beasts.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Shabbat</i> (ibid.) offers the following parable to explain why even though such animals may not be eaten, it is permitted to use their hides. There were two people who were sentenced to death. One was executed by the king himself and the other by the executioner. Which is more noteworthy? Obviously, the one whom the king executed himself.<br>Similarly, in the present instance, the fact that these kosher animals were "executed by the king" - i.e., their death came about at God's decree - distinguishes them from other animals which are never fit to be eaten.</i><br>We may not write on the skin of a kosher fish because of the foul secretions, since the processing of the skin will not cause the foul secretions to cease.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hilchot Keilim</i> 1:4 states: "The skin of a fish is not susceptible to contracting ritual impurity and would be fit for <i>tefillin</i> to be written upon, were it not for its foul secretions." This implies that the only problem is the secretions of the fish skin.</i>
The <i>g'vil</i> for a Torah scroll and the <i>k'laf</i> for <i>tefillin</i> and for a Torah scroll must be processed with this purpose in mind.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 32:8; <i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 271:1) states that when placing the skin in lime at the beginning of the process of making it into parchment, one should explicitly say that one is processing it for these purposes.<br><i>Shulchan Aruch HaRav</i> (32:11) and the <i>Mishnah Berurah</i> (32:26-27) explain that parchment processed to be used as a Torah scroll may be used for <i>tefillin</i> (or for a <i>mezuzah</i>). Parchment processed for the sake of <i>tefillin</i>, however, may not be used for a Torah scroll, because a Torah scroll is on a higher level of holiness.</i> If they were not processed with this intent, they are not acceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Even if no parchment that was processed with the proper intent is available, this parchment should not be used, as stated in Halachah 14.</i><br>Accordingly, if they were processed by a gentile, they are not acceptable. Even when [a Jew] instructed a gentile to process the parchment with the intent that it be used for a Torah scroll or for <i>tefillin</i>, it is not acceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Even if the gentile states that he is processing the parchment with the intent that it be used for these purposes, we do not rely on his word, because we suspect that</i> The gentile follows his own intentions and not those of the person who hires him.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Note the comments of Rav Chayim Soloveitchik, who approaches the concept differently, and explains that the above phrase, "the gentile follows his own intentions...," is a description of the gentile's fundamental nature. It is not that we suspect that the gentile will not listen to the person who hires him, and his act is therefore not acceptable. Rather, because his nature is to "follow his own intentions," he is disqualified from performing any act that requires a sacred intention.<br>According to this interpretation, the question of whether a gentile's actions are accepted if he is supervised by a Jew must be understood as follows: The act of preparing the parchment for a Torah scroll is not, in and of itself, a sacred act, but merely a preparation for performing such an act. Accordingly, one might assume that it is sufficient for a Jew to command the gentile to process the scroll for the proper intent. The gentile would be considered like a machine which performs activities with no will of its own, and it would be as if the Jew performed the act himself. In conclusion, however, it is accepted that because the gentile acts independently, the intention of the Jew who hires him cannot be associated with his acts.</i> Therefore,<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">the scope of this ruling can be expanded:</i> whenever an article must be made with a specific intent in mind,<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">e.g., the strands of tzitzit (<i>Hilchot Tzitzit</i> 1:11) or a bill of divorce (<i>Hilchot Gerushin</i> 3:16)</i> it is unacceptable if made by a gentile.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Rabbenu Asher does not accept the Rambam's ruling with regard to these parchments. Though he accepts the general principle, he explains that this particular instance is an exception.<br>In other instances, the gentile must act according to the Jew's intent for a prolonged period of time. Here, it is absolutely necessary to have the intent to use the parchment for <i>tefillin</i> or a Torah scroll only at the moment it is placed in the lime. We can assume that if a gentile is given instructions to place it in the lime with that intent, he will do so with that intent.<br>The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> quotes both opinions in <i>Orach Chayim</i> 32:9. <i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 271:1, however, mentions only the Rambam's opinion. In both places, the Ramah states that it is customary to follow Rabbenu Asher's opinion.<br><i>Shulchan Aruch HaRav</i> 32:12 and the <i>Mishnah Berurah</i> 32:28-29 quote Rabbenu Asher's statements that a Jew should be present at the time the parchment is placed in the lime and that he should explicitly tell the gentile to put it in the lime to be used for a Torah scroll. Afterwards, he should assist the gentile somewhat in the process of preparing the parchment.</i><br>[The parchment used for] a <i>mezuzah</i> need not be processed with this purpose in mind.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">The Jerusalem Talmud (<i>Yoma</i> 3:6) states, "Parchment which was processed for use as a talisman is acceptable for a <i>mezuzah</i>."<br>In one of his responsa, the Rambam explains that the reason for the difference in law between a <i>mezuzah</i>, on the one hand, and a Torah scroll and <i>tefillin</i>, on the other, stems from the fact that a Torah scroll and <i>tefillin</i> are mitzvot which each person is obligated to fulfill. In contrast, the mitzvah of <i>mezuzah</i> is an obligation that is not incumbent on a person unless he dwells in a house that requires one. (It must be noted, however, that there are opinions which maintain that this responsum was not written by the Rambam.)<br>This ruling is not accepted by the other authorities. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 288:5) states that לכתחילה (at the outset), one must seek a <i>mezuzah</i> that was written on parchment processed with this intention in mind. Only if it is impossible to find such a <i>mezuzah</i>, may one use a <i>mezuzah</i> which was not processed with this intent. (Note also our commentary on Chapter 3, Halachah 15.)</i>
It is a halachah transmitted to Moses on Mount Sinai that a Torah scroll or <i>mezuzah</i> should be written only [on parchment] which has been ruled.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">The parchment should be ruled with a stylus or a reed. It is improper to use a substance that leaves a mark. Each line of the parchment should be ruled and a border made on both sides. If unruled parchment is used for a Torah scroll or for a <i>mezuzah</i>, it is unacceptable (<i>Shulchan Aruch, Yoreh De'ah</i> 271:5, 288:8).</i> [The parchment used for] <i>tefillin</i>, however, need not be ruled,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 32:6) states the top line of <i>tefillin</i> must be ruled because the Sages forbade writing more than three words from a verse from the Bible without ruling the line above them. See Chapter 7, Halachah 16, and also see the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 284:2).<br>In <i>Orach Chayim</i>, the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> continues, explaining that a person who cannot write on a straight line without ruling the parchment should rule the parchment before writing <i>tefillin</i>. The Ramah states that it is customary for even the most competent scribes to write <i>tefillin</i> on ruled parchment. If, however, one wrote <i>tefillin</i> without ruling the parchment, the <i>tefillin</i> are acceptable even if the lines are crooked (<i>Mishneh Berurah</i> 32:21).</i> because they are covered.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">This explains why although a verse from the Torah must always be written on ruled parchment, we are not required to do so for <i>tefillin</i>.<br>Even though a <i>mezuzah</i> is also covered, ruling the parchment is required because it can be removed from its covering easily and must be checked twice in seven years. In contrast, there is no obligation to check <i>tefillin</i> and the parchments are almost never removed from their compartments (<i>Kessef Mishneh</i>, Rabbenu Nissim).</i><br>It is permissible to write <i>tefillin</i> and mezuzot without [looking at] an existent text, because everyone is familiar with these passages.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 32:20, 29), however, emphasizes that one should carefully check the passages of the <i>tefillin</i> to make sure that they were written correctly. One who does not know the passages by heart should write from an existent text.</i> It is, however, forbidden to write even one letter of a Torah scroll without [looking at] an existent text.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Even a person who knows the passages by heart may err, because sometimes the spellings of words are different from their pronunciations (<i>Megillah</i> 18b).<br>The <i>Siftei Cohen</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 274:3) quotes a difference of opinion among the Rabbis if, after the fact, it is permitted to use a Torah scroll that was not written from an existent text.</i>
A Torah scroll, <i>tefillin</i>, or <i>mezuzah</i> written by an <i>apikoros</i><sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">The phraseology used in this halachah is a matter of question. Though our texts follow the standard published texts of the <i>Mishneh Torah</i>, the authoritative manuscripts substitute the word <i>min</i> for <i>apikoros</i>.<br>The difference between the two versions is significant. In <i>Hilchot Teshuvah</i> 3:8, the Rambam defines an <i>apikoros</i> as a person who denies the Torah and/or the prophetic tradition, while in <i>Hilchot Teshuvah</i> 3:7, he describes a <i>min</i> as a person who does not believe in God. When a <i>min</i> writes a Torah scroll, the names of God it contains do not possess any holiness, because he does not believe in God at all. In contrast, since an <i>apikoros</i> does believe in God, were he to write a Torah scroll the names he writes would possess a certain dimension of holiness.</i> should be burned.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Hilchot Yesodei Torah</i> 6:8, the Rambam explains why burning such a scroll does not violate the prohibition against destroying God's name:<br>He does not believe in the sanctity of [God's] name and did not compose it for a sacred purpose. Rather, he considers this to be similar to any other text. Since this is his intent, the names [of God he writes] do not become holy.<br>Significantly, the Rambam does not state that we presume that the scribe had the intention that the name of God refer to a false deity. Note, however, the Rambam's statements in <i>Hilchot Avodat Kochavim</i> 2:5: "[It can be assumed that] a <i>min's</i> thoughts are concerned with false gods." <i>Shulchan Aruch HaRav</i> 39:4 and the <i>Mishnah Berurah</i> 39:13 refer to the latter statement and explain that the passages should be burned, because the names of God refer to a false deity.<br><i>Shabbat</i> 116a draws a parallel between the burning of such a Torah scroll and the scroll of a <i>sotah</i> (a woman accused of adultery). In the process of a <i>sotah's</i> trial, a scroll on which is written a passage containing God's name is blotted out. Our Sages conclude that just as God is willing to allow His name to be wiped out to establish peace between a man and his wife, so, too, He allows His name to be destroyed because of these individuals who disturb the peace that exists between Him and His people.</i> If they were written by a gentile, an apostate Jew, a person who betrays [the Jews]<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">or Jewish property</i> to a powerful person,<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">It was quite common in the Second Temple period for Jews to betray their countrymen or their property to the Roman authorities. The severity of this transgression is emphasized by the Rambam, who includes a <i>moseir</i> in the 24 categories of individuals who do not have a portion in the world to come. (See <i>Hilchot Teshuvah</i> 3:6,12.)<br>Similarly, in <i>Hilchot Chovel UMazik</i> 8:10, the Rambam states that a <i>moseir</i> may be killed to prevent him from betraying a Jew's life or property to gentiles. Because of the severity of this transgression, the Rambam considers such an individual equivalent to an outright nonbeliever.</i> a slave, a woman, or a minor,<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">This includes even a minor who has reached the age when he is trained to wear <i>tefillin</i> (<i>Shulchan Aruch, Orach Chayim</i> 39:1).</i> they are not acceptable<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">for use</i> and must be entombed,<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">lest they be used. (See <i>Turei Zahav, Yoreh De'ah</i> 281:1.) Since there is a possibility that the names of God they contain were written with the proper intent (and hence, they would possess a dimension of holiness), they are not burned.</i> as [implied by Deuteronomy 6:8-9]: "And you shall tie...<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote"><i>tefillin</i></i> and you shall write."<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">a <i>mezuzah</i>.</i> [Our Sages<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Gittin</i> 45b</i> explain that this includes only] those who are commanded to tie [<i>tefillin</i> on their arms]<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">Thus excluding slaves, women and minors, as stated in Chapter 4, Halachah 13.</i> and those who believe in what they write.<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">Thus excluding Jews who do not believe in their heritage, gentiles, and <i>mosrim</i>.<br>The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 39:2) states that anyone who is disqualified from writing <i>tefillin</i> is also disqualified from performing any other activity necessary to prepare them.</i><br>[Sacred articles]<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">Torah scrolls, <i>tefillin</i>, or mezuzot</i> which are found in the possession of an <i>apikoros</i>, and it is not known who wrote them,<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">Since it is possible that the <i>apikoros</i> did not write them himself, they may not be burned. They, however,</i> should be entombed.<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">and may not be used for sacred purposes, because it is possible that the <i>apikoros</i> (or one of his colleagues) wrote them.</i> Those which are found in the possession of a gentile<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">more precisely, the term <i>kuti</i> used by the Rambam means "Samaritan." At one point, the Samaritans converted and were considered to be Jews by the Sages. Towards the latter portion of the Second Temple period, it was discovered that they had remained idolaters. From that time onward, they were regarded as gentiles by the Sages, and the term <i>kuti</i> was used to refer to gentiles. (It must be noted that the authoritative manuscripts of the <i>Mishneh Torah</i> use the term <i>goy</i>, "gentile," rather than <i>kuti</i>.)<br><b>are kosher. -</b> We presume that the gentile is offering sacred articles which he obtained from a Jew, and he did not make them himself.<br>The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 39:6, <i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 281:1) quotes this opinion. <i>Yoreh De'ah</i> (<i>loc. cit.</i>), however, also mentions a conflicting opinion, which forbids scrolls possessed by gentiles to be used. The <i>Mishnah Berurah</i> 39:16 explains that, at present, we can assume that the sacred articles are acceptable, because today a gentile would not know how to make them himself. Accordingly, we can assume that they were taken from a Jew.<br>Even the opinions which do not allow the sacred articles purchased from the gentiles to be used require that they be redeemed and entombed, because of our regard for the sacred articles and our fear that the gentiles would treat them sacrilegiously.<br><b>We should not, however, purchase Torah scrolls, <i>tefillin</i>, or mezuzot from gentiles for more than they are worth -</b> The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 39:7; <i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 281:1) suggests paying slightly more than their worth.<br><b>so that they do not become accustomed to stealing them. -</b> The Sages feared that if the Jews redeemed these sacred articles from the gentiles at more than their market value, the gentiles would make special efforts to steal them in order to receive these higher prices.<br><i>Gittin</i> 45a establishes a similar principle with regard to human captives, stating, "Captives should not be redeemed for more than their worth."</i> are kosher. We should not, however, purchase Torah scrolls, <i>tefillin</i>, or mezuzot from gentiles for more than they are worth, so that they do not become accustomed to stealing them.
A Torah scroll, <i>tefillin</i>, or <i>mezuzah</i> that was written on parchment from a non-kosher animal, beast, or fowl,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">See Halachah 10.</i> or on parchment that was not processed [properly, is not acceptable].<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">See Halachot 6-7.</i> [Similarly,] a Torah scroll or <i>tefillin</i> that was written on parchment that was not processed with the intent to use it for these sacred purposes is not acceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">See Halachah 11.</i>
When a person writes a Torah scroll, <i>tefillin</i>, or <i>mezuzah</i> without having [the proper] intention,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 274:1) states that before a scribe writes a Torah scroll, he should state that he is writing it for the sake of the holiness of a Torah scroll. Making that statement at the outset is sufficient for the entire Torah scroll. If he fails to make this statement, the scroll is not acceptable. Similar rules apply to <i>tefillin</i> (<i>Shulchan Aruch, Orach Chayim</i> 32:19) and mezuzot.<br>Rav Chayim Soloveitchik notes that this is not the Rambam's view. The phrasing of this halachah clearly implies that the Rambam does not consider the lack of proper intention when writing a Torah scroll sufficient to render it unacceptable. Thus, with regard to preparing the parchment, one is required to have the intent that it be used for the mitzvah, while that intent is not necessary when one is actually writing the scroll.<br>Rav Chayim differentiates between the two deeds as follows: Preparing the parchment is, in essence, a mundane act. Accordingly, the dimension of holiness that makes the parchment fit for use as a Torah scroll must be added by our intentions. In contrast, writing the scroll is, in essence, a holy act. Accordingly, there is no need for anything to be added by our intention.</i> should he write one of God's names without the desired intent,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., when writing God's name, one must be aware of its holiness and write it with that intent in mind. Note the passage from <i>Hilchot Yesodei Torah</i> 6:8 quoted in the commentary on Halachah 13.<br>The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 32:19; <i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 276:2) states that one must write God's name with the intent of expressing its holiness.</i> they are not acceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i>, <i>loc. cit.</i>) states that one must make a verbal statement of this intention. If not, the Torah scroll is unacceptable. The <i>Siftei Cohen</i> (276:1) differs and states that while making a verbal statement is preferable, as long as one intended to write the name with the desired intent, the Torah scroll may be used. The Ramah (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 32:19) also states with regard to writing God's name in <i>tefillin</i>, that, if the scribe made a verbal statement that he is writing the Torah scroll for the desired pupose, after the fact, it is sufficient merely to have had the desired intent in mind when writing God's name without expressing it verbally.</i><br>Therefore, when a person is writing God's name, he should not reply even if the king of Israel<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> notes that the words "of Israel" are significant. A Jewish king is expected to comprehend the sanctity of a Torah scroll and, hence, will understand if he is not answered. Should a gentile king greet a scribe and his failure to answer create a threat to his life, he is allowed to reply.</i> greets him.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Siftei Cohen</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 276:4) rules that if the scribe does reply, the Torah scroll is not disqualified.</i> If he is writing two or three names,<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">in succession - e.g., "God is our Lord. God is one," in the Shema, where three names of God are written in succession.</i> he may interrupt between them and reply.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">When he begins to write again, the scribe should state that he is writing the name to express God's holiness (<i>Shulchan Aruch, Yoreh De'ah</i> 276:3).</i>
[When a scribe] dips his pen [in ink] to write God's name, he should not begin [writing] one of the letters of God's name.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">lest too much ink collect on the pen and create an ink blot that must be rubbed out. In doing so, one might also rub out God's name. This is prohibited, as stated in <i>Hilchot Yesodei HaTorah</i> 6:1-2.</i> Rather, he should begin with the letter preceding [God's name].<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Thus, if an error occurs, God's name will not be effected.</i><br>If [a scribe] forgot to write God's name in its entirety, he may insert it in between the lines.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Pit'chei Teshuvah</i> 273:6 suggests writing another word above the line together with God's name.</i> It is, however, unacceptable to have a portion of God's name on the line and a portion inserted [between the lines].<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">From the Rambam's phraseology, it appears that even after the fact, the Torah scroll is not acceptable. Although this decision is accepted by the <i>Turei Zahav</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 276:4), the <i>Bayit Chadash</i> mentions a more lenient view.</i> With regard to other words, if one forgets, one may write half the word on the line and half above the line.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">One should not, however, write the extra word in the margin between the columns of a Torah scroll (<i>Shulchan Aruch, Yoreh De'ah</i> 276:1).</i><br>When does the above apply? With regard to a Torah scroll.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Because there is no obligation that a Torah scroll be written in order. Indeed, one may intentionally write certain words or passages not in order.</i> In contrast, with regard to <i>tefillin</i> and mezuzot, one should not insert even one letter<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">of God's name or of any other word</i> [between the lines].<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">The Jerusalem Talmud, <i>Megillah</i> 1:9, explains that a word cannot be inserted in the passages of <i>tefillin</i> and mezuzot. The commentaries explain that this is because these passages must be written ןרדסכ, "in order." If not, they are invalid. In this instance, the word which is inserted will not have been written in order.</i> Rather, if one forgets even one letter, one should entomb what one has written and write another one.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">There is another alternative. One may rub out the words written after the omitted word (needless to say, provided that they do not include God's name), and then rewrite the words that were rubbed out.</i><br>It is permitted to write<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">any portion of a Torah scroll, even</i> God's name on [parchment where letters] have been scraped off<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">when the ink has dried</i> or rubbed out<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">while still moist. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 32:24) states that it is preferable to wait until the ink dries and then scrape the letters off, rather than rub them out while the ink is still moist. In the latter instance, a mark of the ink is still left. Though it does not disqualify the religious article, it is not attractive.</i> on all [of these sacred articles].<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">See also Chapter 7, Halachah 13.</i>
Scribes who write Torah scrolls, <i>tefillin</i>, and mezuzot<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">and desire to protect their work from being exposed to dust or other factors</i> may not turn the parchment face down.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">This does not show proper respect for the sacred articles (<i>Eruvin</i> 98a).</i> Rather, they should spread a cloth over them or fold them.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">By no means, however, should they leave them open and uncovered, for this is also a sign of disrespect. The <i>Bayit Chadash</i> and the <i>Siftei Cohen</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 277:1) extend this principle to all sacred texts, explaining that they should never be left open.</i>
[The following rule applies when] a scribe who wrote a Torah scroll, <i>tefillin</i>, or <i>mezuzah</i> states: "I did not write the names of God with the proper intent."<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">As mentioned in Halachah 15, if a scribe did not have the proper intent when writing one of God's names, the scroll is not acceptable.</i> Once they have left his hand,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">becoming the property of another individual</i> his statements are not believed with regard to the disqualification of the scroll.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Siftei Cohen</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 281:9) states that this law applies only when the scribe was paid for his services. If he wrote the scroll as a favor, his word is not accepted and the scroll is not disqualified.</i> They are, however, accepted to the extent that he must forfeit his entire wage.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Gittin</i> 54b states that he may not collect his entire wage minus the payment due him for the names of God, because a Torah scroll is worthless unless God's names were written with the proper intent.<br>The Ramah (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 281:4) states that the scribe does not forfeit his entire wage. Although he is not paid the fee due him for a Torah scroll, he still should be paid for producing a text of the<i>Chumash</i>.<br>At that time, printing presses were uncommon and books were written as scrolls. Though such a scroll could not be used for a communal Torah reading, it would be useful for individual study. [Note the objections of the <i>Turei Zahav</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 281:4) to this ruling.]</i><br>Why isn't he believed with regard to the disqualification of the scroll? Because it is possible that he wanted to cause a loss to the purchaser or to the person who hired him, thinking that<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">he would not suffer a major loss</i> with this statement all that he would be required to forfeit would be the payment for the names of God.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Hence, he was willing to suffer a loss of this nature in order to irritate the purchaser.</i><br>Accordingly, were he to say that the parchment of this Torah scroll or <i>tefillin</i><sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam omits a <i>mezuzah</i>, since, as he states in Halachah 11, a <i>mezuzah</i> need not be processed with a sacred intent in mind.</i> was not processed with the proper intent in mind,<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">as required by Halachah 11</i> his statements are accepted with regard to the disqualification of the sacred articles because, [by virtue of these statements,] he forfeits his entire wage.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">We assume that he would not be willing to suffer such a major loss only to cause difficulty to a colleague.</i> Everyone knows that if the parchments were not processed with the proper intent, he does not deserve any payment.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">In this instance, as well, he deserves the remuneration appropriate for writing a scroll useful for individual study.</i>
Tefillin and mezuzot may be written only in Assyrian script.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the calligraphy used for the Hebrew alphabet that closely resembles the printed Hebrew we use today. One of the opinions mentioned in <i>Sanhedrin</i> 22a explains that this calligraphy was indigenous to the Jews, and the Torah itself was originally written in it. It was, nevertheless, not used by the Jews for an extended period until after the Babylonian exile, when it became the standard calligraphy for sacred writings.<br>In his Commentary on the Mishnah (<i>Yadayim</i> 4:5), the Rambam explains the latter opinion, stating that <i>Ashurit</i> means "the finest and most choice." This name was given to this calligraphy because:<br>It is the most choice calligraphy. Its characters are not interchangeable... nor do they resemble each other to the extent that doubt might arise.... This does not apply with regard to other scripts.<br>It was adopted by the Jewish people in the Babylonian exile after the destruction of the First Temple. In previous (and subsequent) generations, Hebrew was generally written with other characters.</i> Permission was granted to write Torah scrolls<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">This leniency was not extended with regard to <i>tefillin</i> and mezuzot. <i>Megillah</i> 9a derives this concept from the exegesis of Deuteronomy 6:6: "And these words shall be <i>totafot</i>...." Our Sages explain that the expression "shall be" implies that they shall remain unchanged from their Hebrew original.</i> in Greek as well.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">From the Rambam's Commentary on the Mishnah, <i>Megillah</i> 1:8, 2:1, it appears that the intent is not to write a Greek transliteration of the Hebrew, but rather a translation. The Rambam explains that the Septuagint - the translation of the Torah into Greek at the order of King Ptolemy (<i>Megillah</i> 9a;<i>Soferim</i> 1:7) - had become popular. Hence, it was permitted to be used for a Torah scroll.</i> That Greek language has, however, been forgotten from the world. It has been confused and has sunk into oblivion.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., contemporary Greek is very different from the classical tongue, to the extent that they can be considered to be two separate languages.</i> Therefore, at present, all three sacred articles may be written using Assyrian script alone.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Significantly, however, in <i>Hilchot Megillah</i> 2:3, the Rambam does not state that Greek is unacceptable at present. Note also <i>Beit Yosef</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 690).</i><br>One must be precise while writing them, making sure that one letter does not become attached to another one, because any letter which is not surrounded by parchment<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">In our commentary on the following halachah, we discuss the laws governing a letter which is not surrounded by parchment because of a hole that is located on its border.</i> on all four sides<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">There is no minimum amount of space required to be left between letters. Furthermore, one must be careful not to leave too much space, to the extent that it appears that the word is divided in two (<i>Shulchan Aruch, Yoreh De'ah</i> 274:4).</i> is unacceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">If the letters are attached, however, the difficulty may be corrected and, afterwards, the sacred article may be used (<i>Shulchan Aruch, Orach Chayim</i> 32:18).</i><br>Any letter that cannot be read by a child who is neither wise nor foolish<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to a child who is able to recognize letters, but not words. Were he able to recognize words, one could assume that he might identify a letter, not by its shape, but because of the meaning of the word in which it is located (Rashi, <i>Menachot</i> 29b).</i> is not acceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Tefillin and mezuzot must be written in order (Chapter 3, Halachah 5; Chapter 5, Halachah 1). Therefore, if a letter which cannot be recognized by a child is written in <i>tefillin</i> or mezuzot, the parchment may never be corrected. Changing the form of the letter is tantamount to rewriting it in its entirety (<i>Shulchan Aruch, Orach Chayim</i> 32:25).</i> Therefore, one must be careful with regard to the form of the letters,<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">This applies not only when the sacred article was originally written, but throughout the time it is being used. If a portion of a letter cracks, fades, is rubbed out, or is torn, in a manner which alters its form, the sacred article is unacceptable.<br>See also the <i>Tur</i>, <i>Shulchan Aruch</i>, and commentaries (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 36) for a detailed description of the proper form for each letter.</i> so that a <i>yud</i> will not resemble a <i>vav</i>, nor a <i>vav</i> a <i>yud</i>;<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">These two letters are very similar, the difference between them being that the leg of the <i>vav</i> is extended and that of the <i>yud</i> is not. Therefore, if a scribe would write a <i>yud</i> with a long leg or a <i>vav</i> with a short leg, difficulties may arise.</i> a <i>kaf</i> should not resemble a <i>beit</i>, nor a <i>beit</i> a <i>kaf</i>;<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">The difference between these two letters is primarily in the lower right-hand corner. In a <i>kaf</i> this corner is rounded, while in a <i>beit</i> it is square. Hence, a scribe's imprecision could cause a difficulty.</i> a <i>dalet</i> should not resemble a <i>resh</i>, nor a <i>resh</i> a <i>dalet</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">The difference between these two letters is primarily in the upper right-hand corner. In a <i>resh</i> this corner is rounded, while in a <i>dalet</i> it is square. Hence, in this instance as well, a scribe's imprecision could cause a difficulty.</i><br>[The same applies in] other similar instances.<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Shabbat</i> 103b mentions other pairs of letters which resemble each other, among them: a <i>samech</i> and a final <i>mem</i>, a <i>zayin</i> and a final <i>nun</i>, and a <i>heh</i> and a <i>chet</i>.</i> [The text must be written in a manner] that a reader will be able to read without difficulty.<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Shabbat</i>, <i>loc. cit.</i>, breaks the word וכתבתם (Deuteronomy 6:9 , which refers to the command to write a <i>mezuzah</i>, into two words, וכתב תם which mean, "And you shall write perfectly." Thus, the command to write a <i>mezuzah</i> - and, by association, <i>tefillin</i> - also serves as an imperative requiring that the writing be precise.</i>
[The following rules apply to] parchment which has holes: One should not write over a hole.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Rashi, <i>Shabbat</i> 108a, associates this ruling with the interpretation, וכתב תם, quoted from <i>Shabbat</i> 103b above. Writing which is "perfect" should not be broken by holes in the parchment.</i> If, however, ink passes over the hole [without seeping through],<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">As long as no ink seeps through the parchment, the writing can be considered as "perfect."</i> the presence of the hole is of no consequence, and one may write upon it.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">This applies even if there is a slight hole in the parchment which can be seen when the parchment is held up to light (<i>Shulchan Aruch HaRav</i> 32:17; <i>Mishnah Berurah</i> 32:32).<br>[The latter principle has ramifications in contemporary society. Today, many scribes check mezuzot and <i>tefillin</i> using light tables and/or magnifying glasses. The cracks or perforations they find in this manner are not significant and cannot disqualify a parchment.]</i> Accordingly, if the skin of a fowl<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">which has many small holes where its feathers were</i> has been processed, it is permissible to write upon it.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Nevertheless, it is not common for scribes to use parchment of this origin.</i><br>[The following rules apply] when a parchment becomes perforated after<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Halachah 19 deals with the requirement that the letters be surrounded by parchment while they are being written. This halachah mentions the ruling when the perforation is made afterwards.</i> it has been written on:<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Since, at the outset, the writing was "perfect," the parchment is not disqualified because of a hole of later origin. Nevertheless, as will be explained, there are other halachic difficulties involved.</i> If the perforation is within the inside of a letter<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">e.g., in the space inside a heh, inside a mem, or inside any of the other letters - it is acceptable. - According to the Rambam, the letter is acceptable even though the hole touches the letter itself. Although the previous halachah states that a letter must be surrounded by parchment on all sides, that statement refers only to the external perimeter of the letter. There is no obligation that the letter be surrounded by parchment on the inside.<br>The <i>Shulchan Aruch, Orach Chayim</i> 32:15, quotes the Rambam's opinion, but also mentions a passage from the Jerusalem Talmud (<i>Megillah</i> 1:9), which requires that the letter be surrounded by parchment on the inside as well. Most Ashkenazic halachic authorities suggest accepting the stringency of the Jerusalem Talmud's ruling.</i> - e.g., in the space inside a <i>heh</i>, inside a<i>mem</i>, or inside any of the other letters - it is acceptable.<br>Despite the fact that a leg of a letter becomes perforated to the extent that it becomes separated [into two portions], it is acceptable if:<br>a) [the length of<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">the portion of</i> the leg]<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">which remains connected to the body of the letter</i> is equivalent to that of a small letter;<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., a <i>yud</i>.<br>In this context, Rabbenu Asher differentiates between the right and left legs of the <i>heh</i>. This distinction, however, is not accepted by the later authorities [<i>Shulchan Aruch</i>, Ramah, (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 32:15)].</i> and<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">if</i><br>b) the letter's [present form]<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., its upper portion</i> does not resemble another letter.<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">For example, when a perforation causes a <i>vav</i> to be separated into two portions, if its upper portion resembles a <i>yud</i>, it is unacceptable. If the length of the upper portion would prevent a child (see the previous halachah) from confusing it with a <i>yud</i>, it is acceptable.</i><br>If [the length of<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">the portion of</i> the leg]<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">which remains<br><b>is not equivalent to that of a small letter, it is -</b> not large enough to be of consequence, and the letter is<br><b>not acceptable. -</b> See also <i>Beit Yosef</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 32).</i> is not equivalent to that of a small letter, it is not acceptable.

Chapter 2

In what manner are the <i>tefillin</i> [placed on] the head written? [The] four passages<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">mentioned in Chapter 1, Halachah 1: <i>Kadesh Li</i> (Exodus 13:1-10), <i>V'hayah ki y'viacha</i> (Exodus 13:11-16), <i>Shema</i> (Deuteronomy 6:4-8), and <i>V'hayah im shamo'a</i> (Deuteronomy 11:13-21).</i> are written on four parchments<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Menachot</i> 34b explains that the word <i>totafot</i>, with which the Torah refers to the head <i>tefillin</i>, is a combination of the Carthaginian and African terms for two. Since two and two equals four, the word thus means "a four-sectioned ornament."</i> and rolled closed,<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">more precisely, folded closed. Though in this halachah, the Rambam mentions only the <i>tefillin</i> of the arm, the parchments in the <i>tefillin</i> of the head are also folded from the end to the beginning, so that when they are opened, one begins reading at the beginning (Chapter 3, Halachah 7).</i> each as a separate entity. They are placed in four compartments, which are covered by a single piece of leather.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 3, Halachah 2, for a description of how these <i>tefillin</i> are made.<br><i>Menachot</i>, <i>loc. cit.</i>, derives this concept from the fact that Exodus 13:9 refers to the head <i>tefillin</i> as "a remembrance," using a singular form of the word. This teaches that all four passages must appear as a single entity, "one remembrance."<br>In his Commentary on the Mishnah (<i>Keilim</i> 18:8), the Rambam also mentions <i>tefillin</i> of the head in which four separate compartments are sewn together. <i>Shulchan Aruch HaRav</i> 32:58 and the <i>Mishnah Berurah</i> 32:172 write that <i>tefillin</i> made in this manner or by gluing the four compartments together are acceptable. They nevertheless suggest that it is preferable to purchase <i>tefillin</i> that are made from a single piece of leather.<br>[At present, frequently, <i>tefillin</i> which are made from four compartments that are either sewn or glued together are referred to as <i>peshutot</i> and sold for a substantially lower price than <i>tefillin</i> made from a single piece of leather. The difficulty in purchasing them extends beyond one's willingness to opt for the more lenient opinion mentioned in the above sources. Since most halachic authorities advise accepting the more stringent view, most careful scribes do not use these <i>peshutot</i>. Thus, it is likely that they will have other problems as well.]</i><br>The four passages of [the <i>tefillin</i> placed on] the arm are written on four columns on a single parchment.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">They may, however, be written on four separate parchments, which are later glued together (<i>Menachot</i>, <i>loc. cit.</i>).</i> They should be rolled closed like a Torah scroll from the end to the beginning and placed in a single compartment.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Menachot</i>, <i>loc. cit.</i>, derives this concept from the exegesis of Exodus, <i>loc. cit.</i>: "And they shall be a sign for you on your hand." The verse implies that just as the <i>tefillin</i>'s external appearance is as a single sign, so too, internally, they should be a single entity.</i>
Care must be taken in writing these passages. If one wrote a passage which should be <i>s'tumah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">As the Rambam explains at length in Chapter 8, there are two general categories for the passages of the Torah, <i>s'tumah</i> and <i>p'tuchah</i>.<br><i>S'tumah</i> means "closed." It refers to a passage whose first word is always written in the middle of a line in the Torah. (See Chapter 8, Halachah 2.)</i> as <i>p'tuchah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><i>P'tuchah</i> means "open." It refers to a passage whose first word is always written at the beginning of a line in the Torah. See Chapter 8, Halachah 1.</i> or a passage which should be <i>p'tuchah</i> as <i>s'tumah</i>, it is invalid.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">The Ramah (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 32:36) quotes an opinion which explains that the <i>tefillin</i> are not disqualified for such an error. Since there is a difference between <i>tefillin</i> and a Torah scroll - in a Torah scroll, there are many other passages between <i>Shema</i> and <i>V'hayah im shamo'a</i> - the <i>tefillin</i> are not disqualified if <i>V'hayah im shamo'a</i> is written as <i>p'tuchah</i>. Contemporary Ashkenazic practice is based on this opinion. [Note our commentary on Chapter 5, Halachah 2.]</i><br>The first three passages are all <i>p'tuchot</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">For this reason, the first two passages should be written in a manner in which they end at least nine letters before the end of the column (<i>Shulchan Aruch, Orach Chayim</i> 32:36).</i> while the final passage, <i>V'hayah im shamo'a</i>, is <i>s'tumah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">As is explained in the commentary on Chapter 8, Rabbenu Asher differs with the Rambam and offers a different interpretation of the terms <i>s'tumah</i> and <i>p'tuchah</i>. Their difference of opinion does not create a difficulty with regard to the first three passages. A problem, however, does arise with regard to the passage, <i>V'hayah im shamo'a</i>. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>loc. cit.</i>) states that the Rambam's opinion should be followed. The <i>Turei Zahav</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 32:26) offers a compromise, which satisfies, according to his interpretation, both the Rambam's and Rabbenu Asher's views. His interpretation, however, is not accepted by all authorities.</i>
One must be careful regarding [the spelling of the words in these passages] with regard to the short or full form.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">There are times when the Hebrew vowels <i>cholam</i> and <i>shuruk</i> are written with a letter <i>vav</i>, and times when that letter is omitted. Similarly, there are times when the vowel <i>chirik</i> is written with a <i>yud</i>, and times when that letter is omitted.<br>The expression, <i>malei</i>, rendered as "full form," refers to the form which includes the extra letter. <i>Chaseir</i>, rendered as "short form," refers to the form which lacks the extra letter.</i> [The manner in which] these four passages are written [in <i>tefillin</i>] should resemble the manner they are written in a Torah scroll that has been checked [for accuracy in this regard].<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">In this context, note the comments of the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 32:20) and <i>Shulchan Aruch HaRav</i> (32:33), which elaborate on the responsibility of a scribe.</i><br>When one writes a word which requires a short form using a full form, it is invalid until one erases the extra letter.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">As mentioned above, every letter of the four passages in the <i>tefillin</i> must be written in order. Nevertheless, although an entire passage was written, correcting a word by erasing an extra letter is not a contradiction to this principle.<br>The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 32:23) mentions that a problem may arise if the letter to be erased is in the middle of a word, since, when it is erased, the space left will cause the word to appear divided. The commentaries explain that the difficulty can sometimes be corrected by extending a letter, for example, writing a <i>resh</i> with an extended upper line.</i> If one writes a word which requires a full form using a short form, it is invalid and may not be corrected.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Because the additional letter will not have been written in the proper order.</i><br>These are [the correct spellings of the words that could present difficulties] with regard to the short and full forms in these four passages.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam's inclusion of these particulars in the next four halachot is a clear expression of the desire he expressed in his introduction to the <i>Mishneh Torah</i>, to compose a text that will serve as "a compilation of the entire Oral Law," that would allow a person to "comprehend the entire Oral Law from it without having to study any other text." He saw the <i>Mishneh Torah</i> as a guide to the performance of the mitzvot, and therefore included in it details that would allow every individual to understand - and thus carry out - the minute particulars involved in the fulfillment of each mitzvah.<br>1. The Rambam does not use the expression, "short form," because the <i>chirik</i> of the <i>alef</i> possesses a <i>yud</i>. Similarly, the Rambam states "without a <i>vav</i>" or "without a <i>yud</i>" in several other instances in these halachot, because the form of the word is neither short or full in its entirety. 2. Generally, the word is written ידך. <i>Menachot</i> 37a explains that this departure from the norm was intended to teach us that <i>tefillin</i> are worn on the left hand, as explained in the commentary on Chapter 4, Halachah 2. 3. There are three sizes of letters in the Torah - the normal size, an enlarged size, and a reduced size - as mentioned in Chapter 7, Halachah 8. The <i>Baal HaTurim</i> notes that <i>ayin</i> and <i>dalet</i> spell the word <i>eid</i>, which means "witness." The <i>Shema</i> describes God's oneness. By reciting this verse and placing it in their <i>tefillin</i> and <i>mezuzot</i>, the Jews serve as witnesses, testifying to that oneness. 4. The Rambam also discusses the subject of crowns in Chapter 7, Halachot 8-9. When discussing this matter, the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 36:3) mentions the obligation to place crowns on the letters, שעטנ"ז ג"ץ. The obligation of placing crowns on these letters is explicitly mentioned in the Talmud, <i>Menachot</i> 29b. Hence, they are regarded with greater stringency.<br>Afterwards, the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> also states that there are scribes who customarily place crowns on other letters. Significantly, even in Yemen, where the Rambam's directives are usually adhered to precisely, an exception is made in this instance, and there are different customs with regard to the crowns placed upon letters.<br>5. <i>Shulchan Aruch HaRav</i> (36:5) and the <i>Mishnah Berurah</i> (36:15) require that one add any crowns that are lacking in the passages. This refers, however, to the crowns on the letters, שעטנ"ז ג"ץ, and not to the crowns required by the Rambam.</i>
[In] the first passage, <i>Kadesh li kol b'chor</i>, [the word] <i>b'chor</i> [is written using] the full form; the word <i>zachor</i> [using] the full form; the word <i>b'chozek</i> [using] the short form; the word <i>hotzi</i> [using] the full form; the word <i>yotzi'em</i> without a <i>vav</i>; the word <i>y'viacha</i> [using] the full form; the word <i>v'ha'emori</i> [using] the short form; the word <i>v'hay'vusi</i> [using] the full form; the word <i>la'avotecha</i> without a <i>vav</i>; the word <i>ha'avodah</i> [using] the short form; the word <i>matzot</i> [using] the short form; the word <i>hash'vi'i</i> [using] the full form; the word <i>matzot</i> [using] the full form; the word <i>s'or</i> [using] the short form; the word <i>g'vulecha</i> [using] the short form; the word <i>ba'avur</i> [using] the full form; the word <i>l'ot</i> [using] the full form; the word <i>ul'zikaron</i> [using] the full form; the word <i>einecha</i> [using] the full form; the word <i>torat</i> [using] the full form; the word <i>hotziacha</i> without a <i>yud</i>; the word <i>hachukkah</i> [using] the short form; and the word <i>l'moadah</i> [using] the full form.
[In] the second passage, <i>V'hayah ki y'viacha</i>, [the word] <i>y'viacha</i> [is written] without a <i>yud</i>; the word <i>chamor</i> [using] the short form; the word <i>b'chor</i> [using] the full form; the word <i>b'chozek</i> [using] the short form; the word <i>hotzianu</i> [using] the full form; the word <i>vayaharog</i> [using] the short form; the word <i>b'chor</i> [using] the full form; the word <i>mib'chor</i> [using] the short form; the words <i>v'ad b'chor</i> [using] the full form; the word <i>zove'ach</i> without a <i>vav</i>; the word <i>b'chor</i> in <i>v'chol b'chor</i> [using] the full form; the word <i>l'ot</i> [using] the full form; the word <i>yadecha</i> is written with a <i>hey</i>; the word <i>ul'totafot</i> without the final <i>vav</i>; the word <i>einecha</i> [using] the full form; the word <i>b'chozek</i> [using] the short form; and the word <i>hotzianu</i> [using] the full form.
[In] the third passage, <i>Shema</i>, the <i>ayin</i> of [the word] <i>Shema</i> and the <i>dalet</i> of [the word] <i>echad</i> are enlarged.<br>The word <i>m'odecha</i> [is written using] the short form; the word <i>l'vanecha</i> [using] the full form; the word<i>b'vetecha</i> without a second <i>yud</i>; the word <i>uv'kumecha</i> [using] the full form; the word <i>l'ot</i> [using] the full form; the word <i>yadecha</i> [using] the short form; the word <i>l'totafot</i> without both <i>vavim</i>; the word <i>einecha</i> [using] the full form; the word <i>mezuzot</i> without the first <i>vav</i>; the word <i>beitecha</i> without a second <i>yud</i>; the word <i>uvish'arecha</i> [using] the full form.
[In] the fourth passage, <i>V'hayah im shamo'a</i>, [the word] <i>shamo'a</i> [is written using] the short form; the word <i>mitzvotai</i> with only one <i>vav</i>; the word <i>yoreh</i> [using] the full form; the word <i>umalkosh</i> [using] the full form; the word <i>v'tiroshcha</i> without a <i>vav</i>; the word <i>v'hishtachavitem</i> [using] the full form; the word <i>y'vulah</i> [using] the full form; the word <i>hatovah</i> [using] the short form; the word <i>notein</i> [using] the short form; the word <i>otam</i> [using] the short form; the word <i>l'ot</i> [using] the full form; the word <i>l'totafot</i> lacking the second <i>vav</i>; the word <i>einechem</i> [using] the full form; the word <i>otam</i> [using] the short form; the word<i>b'vetecha</i> without a second <i>yud</i>; the word <i>uv'kumecha</i> [using] the full form; the word <i>mezuzot</i> [using] the full form; the word <i>beitecha</i> without a second <i>yud</i>; the word <i>uvish'arecha</i> [using] the full form; the word <i>la'avoteichem</i> without a <i>vav</i>.
Care must be taken regarding the placement of crowns on the letters. They are formed like [small] <i>zeiynin</i> on the [tops of the] letters which possess crowns as in a Torah scroll. These are the letters which possess crowns in these four passages.
There is only one letter [with a crown] in the first passage: the final <i>mem</i> of <i>miyamim</i>. There are three <i>zeiynin</i> upon it. In the second passage, there are five letters [with crowns]. Each of these is a <i>heh</i>, and four <i>zeiynin</i> are placed on each of them. They are: the <i>heh</i> of <i>un'tanah</i>, the first and final <i>heh</i> in the word <i>hikshah</i>, the <i>heh</i> of <i>vayaharog</i>, and the <i>heh</i> of <i>yadecha</i>.<br>In the third passage, there are five letters [with crowns.] They are: the <i>kof</i> of <i>uv'kumecha</i>; it has three <i>zeiynin</i>; the <i>kof</i> of <i>uk'shartam</i>; it has three <i>zeiynin</i>; the two <i>tetim</i> and the <i>pei</i> of <i>l'totafot</i>; each of these letters has four <i>zeiynin</i>.<br>In the fourth passage, there are five letters [with crowns.] They are: the <i>peh</i> of <i>v'asafta</i>; it has three <i>zeiynin</i>; the <i>tov</i> of <i>v'asafta</i> has one <i>zayin</i>; the two <i>tetim</i> and the <i>peh</i> of <i>l'totafot</i>; each of these letters has four <i>zeiynin</i>.<br>There are a total of sixteen letters which require crowns. If one did not place crowns above them, added other crowns, or reduced the number of <i>zeiynin</i>, the passages are not invalid.
