A person who purchases <i>tefillin</i> from a person who is not an expert<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Note the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 39:8), which states: "One should not purchase [tefillin] from a person who is not an expert."</i> is required to inspect them.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">checking to see that no extra letters were added, no letters were omitted, and that each letter is formed correctly.<br>[The <i>Shulchan Aruch's</i> directive and the importance of checking <i>tefillin</i> today must be emphasized, because many scribes, particularly those who write inexpensive <i>tefillin</i>, are not experts. Very frequently, people have purchased <i>tefillin</i> only to find that the passages are not acceptable. For this reason, the Rabbis have suggested buying only <i>tefillin</i> that have been written and inspected by an expert.]</i> If he purchased 100 <i>tefillin</i>, he should inspect three, either two head <i>tefillin</i> and one arm <i>tefillin</i>, or two arm <i>tefillin</i> and one head <i>tefillin</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Eruvin</i> 97a states that one must check at least one arm <i>tefillin</i> and one head <i>tefillin</i> to see that the scribe is proficient in writing both.</i> If he finds them acceptable, [from this time onward,] he can assume the scribe [to be proficient].<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">One of the fundamental principles of Torah law is that a <i>chazakah</i> ("assumption upon which one can rely") about a matter can be established when the matter is repeated on three consecutive occasions.</i> Thus, they are all considered to be acceptable and need not be checked.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">provided the person who sells them states that they were all written by the same individual (<i>Mishnah Berurah</i> 39:22).</i><br>If, however, one purchases them in different packages, they must all be checked, because it can be presumed that each package was purchased from a different scribe.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">It is, however, sufficient to check three <i>tefillin</i> from each package.</i>
After a person writes <i>tefillin</i> himself,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">and inspects them</i> purchases them from an expert,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">whose work need not be inspected</i> or purchases them from another person and has them inspected, and places them in their leather [compartments], they need not ever be checked again.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">The commentaries explain that since they are enclosed in compartments that are not exposed to air, we can presume that the letters are intact.<br>Though this decision is quoted in the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 39:10), a qualification is already made. A pair of <i>tefillin</i> which is not worn on a regular basis should be checked twice in seven years. The later authorities (<i>Shulchan Aruch HaRav</i> 39:11 and the <i>Mishnah Berurah</i> 32:26) suggest checking even <i>tefillin</i> that are worn regularly. At present, perhaps because of the differences in the way the compartments are made or in the parchment or ink that is used, it is very common for letters in <i>tefillin</i> to smudge, fade, and crack. The <i>Mateh Efrayim</i> suggests that each person have his <i>tefillin</i> inspected every year. Even authorities who are not that stringent recommend periodic checks.</i><br>As long as their compartments are intact, they are assumed to be acceptable even though several years have passed.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">If, however, the compartments are opened or they are exposed to water, we are obligated to check them (<i>Shulchan Aruch HaRav</i>, <i>loc. cit.</i>, <i>Mishnah Berurah</i>, <i>loc. cit.</i>).</i> We do not suspect that a letter has faded out or been perforated. Hillel the elder stated: "These [tefillin]<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">the <i>tefillin</i> he himself wore</i> are from my maternal grandfather."<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">”and they have not been checked since.”</i>

Chapter 3

There are eight requirements in the making of <i>tefillin</i>. All of them are halachot transmitted to Moses on Mount Sinai<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">In Chapter 1, Halachah 3, the Rambam mentioned that there were ten requirements for <i>tefillin</i> that were communicated as "halachot transmitted to Moses on Mount Sinai." The first two involve the actual composition of the <i>tefillin</i>. They, and the other laws involving that subject, were discussed in the first two chapters. Now the Rambam discusses the requirements involved in making the <i>tefillin</i>'s compartments and straps.</i> and, therefore, it is necessary to fulfill them all. If one deviates with regard to any of them, the [<i>tefillin</i>] are unacceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Though a "halachah transmitted to Moses on Mount Sinai" is not written in the Torah itself, it is as binding upon us as those laws which are stated explicitly.</i> They are:<br>a) The <i>tefillin</i> must be square<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">There is a homiletic dimension to this requirement. Nothing is naturally square. Thus, this shape alludes to man's power of achievement. Wearing <i>tefillin</i> on our heads and opposite our hearts implies that we should exercise our powers of achievement when our minds and hearts are tied to God's mitzvot.<br>The base of the <i>tefillin</i> must also be square. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 32:39) states that this is also a halachah transmitted to Moses on Mount Sinai.<br>Note <i>Shulchan Aruch HaRav</i> 32:59 and the <i>Mishnah Berurah</i> 32:181, which state that since the squareness of <i>tefillin</i> is a halachah transmitted to Moses on Mount Sinai, the <i>tefillin</i> must remain square at all times. If over the course of time, their shape changes, they may no longer be used.</i> and must be sewn closed in a square.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">See Halachah 10.</i> [Both] diagonals<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">of the square on the top and the base of the <i>tefillin</i></i> must be equal,<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">The commentaries cite <i>Sukkah</i> 8a, which states that the diagonal of a square is approximately 1.4 times the length of one of its sides.</i> and thus all four angles will be equal.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Each one being 90 degrees. See Halachot 2 and 4.</i><br>b) The leather of the head [<i>tefillin</i>] should have a <i>shin</i> embossed on both its right and left sides.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">See Halachah 2.</i><br>c) The passages should be wrapped in fabric.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">See Halachah 8.</i><br>d) A hair should be wound around that fabric. Afterwards, they should be placed in their compartment.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">See Halachah 8.</i><br>e) They should be sewn [closed] with the sinews [of an animal].<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">See Halachot 9-10.</i><br>f) The leather compartment in which they are placed should have a place for the straps to pass through so that they can be moved through the [<i>tefillin</i>'s] handle.<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">See Halachot 3-4.</i><br>g) The straps should be black.<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">See Halachot 14.</i><br>h) The knot with which they are tied should be the renowned knot that is formed like a <i>dalet</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">See Halachah 13. Note <i>Shabbat</i> 62a, which states that the knot of the arm <i>tefillin</i>, which is shaped like a <i>yud</i>, is also a halachah transmitted to Moses on Mount Sinai.</i>
How are the head <i>tefillin</i> made? We take a cubic wooden block.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">This appears to be the Rambam's preference. It is not, however, an absolute requirement.</i> [It need not, however, be a perfect cube]. If its height is [slightly] more or less than its width, it is of no consequence. We are required to take care only that its width and length are alike.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Thus, the top of the <i>tefillin</i> must be a perfect square, but its side surfaces need not be square. This ruling is quoted by the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> and the Ramah (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 32:39).<br>The <i>Bi'ur Halachah</i> emphasizes that it is not the top surface of the <i>tefillin</i> alone which must be square. At every point along its height, its circumference must be a perfect square. Thus, even if the top surface itself remains square, should the <i>tefillin</i> be crooked or noticeably indented at the sides, they are unacceptable.</i><br>Three grooves are carved into it so that four projections will be made as depicted.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">The accompanying diagram is based on a drawing by the Rambam himself, which has been copied in all printings of the <i>Mishneh Torah</i>.</i> Leather is taken<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Note our commentary on Chapter 2, Halachah 1, regarding making <i>tefillin</i> by sewing or gluing together separate compartments. This halachah indicates the Rambam's preference for <i>tefillin</i> to be made from a single piece of leather.</i> and soaked in water, and then, the mold is placed within it.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">The authoritative manuscripts of the <i>Mishneh Torah</i> read, "The leather is placed around the mold." This version more accurately describes the process with which <i>tefillin</i> are actually made.</i> The leather is inserted in between the grooves.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">and around theFJ 46sides, so that when it dries four compartments will be created.</i><br>While [the leather] is still wet, it is plucked and squeezed<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">with tweezers. The <i>Orchot Chayim</i> states that one must make the <i>shin</i> in this manner, and it is unacceptable to use a mold. The <i>Beit Yosef</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 32), <i>Shulchan Aruch HaRav</i> 32:65, and the <i>Mishnah Berurah</i> 32:193 all mention that though it is theoretically preferable to follow the <i>Orchot Chayim's</i> ruling, nevertheless, until recently, most <i>shinim</i> were made using a mold. At present, however, there are some manufacturers of <i>tefillin</i> who have reverted to the practice of making the <i>shin</i> with tweezers.</i> until the shape of a <i>shin</i><sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Orchot Chayim</i> mentions that the letter <i>shin</i> is numerically equivalent to 300. In the diaspora, <i>tefillin</i> are worn 300 days during a solar year (according to the opinion which requires that they be worn on <i>chol hamo'ed</i>).<br>4</i> with three heads<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">This is the usual form of the <i>shin</i>.</i> is formed on the right side of the <i>tefillin</i> as they will be worn, and the shape of a <i>shin</i> with four heads<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Beit Yosef</i> (<i>loc. cit.</i>) explains that the letters on the tablets of the Ten Commandments were hewn into the stone. When a three-headed <i>shin</i> is hewn into stone, the protruding stone appears as a four-headed<i>shin</i>.</i> is formed on the left side of the <i>tefillin</i> as they will be worn.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">The Ramah (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 32:42) rules that <i>tefillin</i> are not disqualified if the sides on which the two <i>shinim</i> are placed are reversed, so long as they possess both forms of the <i>shin</i>.</i>
The leather is then left on the mold until it dries, and then it is removed. Thus, the leather will be [formed into a block] with four empty compartments.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">The leather is then left on the mold until it dries, and then it is removed. Thus, the leather will be [formed into a block] with four empty compartments.</i><br> One of the passages from the Torah is placed<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">upright, as a Torah scroll stands in the ark (<i>Shulchan Aruch, Orach Chayim</i> 32:45), with the right side of the passage on the left side of the <i>tefillin</i> (<i>Shulchan Aruch HaRav</i> 32:70).</i> in each compartment,<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">In Halachah 5, the Rambam describes the order in which the passages are placed into the compartments.</i> and then a portion of the leather is folded over beneath them,<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Thus, forming the bottom of the <i>tefillin</i>'s base. This is referred to as the <i>titorah</i>, which means "bridge" (<i>Shulchan Aruch</i>, <i>Orach Chayim</i> 32:44). It is given this name because it extends like a bridge below the <i>tefillin</i> (<i>Shulchan Aruch HaRav</i> 32:66).</i> and then they are sewn closed on all four corners.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">The precise manner in which the <i>tefillin</i> are sewn closed is described in Halachah 10.</i><br>Within this lower piece of leather, a place should be left for the straps to be inserted,<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">and pass through. This portion should extend outward</i> like a handle.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Alternatively, "loop" (<i>Aruch</i>).<br>The width of this "handle" should be less than the width of the base of the <i>tefillin</i>, to distinguish it from the base and thus accentuate the base's squareness (<i>Shulchan Aruch HaRav</i>, <i>loc. cit.</i>, <i>Mishnah Berurah</i> 32:177).</i> It is called a <i>ma'aboret</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">which literally means "passageway."</i>
How are the <i>tefillin</i> of the arm made? We take a wooden block whose length is equal to its width<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Here, also, a square shape is required at the top of the <i>tefillin</i>, along its entire height, and at its base.</i> and is a fingerbreadth<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">According to the <i>Shiurei Torah</i>, a fingerbreadth is 2 centimeters in modern measure. The <i>Chazon Ish</i> differs, and considers it 2.4 centimeters.</i> - or slightly more or slightly less<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">high - Curiously, though the Rambam mentions the preferred size for the arm <i>tefillin</i> in this halachah, he did not suggest a size for the head <i>tefillin</i> in the previous two halachot. <i>Shulchan Aruch HaRav</i> 32:62 relates that people of stature should wear head <i>tefillin</i> that are at least three fingerbreadths high.<br><i>Shulchan Aruch HaRav</i> 32:63 and the <i>Mishnah Berurah</i> 32:189 mention that the width and length of the base of the <i>tefillin</i> should be more than one fingerbreadth. Preferably, the width and length of the head <i>tefillin</i> should be two fingerbreadths.</i> - high, and place wet leather around it.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Thus, forming a single compartment.</i><br>The leather is left on this mold until it has dried, and then it is removed. The four passages<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">which are written on a single piece of parchment</i> are deposited in the place left by the mold.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">As mentioned in the previous halachah, the parchment should be placed upright in the compartment.</i> A portion of the leather is folded over beneath them, and then they are sewn closed on all four corners.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">See Halachah 10.</i> A piece of leather, like a handle, should be left for the straps [to be inserted].<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>ma'aboret</i> of the arm <i>tefillin</i> is governed by the same principles as explained above with regard to the head <i>tefillin</i>.</i>
What is the order of the passages? For the head <i>tefillah</i>, the final passage, <i>V'hayah im shamo'a</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">The order of the passages chosen by the Rambam - when read by a person facing the person wearing the <i>tefillin</i> - reflects their order in the Torah (<i>Menachot</i> 34b). Hence, <i>V'hayah im shamo'a</i> is referred to as the final passage.</i> is placed in the first compartment on the right side of the person putting on the <i>tefillin</i>. <i>Shema</i> is placed next to it. <i>V'hayah ki y'viacha</i> is placed in the third compartment next to <i>Shema</i>, and <i>Kadesh Li</i> is placed in the fourth compartment, on the left side of the person putting on the <i>tefillin</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Rashi also prescribes this order for the passages of the <i>tefillin</i>, and this is the order that is accepted as halachah (<i>Shulchan Aruch, Orach Chayim</i> 34:1). Many other Torah authorities (among them Rav Hai Gaon and Rabbenu Tam) suggest a different order, placing the two passages that begin <i>V'hayah</i>, <i>V'hayah ki y'viacha</i> and <i>V'hayah im shamo'a</i> in the center, <i>Shema</i> on the right side of the person wearing the <i>tefillin</i>, and <i>Kadesh Li</i> on his left side.</i><br>Thus, a person who is facing the person wearing the <i>tefillin</i> will read them in the following order.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">The diagram accompanying this halachah is based on a diagram drawn by the Rambam himself and included in all texts of the <i>Mishneh Torah</i>.<br>Thus, the Rambam (and similarly, Rabbenu Tam and those who follow his opinion) considered that the order of the passages be calculated from the perspective of a person standing opposite the person wearing the <i>tefillin</i>. In contrast, there are other opinions (<i>Shimusha Rabbah</i> and the Ra'avad) who maintain that the order of the passages should be calculated from the perspective of the person wearing the <i>tefillin</i>. (This produces two further perspectives regarding the order of the <i>tefillin</i>. The <i>Shimusha Rabbah</i> follows the same order as the Rambam except that <i>V'hoyoh im Shamoa</i>, is placed in the first compartment on the left side of the person putting on the <i>tefillin</i>, not on the right side. Similarly, the Ra'avad shares Rabbenu Tam's view, but reverses the sides.)</i> If their order is altered, they are not acceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">For this reason, the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 34:2) suggest that a God-fearing person put on both a pair of <i>tefillin</i> that follow the opinion of the Rambam and Rashi, and a second pair, that follow the opinion of Rabbenu Tam. Though the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> mentions certain reservations in this regard, in many communities it has become widespread practice to wear both pairs of <i>tefillin</i>.<br>Significantly, the <i>She'elot UTeshuvot Min HaShamayim</i> writes that with regard to this dispute an answer was received from heaven, "Just as there is a dispute in the earthly realm, there is a dispute in the spiritual realms."</i>
[The passages for] the arm <i>tefillin</i> are written on four columns<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">each passage being written on a separate column</i> on a single piece of parchment<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Though this is not an absolute requirement, it is preferable (<i>Shulchan Aruch HaRav</i> 32:72; <i>Mishnah Berurah</i> 32:219).</i> like a Torah scroll,<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., the parchment is rolled closed.</i> according to the order in which these passages are found in the Torah,<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">If the passages, in part or in totality, are not written in order, they are unacceptable and may not be corrected.</i> in the following manner:<br>If<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">The diagram accompanying this halachah is based on a diagram drawn by the Rambam himself and included in all texts of the <i>Mishneh Torah</i>.</i> they were written on four separate pieces of parchment and placed in the same compartment, one fulfills one's obligation. There is no need to glue them together.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Although the Ramah (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 32:47) accepts the Rambam's decision, he states that it is customary to glue the parchments together. He emphasizes that it is desirable to use glue that does not contain any non-kosher ingredients.</i>
When the passages<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">both of the head and the arm <i>tefillin</i> - are rolled closed - to be inserted into their compartments.</i> - both of the head and the arm <i>tefillin</i> - are rolled closed, they should be rolled from the end to the beginning,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">as a mezuzah must be rolled from אחדtowards שמע(<i>Menachot</i> 31b).</i> so that were the passage to be rolled open, it would be possible to read each portion from the beginning to the end.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Bi'ur Halachah</i> (32) states that although rolling the passages is preferable, if the parchments were placed in the <i>tefillin</i> without being rolled closed they are acceptable.</i>
Before the passages are placed in their compartments, they should be wrapped in a fabric,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">The present custom is to use a piece of parchment (<i>Shulchan Aruch, Orach Chayim</i> 32:44). Although the Rambam considers this requirement to be a halachah transmitted to Moses on Mount Sinai, the Ashkenazic authorities do not agree. Accordingly, they maintain that, after the fact, if the passages are not wrapped in this parchment, the <i>tefillin</i> may still be used (<i>Shulchan Aruch HaRav</i> 32:68; <i>Mishnah Berurah</i> 32:205).<br>The difference between the Rambam and the Ashkenazic authorities depends on their interpretation of a passage from the Jerusalem Talmud (<i>Megilah</i>, Chapter 1). This difference in interpretation is also reflected in the laws regarding correcting a torn Torah scroll. See Chapter 9, Halachah 15.</i> and hair should be wound around them. Afterwards, they may be placed in their compartments.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">All authorities agree that this is a halachah transmitted to Moses on Mount Sinai.<br>The difference of opinion of whether it is necessary to wrap the <i>tefillin</i> in parchment has created a difficulty with regard to this obligation. The opinions which do not require that the <i>tefillin</i> be wrapped in parchment require that this hair be wrapped around the passages themselves. In contrast, the Rambam, who maintains that it is necessary to wrap them, obligates the hair to be placed around that wrapping.<br>In practice, it is customary to satisfy both opinions and wrap the hair around the passages, cover them with a parchment, and then wrap the same hair around the parchment as well (<i>Shulchan Aruch HaRav</i> 32:69, <i>Mishnah Berurah</i> 32:209).</i><br>This hair should be from a kosher species of animal or beast.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in Chapter 1, Halachah 10, all the elements required for <i>tefillin</i> must be made from species which may be eaten.</i> Even when these animals died without being ritually slaughtered or were <i>treifah</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">wounded or possessing an illness that will cause them to die within a year. The meat of such an animal may not be eaten.</i> [their hair is nevertheless acceptable].<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in the commentary on the above-mentioned halachah, although these animals themselves may not be eaten, since they come from a kosher species, they are not disqualified.</i> It has already become a universally accepted custom to wind hair from the tail<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">The hair from the tail is longer and easier to tie than the hair from the other portions of the body.</i> of a calf<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">To recall the sin of the Golden Calf (<i>Shimusha Rabbah</i>).</i> [around these parchments].<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">Based on the <i>Zohar</i> (<i>Parashat Bo</i>), it is customary that the hair which is wound around the passage, <i>V'hayah im shamo'a</i>, be extended and protrude from the compartments slightly (Ramah, <i>Orach Chayim</i> 32:44).</i>
When the <i>tefillin</i> are sewn closed, they may be sewn only with sinews<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 32:50) states that if sinews are not available, one may sew the <i>tefillin</i> closed with thread made from parchment, until sinews are available. Although there are opinions which object to this ruling, it should be followed if there is no other alternative (<i>Shulchan Aruch HaRav</i> 32:76; <i>Mishnah Berurah</i> 32:227).</i> from a kosher species of animal or beast.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 32:49) states that it is preferable to use the sinews of an ox.<br>Significantly, the Rambam does not mention sinews from kosher fowl. The Rabbis have not defined which of a fowl's sinews are classified as גידים and which are not. Therefore, it is desirable to use the sinews from beasts or animals, and thus avoid this difficulty (<i>Mishnah Berurah</i>, <i>loc. cit.</i>).</i> [Sinews taken from] animals which died without being ritually slaughtered or which were <i>treifah</i> [are nevertheless acceptable].<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">See the previous halachah.</i><br>It is customary to take sinews from the heels of kosher animals and beasts. They are white in color. If they are too firm, they are softened by [pounding them with] stones and the like until they become like flax.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">This halachah serves as another example of the Rambam's desire for the <i>Mishneh Torah</i> to serve as a complete guide for the fulfillment of the mitzvot.</i> Afterwards, they are spun and twisted into threads<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Note <i>Shulchan Aruch HaRav</i> 32:75, which states that the process of spinning the sinews into thread must be carried out with the intent that they be used for this sacred purpose. Accordingly, this process may not be carried out by gentiles.</i> and used to sew together <i>tefillin</i> and the sheets of Torah scrolls.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">A Torah scroll contains many sheets of parchment. It is a halachah transmitted to Moses on Mount Sinai to join these sheets by sewing them with sinews, as explained in Chapter 9, Halachah 13.</i>
When the <i>tefillin</i> are sewn closed,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">If the base of the <i>tefillin</i> is made from a separate piece of leather from the compartments, the stitches must also be connected to the edges of the skin covering the compartments (<i>Shulchan Aruch HaRav</i> 32:77; <i>Mishnah Berurah</i> 32:228).</i> they should be sewn as a square.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">If the stitches are sewn in any other shape, the <i>tefillin</i> are unacceptable.</i> It is a widely accepted practice for there to be three stitches on each side, so that there will be twelve stitches in all.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Shimusha Rabbah</i> interprets this to be an allusion to the twelve tribes of Israel.</i> This applies for both the arm <i>tefillin</i> and the head <i>tefillin</i>. If, however, one made ten or fourteen stitches, there is no difficulty.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Although the simple meaning of the Rambam's statements is that any number of stitches is acceptable, the commentaries have noted that the <i>Shimusha Rabbah</i> also attaches significance to the numbers ten and fourteen. Ten represents the twelve tribes minus Levi (the priestly tribe) and Judah (the tribe of royalty). Fourteen includes also the tribes of Menasheh and Ephraim.</i><br>For each of the stitches, the thread<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">or threads</i> must pass through from both sides.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>tefillin</i> should be sewn closed with two needles: one which is initially passed through facing the upper side of the <i>tefillin</i>, and one which is initially facing their lower side (<i>Mishnah Berurah</i> 32:230).</i>
The groove between [each of the compartments] of the head <i>tefillin</i> should reach the stitches [which sew the <i>tefillin</i> closed].<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Thus, the separation between the compartments will be both external and internal.</i> [Nevertheless,] if the groove is discernible, so that the [division into] four compartments is openly visible, [the <i>tefillin</i>] are acceptable even if the groove does not extend until the stitches.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., the separation between the compartments does not extend through the total height of the <i>tefillin</i>.</i> If, however, the groove is not discernible, [the <i>tefillin</i>] are not acceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Note the <i>Mishnah Berurah</i> 32:187, which states that if the groove between the <i>tefillin</i> is not discernible, the <i>tefillin</i> are not acceptable even if they are divided into four separate compartments. Needless to say, however, if only an external distinction is made, but inside, there is no separation between the compartments, the <i>tefillin</i> are not acceptable (<i>Shulchan Aruch HaRav</i> 32:61).</i><br>It is necessary to pass a thread or cord through each of the grooves on [the outer side of] the leather [compartments] to separate between the compartments.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">This decision is not accepted by all the authorities. <i>Tosafot, Menachot</i> 32b, states that it is necessary to pass a cord between the compartments only when all four passages are written on a single piece of parchment. See <i>Shulchan Aruch HaRav</i> 32:71, <i>Mishnah Berurah</i> 32:217.</i> It is common custom to pass one of the sinews used to sew [the <i>tefillin</i> closed] between each of these three grooves.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">This halachah raises questions with regard to the process in which <i>tefillin</i> are customarily made today. At present, after the compartments of the <i>tefillin</i> are fashioned into a single block, a sinew is passed between the compartments. Afterwards, they are glued together, and then the entire block is shaped into a square. The grooves we see do not represent the real divisions between the blocks, but are merely external impressions.<br>This process is employed, because were the compartments not to be glued together, it would be difficult to form the <i>tefillin</i> into a square. Even if that were possible at the outset, problems might arise over the course of time, because the compartments might spread further apart, and thus prevent the square shape from being maintained. Though it is acceptable to use <i>tefillin</i> made in the present manner, the Rabbis suggest using those in which the separation between the compartments remains openly visible afterwards. (See the Responsa of the <i>Chatam Sofer</i>, <i>Orach Chayim</i> 5; and the <i>Bi'ur Halachah</i> 32.)</i>
How are the straps made? We take leather straps<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">See Halachah 15.</i> [at least] the length of a barley-corn in width.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Mishnah Berurah</i> 27:42 emphasizes that when the <i>tefillin</i> are being tied around the arm, care must be taken that the straps do not wrinkle - particularly around the knot, where it is natural that they do - so that this minimum width is maintained.<br>Based on Chapter 9, Halachah 9, we can conclude that this measure is approximately 1 centimeter according to <i>Shiurei Torah</i> and 1.2 centimeters according to <i>Chazon Ish</i>.</i> If they are wider than that, they are acceptable. The length of the straps of the head <i>tefillin</i> should be sufficient to surround the head, tie the knot,<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">See the following halachah.</i> and extend on either side of the head until they reach the navel or slightly above it.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Tur</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 27) writes that, according to one opinion, the strap on the right side should reach the navel and the strap on the left should reach the chest. Another opinion states that the right strap should reach the genitalia, and the left, the navel.</i><br>The length of the strap of the arm should be sufficient to surround the forearm,<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">at the muscle (Chapter 4, Halachah 2)</i> tie its knot,<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">See the following halachah.</i> and extend<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Significantly, neither in this halachah nor in the following chapter, where the Rambam describes the manner in which <i>tefillin</i> are worn, does he mention the custom of winding the <i>tefillin</i> strap seven times around the arm.</i> until it can be wound three times around the middle finger and tied.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">In one of his responsa, the Rambam explains that although the verse, "And you shall tie them for a sign on your hand," refers to the knot tied on the forearm, our Sages divided the word וקשרתם in half, תם וקשר, meaning "And you shall tie a perfect knot." For the knot of the <i>tefillin</i> to be "perfect," the straps should be tied on the hand as well.</i> If the straps are longer than this, they are acceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">From the Rambam's statements in Halachah 19, it would appear that he maintains that the minimum standards mentioned in this halachah are absolute requirements.<br>The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 27:11) states that since the minimum measure mentioned in this halachah for the head <i>tefillin</i> is not found in the Talmud, if the only <i>tefillin</i> available do not have straps that conform to these measures, these <i>tefillin</i> should be worn. The straps of the head <i>tefillin</i>, however, must be long enough so that they can be draped in front of the person at least slightly (<i>Shulchan Aruch HaRav</i> 27:22; <i>Mishnah Berurah</i> 27:44).</i>
One places the straps through their handle,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">The extension of the <i>tefillin</i>'s base described in Halachah 4.</i> leaving space for the [circumference of] one's head, and ties<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Pri Megadim</i> writes that this knot must be tied with the specific intent that it be used for the mitzvah.</i> a square knot, which resembles a <i>dalet</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam's intent is that though the knot is square, the straps extending from either side each appearFJ 46as the legs of a <i>dalet</i>. Note the <i>Beit Yosef</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 32), which states that it is preferable to tie a knot which is shaped like a <i>dalet</i> itself. (Unlike the Rambam's knot, which is square, this knot is a right angle.) Sephardic and Chassidic custom is to follow the <i>Beit Yosef</i>. Ashkenazim and Yemenites follow the Rambam's view.</i> Every Torah scholar should learn how to tie this knot.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Chulin</i> 9a mentions this as one of the basic points of knowledge which every Torah scholar should possess.</i> It is impossible to describe this knot in writing. Rather, it must be seen.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Eshkol</i> associates this statement with the interpretation (<i>Menachot</i> 35b) of Exodus 33:23: "And you shall see My rear," that God showed Moses the knot of His head <i>tefillin</i>. Since it is impossible to describe that knot, God actually showed it to Moses.</i><br>The straps of the hand <i>tefillin</i> should be tied with a knot that resembles a <i>yud</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Tur</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 32) writes that - together with the <i>shin</i> embossed on the head <i>tefillin</i> and the <i>dalet</i> mentioned above - this <i>yud</i> completes God's name ידש.<br>Significantly, in Halachah 1, the Rambam does not mention the <i>yud</i> as a "halachah transmitted to Moses on Mount Sinai." Our text of <i>Shabbat</i> 62a confers that status on all three of these letters. <i>Tosafot</i> (<i>Shabbat</i>, <i>loc. cit.</i>) suggests emending the text of that passage and conferring that status on the <i>shin</i> alone.The Rambam appears to take an intermediate position.</i> This knot should<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">have a loop to</i> allow the strap to pass through it so that it can be widened or narrowed while one is tying the <i>tefillin</i> on one's arm.
The outer surface of the straps of both the head and the arm <i>tefillin</i> must be black.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 33:4) states that it is preferable that the straps be dyed black by a Jew with the intent that they be used for the mitzvah of <i>tefillin</i>. According to the Ramah, this is an absolute requirement.</i> This is a halachah transmitted to Moses on Mount Sinai.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">The straps must remain black at all times. Frequently, after the <i>tefillin</i> have been worn for an extended period, the dye on the straps begins to fade (in particular, near the knots). Since the color of the straps is "a halachah transmitted to Moses on Mount Sinai," the <i>tefillin</i> are unacceptable if that color has faded. Accordingly, from time to time, it is desirable to check the straps and, if necessary, dye them again (<i>Mishnah Berurah</i> 33:19).</i><br>In contrast, with regard to the inner surface, since it faces the inside, it is acceptable if it is green or white.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">or any other color (<i>Shulchan Aruch, Orach Chayim</i> 33:3).</i> One should not make this [side of the straps] red, since it will be embarrassing for him if they become overturned.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">People might think that he is infested with skin ulcers and it is the blood oozing from them which makes the straps red (Rashi, <i>Menachot</i> 35a).</i><br>The back of the straps should be the same color as the compartment;<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">before it is dyed; alternatively, the color of the underside of the compartment (<i>Kin'at Eliyahu</i>).</i> if it is green, they should be green; if it is white, they should be white.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., it should be the natural color of the leather.</i> It is attractive for <i>tefillin</i> to be entirely black, the compartments<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 32:40) uses the expression, "It is a mitzvah that they be black." The <i>Nodah B'Yehudah</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 1) cites the Jerusalem Talmud (<i>Megillah</i> 1:9), which states that it is a halachah transmitted to Moses on Mount Sinai that the compartments be black. The Rambam, however, does not consider dyeing the compartments an obligation of this nature.</i> and the entire strap.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., both sides. This practice was never widely accepted (<i>Beit Yosef, Orach Chayim</i> 33).</i>
The leather used to cover the <i>tefillin</i><sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., the compartments</i> and from which the straps are made should come from a kosher species of animal, beast, or fowl. Even when these animals died without being ritually slaughtered or were <i>treifah</i>, [their hides are nevertheless acceptable].<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Shabbat</i> 108a derives this concept through the exegesis of Exodus 13:9, "So that the Torah of God will be in your mouths."</i> If, however, leather from a non-kosher species<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">since it is not permitted to be eaten, it may not be used for <i>tefillin</i></i> was used or if they were covered with gold, they are not acceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam appears to be stating that the compartments themselves may not be made from leather from a non-kosher species or from gold. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 32:48), adds that this prohibition forbids attaching these substances to kosher <i>tefillin</i>.</i><br>The leather used for the straps must be processed with the intent that it be used for the mitzvah.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">If it was not processed with this intent, it is unacceptable (<i>Shulchan Aruch HaRav</i> 33:3; <i>Mishnah Berurah</i> 33:17).</i> In contrast, the leather used to cover the <i>tefillin</i><sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., the compartments</i> need not be processed at all.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Accordingly, if processed, it is not necessary that it be processed with the intent that it be used for a mitzvah.<br>The Rambam's view is not accepted by most authorities. Rabbenu Asher states that the leather must be processed and that the processing must be carried out with the intent that the leather be used for the mitzvah. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 32:37) accepts Rabbenu Asher's view, but writes that if no other <i>tefillin</i> are available, it is possible to rely on the Rambam's opinion.</i> It is even acceptable if it is made from <i>matzah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Shabbat</i> 79a describes this as leather which was not processed with flour and salt and, therefore, likened to matzah, which is simple, without any flavoring.</i> [Indeed,] this is the practice in many communities.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">In one of his responsa, the Rambam writes that such leather is used because it is easier to shape. Others state that such leather becomes firmer.</i>
<i>tefillin</i> may be made only by a Jew, since making them is equivalent to writing [the passages],<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">for which a gentile is disqualified, as explained in Chapter 1, Halachah 13.</i> because of the <i>shin</i> [embossed] in the leather [compartment] mentioned above.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Halachah 2. Since embossing that <i>shin</i> is equivalent to writing, it must be done by a Jew with the proper intent.</i> Therefore, if they were made by a gentile or sewn closed by him, they are unacceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Beit Yosef</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 39) explains that since making <i>tefillin</i> has one element which is equivalent to writing the passages, every deed connected with making them may be performed only by those permitted to write them.</i><br>Similarly, they may not be made by any others whose writing [of the passages] is not acceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">for example, an apostate, a woman, a minor, or a Canaanite slave (Chapter 1, Halachah 13).</i>
A head <i>tefillah</i> may not be made into an arm <i>tefillah</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">for the reason to be explained immediately</i> but an arm <i>tefillah</i> may be made into a head <i>tefillah</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">by making four compartments, separating the passages, and placing each one in its respective compartment. Similarly, a <i>shin</i> must be embossed on either side of the <i>tefillin</i>.</i> because an article should not be lowered from a higher level of holiness<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Rashi (<i>Menachot</i> 34b) explains that the head <i>tefillin</i> are considered to be on a higher level because they have the first two letters of the name שדי (the embossed <i>shin</i> and the <i>dalet</i> of the knot). In contrast, the arm <i>tefillah</i> has only one letter, the <i>yud</i> of the knot.</i> to a lesser one.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">This principle is applied in many other contexts in Torah law. For example, <i>Hilchot</i> tefillah 11:14 states that a house of study may not be transformed into a synagogue, because a house of study is on a higher level of holiness.</i> Similarly, the strap of a head <i>tefillah</i> should not be used for an arm <i>tefillah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Within its discussion of these laws, the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 42:3) also mentions the prohibition against using articles associated with <i>tefillin</i> - e.g., a carrying bag - for mundane purposes.</i><br>When does the above apply? After one has worn them. However, if head <i>tefillin</i> have never been worn, one may make them into arm <i>tefillin</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">The commentaries explain that this halachah communicates a fundamental principle of Torah law, הזמנה, designating an article to be used for a sacred purpose, alone is not sufficient for this holiness to be imparted to them. They must first be used for the purpose for which they were intended.<br>[Homiletically, this teaches us how important it is to express in deed all the resolves which we have made.]</i> How is this done? One drapes leather around them until they become a single [compartment] and [then, one can] tie them on his hand.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">The fact that inside, they are still divided into four compartments is not significant.</i>
[The following laws apply when] the stitches of the <i>tefillin</i><sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">which sew the upper portion of the <i>tefillin</i>'s base to the lower portion, as described in Halachot 9 and 10</i> become torn: If two stitches which are located next to each other become torn, or three stitches become torn even though they are not located next to each other, [the <i>tefillin</i>] are unacceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Menachot</i> 35a mentions these laws with regard to "<i>tefillin</i> being torn." The Rambam explains that this refers to the stitches, as stated in this halachah. Rabbenu Asher, however, interprets this passage as referring to the leather separating one of the compartments of the head <i>tefillin</i> from the other. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 33:1-2) quotes both opinions as halachah.</i><br>When does the above apply? With regard to old [<i>tefillin</i>]. With regard to new [<i>tefillin</i>], however, if their base remains intact, they are acceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Our translation of טבלה as "base" follows the interpretation of most authorities. Note, however, the <i>Bi'ur Halachah</i> (33), which stresses the emphasis the Rambam placed on the stitches being sewn in a square shape. (See Halachot 1 and 10.) If some of the stitches are torn, he explains, then the shape of these stitches may no longer be square, and that is the difficulty to which the Rambam is referring. Thus, he interprets טבלה is referring to "the square shape of the stitches."</i> [<i>tefillin</i> are considered] to be "new" as long as the leather remains strong and does not tear when one takes hold of a portion of the leather where the stitch was torn and hangs the <i>tefillin</i>. If the leather is not fit to hang the <i>tefillin</i> because it will tear, the [<i>tefillin</i> are considered] "old."<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Note that Rashi (<i>Menachot</i>, <i>loc. cit.</i>) and others interpret the passage as ruling more leniently with regard to "old" <i>tefillin</i> than "new" <i>tefillin</i>. Accordingly, <i>Shulchan Aruch HaRav</i> 33:2 and the <i>Mishnah Berurah</i> 33:15 state that, at the outset, one should follow the stringencies implied by both opinions, and replace the stitches whether the <i>tefillin</i> are new or old. If that is impossible and it is impossible for the person to obtain other <i>tefillin</i>, he may wear those with torn stitches whether they are new or old.</i>
Should a strap be torn, [the pieces] should not be tied or sewn together.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Menachot</i> 35b derives this concept from the word וקשרתם, “And you shall tie them." As mentioned, this word can be divided in half – תם וקשר - which means "and you shall tie perfectly."</i> Rather, it should be removed and entombed,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Since it was used for a mitzvah, it may not be discarded casually (<i>Megillah</i> 26b).</i> and another one [substituted for it].<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 33:5) mentions an opinion which allows one to sew (but not tie) the two portions of the torn strap together. It concludes that it is preferable to follow the Rambam's view, but if there is no alternative, the more lenient opinion may be followed.</i><br>The remnants of [torn] straps are not acceptable<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., if a strap tears, one may not continue using one of the pieces</i> unless their length and width meets<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">or exceeds - the minimum requirements - mentioned in Halachah 12.</i> - or exceeds - the minimum requirements.<br>At all times, a person should be careful that the external surface of the straps faces upward<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Menachot</i> 35b states, "Their attractive side should face outward." <i>Mo'ed Katan</i> 25a relates that Rav Huna fasted for forty days to atone for the fact that his <i>tefillin</i> strap once became overturned.</i> when he ties them on his arm and head.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Shulchan Aruch HaRav</i> 27:19 and the <i>Mishnah Berurah</i> 27:38 place greater emphasis on the portion of the strap which is tied around one's head and arm. If these portions of the strap become overturned, one should seek atonement by fasting or by donating to charity. Nevertheless, care should also be taken that the remainder of the straps do not turn over.</i>

Chapter 4

Where are the head <i>tefillin</i> placed?<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Though all four Biblical passages state that the <i>tefillin</i> should be worn "between your eyes," <i>Menachot</i> 37b establishes a correlation between these verses and Deuteronomy 14:1, "Do not place a bald spot between your eyes," and explains that, just as in the latter verse, "between your eyes" refers to the skull, so, too, the verses which concern <i>tefillin</i> imply a place on the skull.</i> They should be placed at the point of the skull, the end of the hairline towards the face,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Tur</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 27) states that the place for the head <i>tefillin</i> is the point of the skull, "the end of the hairline towards the face until the place where a child's brain [can be felt] to pulsate." In his commentary on the <i>Tur</i>, Rav Yosef Karo notes the difference in phraseology between the <i>Tur</i> and the Rambam, and in his <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 27:9), he quotes the <i>Tur's</i> text.</i> the place where a child's brain [can be felt] to pulsate.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Our translation is based on the <i>Kessef Mishneh</i>. Instead of "pulsate," Rashi (<i>Menachot</i> 37a) interprets רופס as "is soft."</i><br>Care must be taken to position them in the center,<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">of the head,</i> so that they will be<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">parallel to the place</i> "between the eyes."<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">However, to place the <i>tefillin</i> actually between one's eyes is forbidden and is considered as heresy (Halachah 3; <i>Megillah</i> 4:7).</i> The knot<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">mentioned in Chapter 3, Halachah 13</i> should be at the top portion of the neck,<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Menachot</i> 35b states that lifting the knot to the top portion of the neck will cause "Israel to be above and not below."</i> the bottom of the skull.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">the portion opposite the face. Care should be taken that the knot be positioned in the center of the neck and not move from side to side (<i>Shulchan Aruch</i>, <i>Orach Chayim</i> 27:10). This point is directly opposite the space between the eyes.</i>
The arm [<i>tefillin</i>] should be tied to one's left arm<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Menachot</i> 37a brings a number of exegetical references which indicate that <i>tefillin</i> should be placed on the left arm. Among them, "It is written, 'And you shall tie' and 'And you should write.' Just as writing is done with the right hand, so, too, tying should be done with the right hand." Tying <i>tefillin</i> with one's right hand implies that they are placed on the left.</i> at the muscle<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">but not above the muscle (<i>Shulchan Aruch HaRav</i> 27:2; <i>Mishnah Berurah</i> 27:4)</i> - i.e., the bulging flesh of the arm between the shoulder and the elbow.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Menachot</i> 37b explains that though the verse literally states that <i>tefillin</i> should be placed on the hand, the exegesis of several verses indicates that they are placed on the muscle. The interpretation of the verse, "And these words..." quoted by the Rambam is one of the proofs cited there.</i> Thus, if one presses his arm to his ribs, the <i>tefillah</i> will be opposite his heart,<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>tefillin</i>, and, in particular, the <i>yud</i> of the knot, should be tilted to face the heart (<i>Shulchan Aruch, Orach Chayim</i> 27:1,2).</i> thus fulfilling the directive [Deuteronomy 6:6], "And these words... shall be upon your heart."<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">This position implies that one should subjugate the desires and feelings of his heart to God (<i>Shulchan Aruch, Orach Chayim</i> 25:5).</i>
A person who places the arm <i>tefillah</i> on his palm,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">in contrast to our Sages' definition of the phrase, "on your hand"</i> or the head <i>tefillah</i> on his forehead,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">in contrast to our Sages' definition of the phrase, "between your eyes"</i> follows the way of the Sadducees.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">In his commentary on <i>Avot</i> 1:3, the Rambam writes that Tzadok and Baithos were students of Antigonus of Socho. When they heard their teacher declare, "Do not serve the master for the sake of receiving a reward," they were upset, since they thought that he was implying that no reward would be given for the performance of mitzvot. They spoke about the matter between themselves and decided to reject the Torah.<br>They began splinter groups which rejected the core of Jewish practice, and coveted material wealth. They found that they could not convince the majority of the people to reject the Torah entirely, so they adopted a different tactic. They claimed that they were true to Torah, but the only Torah that was Divine was the Written Law. The Oral Law was merely a human invention.<br>This thesis was only a ruse to sway the people from the performance of the mitzvot. Accordingly, the Sages would frequently refer to all those who deny the Torah and its tradition to be Sadducees ("followers of Tzadok") or Baithosees ("followers of Baithos").</i> A person who makes his <i>tefillin</i> rounded like a nut<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Megillah</i> 24b, the source for this law, adds, "they are dangerous," for they can fracture the person's skull. Though absent in the standard published edition, this line is included in many texts of the <i>Mishneh Torah</i>. The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i>, however, explains the line's omission since it does not teach us any halachic concepts.</i> does not fulfill the mitzvah at all.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">because, as stated in Chapter 3, Halachah 1, <i>tefillin</i> must be square.</i><br>A left-handed person puts <i>tefillin</i> on his right hand, since [figuratively,] it is his left hand.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Menachot</i> 37a states that <i>tefillin</i> must be placed on the weaker hand. For a left-handed person, this is the right hand.</i> If he is ambidextrous, he should place them on his left hand<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., his left hand in a literal sense. - Note the Ramah (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 27:6), who explains that everything depends on the hand with which the person writes. If he writes with his left hand, the <i>tefillin</i> should be placed on the right.<br>Rav Shlomo Kluger, the <i>Tzemach Tzedek</i>, and other later authorities have dealt with questions concerning people who are ambidextrous to varying degrees. Frequently, they have advised that such a person wear <i>tefillin</i> on one of his arms during the prayer service, and afterwards place them on the other arm, to make sure that he fulfills the mitzvah.</i> - i.e., his left hand in a literal sense.<br>The places where to tie and place<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">The arm <i>tefillah</i> is "tied," while the head <i>tefillah</i> is "placed." This reflects the commandment in Deuteronomy 6:8, "And you shall tie them... on your hand and they shall be... between your eyes." We must actually tie the arm <i>tefillin</i> upon our bodies. In contrast, the mitzvah of the head <i>tefillin</i> is that "it be" - i.e., be positioned in its appropriate place.</i> the <i>tefillin</i> were received as part of the oral tradition.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">In his Introduction to the Commentary on the Mishnah, the Rambam explains that, on Mount Sinai, Moses was given instructions how to observe all the mitzvot. This constituted the "oral tradition." Though the Sages of the Talmud may have used certain verses from the Torah as allusions to these instructions, the fulfillment of the mitzvot began at Sinai (or shortly afterwards, depending on the mitzvah). The people did not need the allusions from the Torah to tell them how to fulfill the mitzvot, since they had already received this information orally from Moses.<br>To relate these concepts to the present context: Although the Sages mentioned several exegetical references to the places where <i>tefillin</i> are worn, it is not that the Sages discovered the proper position for <i>tefillin</i>. Rather, directly after they were given the commandment to put on <i>tefillin</i>, the Jews placed them on their arms and heads. Years later, the Sages sought allusions for these practices in the Written Law.</i>
The [absence of the] head <i>tefillah</i> does not preclude [wearing <i>tefillin</i>] on the arm,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., if a person does not have a head <i>tefillah</i> or is in a situation where it is inappropriate to wear the head <i>tefillah</i> (see Halachah 23), he should still wear the arm <i>tefillah</i></i> and similarly, the [absence of the] arm <i>tefillah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">or if a person lost the arm on which he should place <i>tefillin</i></i> does not preclude [wearing <i>tefillin</i>] on the head.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> mentions a version of the Rambam's Commentary on the Mishnah (<i>Menachot</i> 4:1) which differs with this ruling. Rav Kapach explains that he is referring to the initial version of the Commentary on the Mishnah, which the Rambam later revised.<br>In that initial version, the Rambam explains (based on <i>Menachot</i> 44a) that the Sages feared that if a person were given license to wear only one <i>tefillah</i>, he might hesitate from purchasing the other one. Therefore, they allowed one to wear a single <i>tefillah</i> only when he already possessed the second one. When the Rambam reviewed that commentary, he emended the text to concur with the decision here.</i> They are two mitzvot, each one to be considered independently.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Since there are two different commandments in the Torah, "And you shall tie... and they shall be...," they are regarded to be two separate mitzvot.</i><br>What blessings are recited? On the head <i>tefillin</i>, one recites:<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">See the following halachah, where the Rambam explains that, generally, it is proper to recite only a single blessing.</i> "[Blessed are You...] who has sanctified us with His commandments and commanded us concerning the mitzvah of <i>tefillin</i>." On the arm <i>tefillin</i>, one recites: "[Blessed are You...] who has sanctified us with His commandments and commanded us to put on <i>tefillin</i>."<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">It is possible to explain the difference in the two blessings as follows: With regard to the arm <i>tefillin</i>, the Torah states, "And you shall tie them," requiring a deed, and hence the expression "put on." In contrast, the head <i>tefillin</i> are required "to be... between your eyes." Since less emphasis is placed on our actions, it is appropriate to praise God "concerning the mitzvah of..." (Rav Yehudah ben Yakar).</i>
When does the above apply? When one puts on only one of them.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">As mentioned in the previous halachah, at times one can put one on without the other. On such occasions, the appropriate blessing should be recited for each <i>tefillah</i>.</i> If, however, one puts them both on,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">one after the other without interruption. (The rulings governing an interruption are discussed in the following halachah.)</i> one recites only a single blessing, "...to put on <i>tefillin</i>."<br>One<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">This phrase can also refer to putting on the head <i>tefillin</i>, since both mitzvot share the same intent. Hence, if no interruption is made between the two, it is unnecessary - and therefore, we are forbidden - to recite a second blessing.<br>This ruling is quoted by the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 25:5) and is followed by all Sephardic authorities and some Ashkenazic authorities. The majority of the Ashkenazic community follows the opinion of Rabbenu Asher (quoted by the Ramah, <i>Orach Chayim</i>, <i>loc. cit.</i>) which maintains that the two blessings should be recited even when no interruption is made between putting on the two <i>tefillin</i>.<br>This opinion agrees that the blessing "...to put on <i>tefillin</i>" also applies to the head <i>tefillin</i>. Therefore, even according to this opinion, it is forbidden to make an interruption between putting on the two <i>tefillin</i>; nevertheless, the Sages instituted a second blessing in recognition of the unique importance and holiness of the head <i>tefillin</i>.<br>The later authorities have added that because of the possibility that the second blessing is being recited in vain, one should recite the phrase <i>Baruch shem kavod malchuto le'olam va'ed</i>.</i> should tie the <i>tefillin</i> on one's arm,<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Rabbenu Asher and the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 25:11) write that one should put on the head <i>tefillin</i> directly after tying the <i>tefillin</i> to the arm, before continuing to wind the straps around the hand. Thus, no interruption at all will be made between the fulfillment of the two mitzvot.<br>There are, however, certain opinions (see the commentary of Rav David Arameah) that maintain that since tying the arm <i>tefillin</i> around the middle finger is an essential element of the mitzvah, this should be done before putting on the head <i>tefillin</i>.<br>Our present practice follows the <i>Ari zal</i> who would wind the <i>tefillin</i> straps around his forearm, put on the head <i>tefillin</i>, and then, tie the straps around his hand (<i>Shulchan Aruch HaRav</i> 25:24, <i>Mishnah Berurah</i> 25:38).</i> and afterwards put on the head <i>tefillin</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">following the order stated in the Biblical commandment (<i>Menachot</i> 36a).</i> When one removes them, the head <i>tefillin</i> should be removed, and then the arm <i>tefillin</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Menachot</i> (<i>loc. cit.</i>) continues, explaining that the phrase, "they shall be <i>totafot</i> (plural) between your eyes," teaches us that "as long as the <i>tefillin</i> are 'between your eyes,' 'they should be' - i.e., one should wear both <i>tefillin</i>. Thus, the head <i>tefillah</i> should be removed before the arm <i>tefillah</i>.</i>
[After] reciting the blessing, "...to put on <i>tefillin</i>," and tying the <i>tefillin</i> on one's arm, one is forbidden to talk<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">for doing so would constitute an interruption between putting on the two <i>tefillin</i>. Other interruptions - e.g., to signal to a colleague or to wink at him - are also forbidden. They do not, however, require a second blessing.</i> - even to respond to a greeting from one's teacher<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam chooses this example because a person is required to show deference to his teacher. Accordingly, although normally one is forbidden to interrupt the recitation of the <i>Shema</i>, one may do so to greet his teacher or return his greetings, according to the rules outlined in <i>Hilchot Kri'at Shema</i> 2:15-16.<br>The comparison to a teacher is also significant from a different perspective. <i>Hilchot Kri'at Shema</i> (<i>loc. cit.</i>) discusses when one may interrupt one's prayers "because of fear" - e.g., when one encounters a gentile king. Whenever an interruption is allowed in deference to such a king, one may also respond to <i>Barchu, Kedushah,</i> or <i>Kaddish</i>, for they are expressions of deference to the King of kings (<i>Tur, Orach Chayim</i> 66).<br>From the fact that the Rambam mentions the prohibition against talking between putting on the two <i>tefillin</i> in the context of deference to one's teacher, one might assume that he would allow an interruption in the above situations when deference to God is involved. Though this opinion is accepted by many early and later authorities (Rabbenu Tam, Rav Shneur Zalman of Liadi), the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 25:10) forbids making such an interruption.</i> - until he puts on the head <i>tefillin</i>. If he talks, it is considered to be a transgression.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">since, by doing so, he obligates himself to recite a second blessing that would otherwise not be required. Reciting an unnecessary blessing violates the prohibition, "Do not take the name of God, your Lord, in vain" (Exodus 20:7 .<br>Based on Deuteronomy 20:8, the Sages explained that the Jewish army should be composed only of righteous men. Any sinners were excused from military service. Significantly, there are opinions which consider this transgression significant enough for a person to be included in this category (<i>Sotah</i> 44b).</i> [In such an instance,] one is required to recite the second blessing, "...concerning the mitzvah of <i>tefillin</i>," and then put on the head <i>tefillin</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">The Ramah (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 25:9) requires that the person also repeat the blessing "...to put on <i>tefillin</i>" in this instance. According to his opinion, the blessing "...concerning the mitzvah of <i>tefillin</i>" was instituted as praise for the special holiness of the head <i>tefillin</i>, and is not recited for the performance of the mitzvah itself.</i>
Whenever a person puts on <i>tefillin</i><sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">This halachah may be difficult for many people to comprehend, because they associate wearing <i>tefillin</i> with the prayer service alone. In truth, as explained in Halachot 10, 25, and 26, the mitzvah of <i>tefillin</i> applies throughout the entire day, and the restriction of wearing them only during the prayer service is a relatively recent development.</i> - even if he removes them and puts them on several times throughout the day<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Every moment one wears <i>tefillin</i>, one fulfills a mitzvah. Therefore, it is proper to recite a blessing each time one puts them on.</i> - he should recite a blessing for them.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">The Ramah (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 25:12) states that if a person removes his <i>tefillin</i> with the intent of putting them on again in the near future, it is not necessary for him to recite a blessing.</i><br>With regard to all mitzvot: one recites a blessing for them before performing them.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Berachot</i> 11:2-8 for a discussion of this principle.</i> Therefore, one should recite the blessing for the arm <i>tefillah</i> after placing it on one's muscle,<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">and not beforehand, so that the blessing is recited directly before the mitzvah is fulfilled (<i>Kessef Mishneh</i>).</i> before tying it,<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., tightening the knot around the muscle</i> since tying it comprises the fulfillment of the mitzvah.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">See the commentary on Halachah 3.<br>One should recite the blessing for the head <i>tefillah</i> after placing it on one's head, before adjusting the straps around the head (Ramah, <i>Orach Chayim</i> 25:8).</i>
When a person removes his <i>tefillin</i> to place them in a container,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the bag in which the <i>tefillin</i> are held, and not the cases in which they are usually placed. Based on <i>Shulchan Aruch HaRav</i> 25:3, it appears that as long as the <i>tefillin</i> are enclosed in these cases, the laws mentioned below would be modified slightly.</i> he should not place the arm <i>tefillah</i> below and the head <i>tefillah</i> above, because when he wants to put them on, he will encounter the head <i>tefillah</i> first.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">From <i>Shulchan Aruch HaRav</i> 25:3 and the <i>Mishnah Berurah</i> 25:3, it appears that "encounter" must be taken literally. One need not actually hold the <i>tefillin</i> for these laws to apply.</i> Thus, he will [be forced to] ignore it and take out the arm <i>tefillah</i>, since one should not put on the head <i>tefillah</i> before the arm <i>tefillah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">as explained in Halachah 5.</i><br>[This is undesirable because] it is forbidden for a person to ignore one mitzvah and proceed to the fulfillment of another mitzvah.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">From the Rambam's statements, it appears that even when one encounters the head <i>tefillin</i> first, it is proper to put on the arm <i>tefillin</i> first. Although there are authorities (e.g., Rabbenu Yerucham) who do not accept this decision, the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 25:6) rules in this manner.<br>This decision can be explained as follows: The concept not to bypass the performance of a mitzvah is a Rabbinic law. (Although below, a verse from the Torah is cited as the source for this concept, that verse is merely an allusion.) In contrast, the obligation to put the arm <i>tefillin</i> on first is derived from the Torah itself. Hence, it is given priority (<i>Mishneh Berurah</i> 25:23).</i> Rather, as soon as a mitzvah comes to a person's hand, he should occupy himself with it.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">On Exodus 12:17, "And you shall watch the <i>matzot,</i>" the <i>Mechilta</i> comments, "Do not read '<i>matzot</i>'; read 'mitzvot,' and explains that just as <i>matzot</i> must be baked hurriedly so they do not leaven, mitzvot should be performed with eagerness, without delay.</i> Therefore, a person should place the arm <i>tefillin</i> above, so that he will encounter it first, and thus put on the <i>tefillin</i> in the proper sequence.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam's statements are quoted by the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 28:2). <i>Shulchan Aruch HaRav</i> 28:8 and the <i>Mishnah Berurah</i> 28:7, however, suggest that it is preferable not to place the arm <i>tefillin</i> directly above the head <i>tefillin</i>, because the head <i>tefillin</i> possess a higher level of holiness (Chapter 3, Halachah 17). Rather, a wide bag should be made, where they can be placed side by side, with the arm <i>tefillin</i> slightly above.</i>
A container that was made for <i>tefillin</i> to be placed in and which was used<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">even once (<i>Shulchan Aruch, Orach Chayim</i> 42:3)</i> for that purpose<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">As will be explained, both these particulars are necessary.</i> becomes holy. It is forbidden to use it for mundane purposes.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">e.g., to serve as a container for ordinary articles. If, however, when the container was made, the person had the intent to use it for <i>tefillin</i> only temporarily, and then to use it for another purpose, the Ramah (<i>Orach Chayim</i>, <i>loc. cit.</i>) allows it to be used for mundane purposes.</i><br>If a container was prepared for that purpose, but never used for it,<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">This ruling depends on the principle mentioned in the commentary on Chapter 3, Halachah 17, that הזמנה, designating an article to be used for a sacred purpose, is not sufficient for holiness to be imparted to the article. It must first be used for the purpose for which it was intended.</i> or if a container was temporarily<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Shulchan Aruch HaRav</i> 42:4 and the <i>Mishnah Berurah</i> 42:24 explain that even if a person used a container for <i>tefillin</i> many times, as long as he never intended the container to be used for this purpose continually, it is not considered as holy. Should, however, a person even once consider the container as intended for <i>tefillin</i>, it may never be used for another purpose.</i> used for <i>tefillin</i>, but was not prepared for that purpose, it does not become holy. Rather, it is considered an ordinary article as before.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Shulchan Aruch HaRav</i>, <i>loc. cit.</i>, allows one to use the container for mundane purposes at the same time it is used for <i>tefillin</i>. The <i>Mishnah Berurah</i> 42:26, however, suggests that this does not show respect for the <i>tefillin</i>.</i><br>It is forbidden to suspend <i>tefillin</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">from a hook. Doing so is considered as disgracing the mitzvah. <i>Berachot</i> 24a states, "The life of a person who hangs <i>tefillin</i> will also hang [by a thread]."</i> [This applies regardless of whether one suspends them] by their straps or from the <i>tefillah</i> itself.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">Holding the head <i>tefillin</i> by the straps and letting the <i>tefillah</i> hang while putting it on is not included in this prohibition (<i>Shulchan Aruch HaRav</i> 40:1; <i>Mishnah Berurah</i> 40:1). It is, nevertheless, proper to put them on without letting the <i>tefillah</i> hang.</i> One may, however, suspend the bag in which they are placed.
The time for wearing <i>tefillin</i> is the day and not the night, as [Exodus 13:10] states: "And you shall observe this statute in its appointed time, from day to day." "This statute" refers to the mitzvah of <i>tefillin</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Most commentaries on the Torah interpret "this statute" as referring to the Paschal sacrifice, and render the Hebrew מימים ימימה as "from year to year." Though this is the accepted meaning with regard to the Torah's simple interpretation, from a Halachic perspective the matter is the subject of a debate between the Sages (<i>Menachot</i> 36b), and the interpretation quoted by the Rambam is advanced by Rabbi Yosse HaG'lili.</i><br>Similarly, Sabbaths and holidays<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">The expression יום טוב generally refers to the days of the festival on which it is forbidden to do work - and not <i>chol hamo'ed</i>, the intermediate days. Thus, it would appear that the Rambam requires wearing <i>tefillin</i> on <i>chol hamo'ed</i>. Similarly, the <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> cites<i>Hilchot Sh'vitat Yom Tov</i> 7:13 which also appears to indicate that one should put on <i>tefillin</i> on <i>chol hamo'ed</i>.<br>The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> concludes by stating that, originally, it was customary to wear <i>tefillin</i> on <i>chol hamo'ed</i>.
