Afterwards, the later Rabbis discovered a statement of the <i>Zohar Chadash</i> (<i>Shir HaShirim</i> 1:3), which severely criticizes those who wear <i>tefillin</i> on these days, and the custom of not wearing <i>tefillin</i> spread.<br>The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 31:1) forbids wearing <i>tefillin</i> on <i>chol hamo'ed</i>. The Ramah (<i>loc. cit.</i>) maintains that they should be worn and a blessing recited. At present, even those who wear <i>tefillin</i> on <i>chol hamo'ed</i> generally do not recite a blessing (<i>Mishnah Berurah</i> 31:8). In the Sephardic and Chassidic communities and, similarly, in all communities in <i>Eretz Yisrael</i>, it is customary not to wear <i>tefillin</i> on these days.</i> are not days on which <i>tefillin</i> [are worn],<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Not only are we not obligated to wear <i>tefillin</i> on these days, doing so is considered to be a disgrace to the Sabbath and a transgression of the prohibition forbidding us to add to the Torah's commandments (<i>Shulchan Aruch HaRav</i> 31:1; <i>Mishnah Berurah</i> 31:5). (See also the Rambam's Commentary on the Mishnah: <i>Eruvin</i> 10:1; <i>Beitzah</i> 1:10.)</i> as [Exodus 13:9] states: "And they shall be a sign for you."<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">The citation of this proof-text reflects a general pattern within the <i>Mishneh Torah</i>. As mentioned in <i>Menachot</i> (<i>loc. cit.</i>), there is a debate whether the verse, "And you shall observe this statute..." applies to <i>tefillin</i> or the Pesach.<br>Rabbi Yosse HaG'lili interprets the verse as referring to <i>tefillin</i>, and explains that it teaches that <i>tefillin</i> should not be worn at night, nor on Sabbaths and holidays. Rabbi Akiva differs, and interprets the verse as referring to the Paschal sacrifice. Though he maintains that <i>tefillin</i> should be worn at night, he agrees that they should not be worn on Sabbaths and holidays, and derives that concept from the verse, "And they shall be a sign...."<br>The Rambam quotes the proof-texts cited by both the differing opinions because each one clearly alludes to the halachic principles he seeks to express (Radbaz, Vol. V, Responsum 1693). For this same reason, he sees no difficulty in quoting the verse, "And you shall observe these statutes..." in <i>Hilchot Kiddush HaChodesh</i> 1:7 according to the interpretation of Rabbi Akiva.</i> Since the Sabbaths and the holidays are themselves signs,<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">See Exodus 31:13: "It (the Sabbath) is a sign between Me and you." This sign refers to the mitzvot associated with the Sabbath and holidays (<i>Tosafot</i>) or to the prohibition against performing work on these days (Rabbenu Asher).</i> [the sign of <i>tefillin</i> is unnecessary].<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Two witnesses are required by Torah law. Thus, at all times we must have two signs of our commitment to Torah. One is circumcision; the other, either <i>tefillin</i>, or the Sabbath or the festivals.</i><br>When does the time to put them on begin? When one can see<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">13 Rabbenu Yonah explains that <i>tefillin</i> are associated with sight because the Torah also mentions them in connection with that sense.<i>Menachot</i> 35b interprets the verse, "And all the nations of the earth shall see that the name of God is called upon you" (Deuteronomy 28:10 , as a reference to <i>tefillin</i>.</i> a colleague standing four cubits away<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">The Jerusalem Talmud (<i>Berachot</i> 1:2) explains that this refers to a colleague with whom one shares occasional contact. One would recognize a close friend easily, and a person with whom one is not acquainted would never be recognized.</i> and recognize him.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">This time is between עלות השחר, "dawn," the first shinings of the sun's rays, and הנץ החמה, "sunrise," the appearance of the sun on the horizon.</i> [It continues] until sunset.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">More particularly, most authorities interpret the Rambam to be referring to the appearance of the stars. The <i>Ari zal</i>, however, states that the <i>tefillin</i> should be removed at sunset (<i>Mishnah Berurah</i> 30:15).</i>
It is permitted for a person who put on <i>tefillin</i> before sunset to continue wearing them after nightfall.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Menachot</i> 36a relates that Rav Ashi wore his <i>tefillin</i> at night.</i> They may even remain upon him the entire night.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">One must, however, remove them before going to sleep. Sleeping in <i>tefillin</i> is forbidden, as stated in Halachah 15.</i><br>This law, however, is not to be taught in public.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">lest the common people fail to treat the matter with the proper concern.</i> Instead, everyone should be taught not to wear <i>tefillin</i> at night and to remove them before sunset.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">lest one fall asleep while wearing them.</i><br>A person who puts on <i>tefillin</i> after sunset transgresses a Scriptural prohibition,<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">This prohibition is not, however, included as one of the 613 mitzvot, nor is it punished by lashes. The Radbaz (Vol. V, Responsum 1468) explains that since a person may continue wearing <i>tefillin</i> at night, we see that this prohibition does not have the same force as others. Therefore, its violation is not punished by lashes.</i> as [implied by Exodus 13:10]: "And you shall observe<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">The word השמר and its derivitives connote a Torah prohibition (<i>Menachot</i> 36b).</i> this statute... from day to day."<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam's statements represent an intermediate position between the opinions of the Ashkenazic authorities, who maintain that the prohibition against wearing <i>tefillin</i> at night is merely a Rabbinic decree, and the practice of "the inhabitants of <i>Eretz Yisrael</i> who would recite the blessing, 'who sanctified us with His commandments and commanded us to observe His statutes' before removing their <i>tefillin</i> at night.<br>According to the Rambam, the prohibition against wearing <i>tefillin</i> at night stems from the Torah. A blessing, nevertheless, is not required when removing them.</i>
When a person is wearing <i>tefillin</i> in the midst of a journey and the sun sets, marking the commencement of the Sabbath,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Our translation follows the standard published text of the <i>Mishneh Torah</i>, which reflects Rashi's commentary in <i>Beitzah</i> 15a.<br>Other versions of the text divide the halachah in two: with the first clause speaking about nightfall during the week, and the second clause speaking about the commencement of the Sabbath. The manner in which these laws are stated in the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 30:4) follows the latter version.</i> he should cover them with his hand<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">so that others will not see him and think that it is proper to wear <i>tefillin</i> at this time</i> until he reaches his home<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Since one wears <i>tefillin</i> like a garment, taking them home in this manner does not constitute a violation of the prohibition against carrying on the Sabbath.</i> and then, remove them.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">According to the versions which state that this clause applies during the week, the person does not remove the <i>tefillin</i> and carry them during his journey, lest they fall from his hand.</i><br>If he is sitting in the house of study<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">In Talmudic times, the houses of study were located in the fields, and it was not safe to leave articles there.</i> and the sun sets, marking the commencement of the Sabbath,<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">The authorities agree that this refers to sunset and not the appearance of the stars, since it is forbidden to carry from sunset onwards.</i> he should cover them with his hand until he reaches his home, and then remove them.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">he should cover them with his hand until he reaches his home, and then remove them.</i><br> If there is a house located near the wall [of a city] where they would be safe, he should place them there.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">This applies both on the Sabbath and during the week. Since the person has a safe place where the <i>tefillin</i> could be kept, he is not allowed to continue wearing them.</i> If, however, he did not remove his <i>tefillin</i> after sunset because he did not have a place where they would be safe, it is permissible for him to continue wearing them in order to protect them.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> explains that, although in the previous halachah, the Rambam had also stated that it is permissible to continue wearing <i>tefillin</i> at night, people should not be informed about this leniency. In contrast, when one's intent is to protect the <i>tefillin</i>, one may wear them without compunction (<i>Kessef Mishneh</i>). One may not, however, put on <i>tefillin</i> at night even for the purpose of protecting them (<i>Shulchan Aruch HaRav</i> 30:4; <i>Mishnah Berurah</i> 30:9).</i>
All those who are exempt from the obligation to recite the <i>Shema</i><sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">This includes:a) women and Canaanite slaves (<i>Hilchot Kri'at Shema</i> 4:1), who are not obligated by either of these mitzvot, since these mitzvot are associated with certain time limits (מצות עשה שהזמן גרמה).b) minors, who, according to Torah law, are not obligated to perform any mitzvot. (There is, however, a Rabbinic obligation to educate them in the performance of mitzvot.)c) individuals whose thoughts are unsettled or who are occupied with the performance of other mitzvot. (See the subsequent halachot of <i>Hilchot Kri'at Shema</i>, Chapter 4.)</i> are exempt from the obligation to wear <i>tefillin</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Significantly, in <i>Hilchot Tzitzit</i> 3:9, the Rambam mentions that women and slaves may wear <i>tzitzit</i> or perform any other mitzvah which they are not obligated to fulfill. Perhaps, he does not make that statement with regard to <i>tefillin</i>, because as the Ramah (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 38:3) states, the obligation to concentrate one's thoughts on the <i>tefillin</i> and to control one's body is the reason that women should not take on the practice of wearing <i>tefillin</i>. Our Sages, nevertheless, cite the example of Michal, King Saul's daughter, who would wear <i>tefillin</i>.</i> If a minor knows [the importance of] guarding his <i>tefillin</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">who knows not to sleep, not to enter a lavatory, and not to release gas while wearing them (<i>Shulchan Aruch</i>, Ramah, <i>Orach Chayim</i> 37:3)</i> his father should obtain <i>tefillin</i> for him,<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">In some Sephardic communities, it is customary for children to wear <i>tefillin</i> from the age of nine. In Ashkenazic communities, however, a minor begins putting on <i>tefillin</i> two to three months before becoming Bar-Mitzvah (<i>Shulchan Aruch HaRav</i> 37:3; <i>Mishnah Berurah</i> 37:12).</i> to educate him regarding the performance of mitzvot.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Many times throughout the <i>Mishneh Torah</i> - e.g., <i>Hilchot Tzitzit</i> 3:9, <i>Hilchot Berachot</i> 5:1, <i>Hilchot Sukkah</i> 6:1 - the Rambam mentions a father's obligation to educate his children regarding the performance of mitzvot.</i><br>A person with stomach problems and anyone who can contain his excretory functions only with difficulty<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">because he may release gas (see Halachah 15) and because he may not be able to concentrate on the <i>tefillin</i></i> are exempt from the obligation to wear <i>tefillin</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">If, however, one feels that he can control his body and thoughts for a limited time, he should wear them for that interval (<i>Shulchan Aruch, Orach Chayim</i> 38:2).</i> [In contrast,] all those ritually impure are obligated to wear <i>tefillin</i> like one who is pure.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Sefer Torah</i> 10:8.</i><br>A person who suffers discomfort, or someone who is disturbed and cannot concentrate his thoughts, is exempt from the obligation to wear <i>tefillin</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">The Jerusalem Talmud (<i>Berachot</i> 2:3) cites the example of Rabbi Yannai, who would not put on <i>tefillin</i> until the third day after he recovered from illness.</i> since a person who wears <i>tefillin</i> is forbidden to divert his attention from them.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">as explained in the following halachah.</i><br>Priests who are in the midst of [the Temple] service,<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">offering or partaking of the sacrifices</i> Levites who chant<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">while the sacrifices are being offered</i> on the platform,<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">the three steps which separated the courtyard of the Israelites from the Priestly Courtyard. (See <i>Hilchot Beit HaBechirah</i> 6:2.)</i> and Israelites while they are attending the Temple [ceremonies]<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the Israelites who were part of the <i>ma'amadot</i> and would attend the Temple service as emissaries of the entire Jewish people. (See <i>Hilchot Klei HaMikdash</i> 6:5.)</i> are exempt from the obligation to pray and to wear <i>tefillin</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">Since a person who is occupied with the performance of one mitzvah is exempt from the obligation of performing others (Rashi, <i>Zevachim</i> 19a).<br>That passage continues to explain that these individuals were allowed to wear <i>tefillin</i> and would do so - with the exception of the priests, who would not wear the arm <i>tefillin</i>, for doing so would cause a separation between the priestly garments and their flesh. (See also <i>Hilchot Klei HaMikdash</i> 10:3,6.)</i>
A person should touch his <i>tefillin</i><sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">touching the arm <i>tefillin</i> before the head <i>tefillin</i> (<i>Shulchan Aruch, Orach Chayim</i> 28:1)</i> [from time to time] during the entire time he is wearing them,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i>, <i>loc. cit.</i>) quotes this law and adds that, in particular, one should touch the <i>tefillin</i> when reciting the verse, "And you shall tie them... and they shall be..." in the <i>Shema</i>.</i> so that he will not divert his attention<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Mishnah Berurah</i> (28:1) also mentions that one should check that they have not moved from their proper place.</i> from them even for a single moment,<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">As mentioned in the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 25:5), when putting on <i>tefillin</i>, a person should intend "to subjugate his desires and thoughts to God." There is no need, however, for this intent to be in the forefront of one's thoughts throughout the entire time one is wearing <i>tefillin</i>. On the contrary, in Talmudic times (see Halachah 25), <i>tefillin</i> would be worn throughout the day, even during one's involvement in mundane affairs.<br>Accordingly, the Rabbis (see <i>Shulchan Aruch HaRav</i> 28:1; <i>Mishnah Berurah</i> 44:3) explain that here, by "diversion of attention," the Rabbis meant that a person's mind should not become focused on frivolous matters or on his bodily needs to the extent that he forgets about the fear of God. Thus, the Ramah (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 38:4) states that a person who cannot control himself from thinking about lewd things should not put on <i>tefillin</i>, and <i>Shulchan Aruch HaRav</i> 38:8 and the <i>Mishnah Berurah</i> 38:30 free a person who is troubled by cold from the obligation of wearing <i>tefillin</i>.<br>The <i>Sha'agat Arieh</i> (Responsum 39), however, interprets the Rambam's words very strictly and maintains that, according to the Rambam, even the slightest diversion of attention from <i>tefillin</i> is forbidden.</i> for<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam emphasizes that because <i>tefillin</i> possess such great holiness, diverting one's attention from them would be considered an act of disrespect, and is therefore forbidden (<i>Likkutei Sichot</i>, Vol. 14).</i> their holiness surpasses that of the <i>tzitz</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">The head-plate worn by the High Priest. (See Exodus 28:36-38; <i>Hilchot Klei HaMikdash</i> 9:1-2.)</i> The <i>tzitz</i> has God's name [written] upon it only once,<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">The words קדש לי-ה-ו-ה (consecrated unto God) are embossed on it.</i> while the head <i>tefillin</i><sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">and similarly, the arm <i>tefillin</i> - contain the name י-ה-ו-ה 21 times - in the passages from the Torah they contain.</i> - and similarly, the arm <i>tefillin</i> - contain the name י-ה-ו-ה 21 times.
<i>tefillin</i> require a clean body;<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">In this context,<i>Shabbat</i> 130a cites the example of Elisha, <i>ba'al hak'nafayim</i>.</i> i.e., that one should be careful not to release gas while wearing them.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">As mentioned in Halachah 13, a person who cannot control himself and feels that he must release gas, is exempt from the obligation of wearing <i>tefillin</i>.</i><br>Accordingly, it is forbidden to sleep while wearing them<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Shabbat</i> 130a explains that the Rabbis forbade sleeping in <i>tefillin</i> lest one accidentally release gas. Significantly, the Rabbis did not consider sleeping a diversion of one's attention from the <i>tefillin</i> (<i>Shulchan Aruch HaRav</i> 44:1; <i>Mishnah Berurah</i> 44:3).</i> - neither a long sleep nor a nap. If, however, one places a cloth over them<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> interprets <i>Sukkah</i> 26a as indicating that this will keep the person's attention focused on the <i>tefillin</i> and prevent him from releasing gas.</i> and a woman is not with him,<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">lest this lead to sexual relations, which are forbidden in the presence of <i>tefillin</i>, as stated in Halachah 24.</i> one may nap while wearing them. What should he do? Place his head between his knees and sleep while sitting.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">This will prevent him from falling into a deep slumber in which he might lose control of himself.</i>
A person who has <i>tefillin</i> wound around his hand<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">If, however, he is merely holding them, it is forbidden, lest they drop from his hand (<i>Kessef Mishneh</i>). The Ramah (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 44:1) states that if the <i>tefillin</i> are placed in a container, there are no restrictions.</i> is permitted to sleep with them. [This applies] even to a long sleep.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Since he is not wearing them, there is no difficulty even if he were to release gas (<i>Shulchan Aruch HaRav</i> 44:1; <i>Mishnah Berurah</i> 44:6).</i><br>He may eat only a snack<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">a meal of less than a<i>k'beitzah</i>, approximately 2 ounces in modern measure.</i> while wearing them. If, however, he enters to partake of a regular meal, he should remove them<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">lest he become drunk while eating and act in a manner unbecoming to the <i>tefillin</i> (<i>Berachot</i> 23b).</i> and place them on the table until after he washes his hands [at the conclusion of the meal].<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam discusses the obligation of washing after eating (<i>mayim acharonim</i>) in <i>Hilchot Berachot</i> 6:3.</i> Afterwards, he should put them on and recite grace over his meal while wearing them.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Wearing <i>tefillin</i> while reciting grace will add to one's concentration on the blessing.</i>
[The following rules apply with regard to a person] wearing <i>tefillin</i> who must use the lavatory:<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">In Talmudic times, the lavatories were outhouses in the fields.</i> [He should remove his <i>tefillin</i><sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Rashi (<i>Berachot</i> 23a) relates that this is a Rabbinic decree ordained lest one relieve himself while wearing them. There is no prohibition in the Torah itself against wearing <i>tefillin</i> in a place of filth.<br>[Note the contrast between this position and the prohibition against reciting prayers and other holy matters in a lavatory (<i>Hilchot Kri'at Shema</i> 3:2,4). The latter prohibition appears to have its source in the Torah itself.]</i> but,] while he enters, he should not place them in the holes [of the outer wall of the lavatory] which are near the public domain, lest they be taken by passersby.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Berachot</i> 23b relates that once, a student of Torah left his <i>tefillin</i> in a hole in the wall of a public lavatory. A woman took them, and afterwards came to the house of study. She told the student's colleagues, "See what so-and- so gave me as payment."<br>When the student heard this, he climbed to the roof of the house of study to hide himself in shame. Shortly afterwards, he slipped and fell to his death. When the story became known, the Sages ordained that a person should bring his <i>tefillin</i> into a lavatory lest the story repeat itself.</i><br>What should he do? Even if he [merely] has to urinate, he should remove his <i>tefillin</i> four cubits away [from the lavatory] and roll them in his clothes like a Torah scroll,<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Beit Yosef</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 43) points out that Rashi and other Ashkenazic authorities do not require that the <i>tefillin</i> be covered.</i> holding them near his heart with his right hand.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">since a person uses his left hand to wipe himself (<i>Shulchan Aruch HaRav</i> 43:3; <i>Mishnah Berurah</i> 43:20).</i> He must take care that the strap does not protrude more than a handbreadth from his hand.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">The straps also possess a dimension of holiness, and hence, care should be taken regarding them.</i> Afterwards, he should enter and relieve himself. After leaving the lavatory and walking more than four cubits away,<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">When our Sages established the decree forbidding a person from wearing <i>tefillin</i> in a lavatory, they added this distance as a safeguard.</i> he should put them on.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">It must be emphasized that this and the following two halachot no longer apply, since, at present, homes are equipped with toilet facilities, and there is no longer a need to use public outhouses. At home, or in other places where one could keep the <i>tefillin</i> in a safe place, it is forbidden to wear <i>tefillin</i> in a lavatory (<i>Shulchan Aruch HaRav</i> 43:5).1. See Halachah 11, which prohibits putting on <i>tefillin</i> after sunset.2. As explained in Halachah 24, a container which is always used for <i>tefillin</i> or other sacred articles is considered as an accessory to the <i>tefillin</i> with no importance of its own. Thus, it is as if the <i>tefillin</i> are being held without any covering at all. Nevertheless, if it is a handbreadth in size, it is given halachic significance as an <i>ohel</i>. Hence, it is considered to be a separation between the <i>tefillin</i> and the lavatory.<br>Needless to say, these laws apply only when the person does not have a safe place where he can deposit his <i>tefillin</i>. If such a place is available, he may not bring <i>tefillin</i> into a lavatory (<i>Kessef Mishneh</i>).3. See Halachah 24, which forbids engaging in intercourse in a room which contains <i>tefillin</i> that are open.4. Thus, it is feared that they may have touched the genitalia (Rashi, <i>Sukkah</i> 26b). Note the Ramah (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 40:7), who prohibits a person from wearing <i>tefillin</i> if he has any traces of semen on his body. 5. This halachah is dependent on the description of a bathhouse in<i>Shabbat</i> 10a. There were three rooms: a waiting room, a dressing room, and the actual bathing room.6. <i>Shulchan Aruch HaRav</i> 45:3 and the <i>Mishnah Berurah</i> 45:5 explain that this applies even when no one there is actually naked. Those sources also state that these laws apply only in bathhouses which contain filth. In contrast, there are no restrictions against wearing <i>tefillin</i> in the bathing room of a mikveh if no people are standing there naked. Compare also to <i>Hilchot Kri'at Shema</i> 3:3-4, 16.</i>
When does the above apply? In a permanent lavatory where drops [of urine] will not sprinkle upon him. In contrast, in a place that is temporarily being used as a lavatory, one should not enter, [holding <i>tefillin</i>] wound up in one's hand. Instead, one should remove them [outside the lavatory] and give them to a colleague to guard.<br>Urine cannot be expelled [without drops sprinkling] even in a permanent lavatory unless one squats. If [the floor is covered] with soft dust, [drops will not sprinkle] even when one stands. If the floor is hard, one should stand on an incline [and urinate], so that drops will not sprinkle on him.
When a person is wearing <i>tefillin</i> and must relieve himself in the evening, when there would be no time left in the day to put them on again after he finishes, he should not enter a lavatory, [holding them] rolled up in his clothes.<br>What should he do [instead]? He should remove them and place them in their container if it is a handbreadth in size, or in another container which is not specific for them even though it is not a handbreadth in size. He should hold the container in his hand when he enters the lavatory. Similarly, if he needs to relieve himself at night, he should place them in a container and enter the lavatory, holding the <i>tefillin</i> in his hand.
If a person forgot and entered a lavatory while wearing <i>tefillin</i>, he should cover them with his hand until he completes expelling the first issue of feces or urine, and then leave the lavatory, remove the <i>tefillin</i>, return, and complete relieving himself. Were one to interrupt in the midst of expelling the first outburst of feces or urine, he might become very dangerously ill.
If a person forgot and had intercourse while wearing <i>tefillin</i>, he should not hold either the straps or the compartments themselves until he washes his hands. [Then, he should] remove them. [This restriction was instituted] because hands are active.
[The following rules apply to] a person who enters a bathhouse: In the rooms where people stand clothed, it is permitted to put on <i>tefillin</i>. In the rooms where some of the people stand naked and some clothed, one need not remove one's <i>tefillin</i>, nor, at the outset, should one put <i>tefillin</i> on there. In the rooms where [everyone] stands naked, one should remove one's <i>tefillin</i> and, needless to say, one should not put them on.
A person should not walk in a cemetery<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">even if he is not within four cubits of a grave (<i>Shulchan Aruch HaRav</i> 45:1; <i>Mishnah Berurah</i> 45:1)</i> wearing his head <i>tefillin</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Arm <i>tefillin</i>, however, need not be removed, for they can be worn under one's sleeve. As mentioned in Halachah 4, the two <i>tefillin</i> are two separate mitzvot, whose observance is not necessarily dependent on each other. One must be careful, however, to cover the straps of the arm <i>tefillin</i>, even those around one's hand and finger (<i>Shulchan Aruch HaRav</i> 45:2; <i>Mishnah Berurah</i> 45:3).</i> Even [outside a cemetery], a person should remove his<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">head</i> <i>tefillin</i> if he comes within four cubits of a corpse or a grave,<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Proverbs 17:5 states: "One who mocks the poor (רש) reproaches his Creator." <i>Berachot</i> 18a explains that the word רש can also refer to the dead, and states that performing mitzvot in the presence of a corpse or by his graveside would be, in a certain sense, mocking him, since he is unable to perform mitzvot. Accordingly, the Sages forbade Torah study, the recitation of the <i>Shema</i>, and the performance of other mitzvot in these situations.</i> until he distances himself four cubits from them.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Beyond this distance, one is not considered to be in the actual presence of the corpse. If a cemetery is surrounded by a wall, one may wear <i>tefillin</i> outside the wall even within four cubits of a grave, because the wall constitutes a separation (<i>Shulchan Aruch HaRav</i> 45:1; <i>Mishnah Berurah</i> 45:1).</i><br>A person should not put on <i>tefillin</i> [when he is naked]. He must first cover his genitalia<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">From the Jerusalem Talmud (<i>Berachot</i> 2:3), it appears that, in addition to wearing a cloak over one's body, one must also cover one's genitalia before putting on <i>tefillin</i>.</i> and put on his clothes.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Shulchan Aruch HaRav</i> 45:3 and the <i>Mishnah Berurah</i> 45:5 associate this prohibition with the prohibition against standing naked in the presence of sacred texts.</i><br>A person who is carrying a load on his head should remove his head <i>tefillin</i> [and not put it on again] until he puts down his load.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Bava Metzia</i> 105b considers this a lack of respect for the <i>tefillin</i>.</i> It is even forbidden to wear a handkerchief<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">or any other article which is not a garment and is not usually worn on one's head (<i>Shulchan Aruch HaRav</i> 41:1)</i> around one's head when wearing <i>tefillin</i>. One may, however, wear a hat over the <i>tefillin</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Preferably, the <i>tefillin</i> themselves should not be covered. Since the hat is being worn as a garment, it is not considered disrespectful to the <i>tefillin</i>. Our Sages relate that the High Priest would wear his turban over the <i>tefillin</i> (<i>Zevachim</i> 19b), and the king would wear his crown above the <i>tefillin</i> (<i>Avodah Zarah</i> 44a).</i>
When <i>tefillin</i> or a Torah scroll are in a room, it is forbidden to engage in sexual relations, unless they are removed or placed into a container, and that container placed into a second container<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">These laws also apply with regard to sacred texts (<i>Mishnah Berurah</i> 40:4) and mezuzot (<i>Shulchan Aruch HaRav</i> 40:5).</i> which is not specific to them.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Covering the <i>tefillin</i>'s container with another garment is sufficient. There is no need for a second container. The bag in which the <i>tefillin</i> bag and the <i>tallit</i> are placed is also considered specific to the <i>tefillin</i> bag and another covering is necessary (<i>Shulchan Aruch HaRav</i> 40:3; <i>Mishnah Berurah</i> 40:7).</i> If, however, the second container designated for them, even ten<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">or a hundred (<i>Shulchan Aruch, Orach Chayim</i> 40:2).</i> containers are considered as a single container.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Note that in Chapter 10, Halachah 7, the Rambam offers a third alternative: setting up a partition ten handbreadths high between a Torah scroll and the bed. This is also acceptable for <i>tefillin</i>. See also the commentary on that halachah.</i><br>Should a person place [the sacred articles] in two containers, he may place them at the head of his bed,<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Placing them at the foot of the bed is considered to be disrespectful to the <i>tefillin</i> and is forbidden, even though one is not sleeping together with one's wife (<i>Shulchan Aruch, Orach Chayim</i> 40:3).</i> between a cushion and a pillow,<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Our translation of רכ and תסכ is based on Rav Kapach's text of the Rambam's Commentary on the Mishnah, <i>Shabbat</i> 4:2. The <i>Aruch</i> renders the phrase, "between the mattress and the pillow."</i> as long as they are not under his head,<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Placing them under one's head would be tantamount to using them as a pillow and would be considered disrespectful to the <i>tefillin</i>. Hence, it is forbidden at all times (<i>Shulchan Aruch</i>, <i>loc. cit.</i>).</i> so that he can protect them.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">from thieves; alternatively, from mice (Rashi, <i>Berachot</i> 24a).</i> [This applies] even if his wife is together with him in bed.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">and they intend to be intimate (<i>Shulchan Aruch</i>, <i>loc. cit.</i>).</i>
The holiness associated with <i>tefillin</i> is very great.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">See Halachah 14 above and also <i>Hilchot Sh'vuot</i> 11:11-12, where the Rambam equates the sanctity of <i>tefillin</i> with that of a Torah scroll.</i> As long as a person is wearing <i>tefillin</i> on his head and arm, he will be humble and God-fearing and will not be drawn to frivolous behavior or empty speech.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Berachot</i> 30b relates that when Rabbah reproached Abbaye for light-headedness, which he thought would lead to frivolous behavior, Abbaye answered him, "I am wearing <i>tefillin</i>," implying that the <i>tefillin</i> would prevent him from losing self-control.</i> He will not turn his thoughts to evil matters, but rather will direct his heart to words of truth and justice.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Menachot</i> 43b states that a person who wears <i>tefillin</i> on his head and arm will surely not sin.</i><br>Accordingly, a person should try to wear [<i>tefillin</i>] throughout the entire day,<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">In Talmudic times, not only Torah scholars, but also common people would wear their <i>tefillin</i> throughout the entire day. In the later generations, this practice was generally followed only by Torah scholars, as the <i>Hagahot Maimoniot</i> relates in the name of Rav Amram Gaon:<br>We saw the <i>Geonim</i>, the heads of the court, and the giants of the previous generations... who would not remove their <i>tefillin</i> until after... the <i>Shema</i> of the evening service.<br>Nevertheless, at present, even Torah scholars have accepted the practice of wearing <i>tefillin</i> only during the morning prayers. This practice was instituted since <i>tefillin</i> require "a clean body" (Halachah 15) and one cannot divert his attention from them (Halachah 14), and most people cannot meet these requirements (<i>Shulchan Aruch, Orach Chayim</i> 37:2).</i> for this is the mitzvah associated with them.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Note the questions in the <i>Pri Megadim</i> and the <i>Bi'ur Halachah</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 37): Is the mitzvah of <i>tefillin</i> to wear them all day, or is the mitzvah to put <i>tefillin</i> on once each day, with the remaining time one wears them being merely the continuation of the mitzvah.</i> Among the praises conveyed upon Rav, the student of Rabbenu Hakadosh,<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Though we find <i>Megillah</i> 28a conveying such praise on Rav Zeira, there is no passage in the Talmud which describes Rav in these terms. Nevertheless, the responsa of the <i>Geonim</i> (<i>Damasek Eliezer</i> 178) include these among the ten acts of pious behavior for which Rav was noted.</i> was that he was never seen walking four cubits without [reciting words of] Torah, without <i>tzitzit</i>, and without <i>tefillin</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Yesodei HaTorah</i> 5:11, which describes such behavior as an example of <i>Kiddush Hashem</i> (the sanctification of God's name).</i>
Although it is a mitzvah to wear [<i>tefillin</i>] throughout the entire day, it is most important during prayer. Our Sages declared: "Whoever recites the <i>Shema</i> without <i>tefillin</i> is considered as if he is giving false testimony."<br>Whoever does not wear <i>tefillin</i> transgresses eight positive commandments, for in each of the four passages contained in the <i>tefillin</i> we are commanded to wear both head and arm <i>tefillin</i>. [The rewards for wearing <i>tefillin</i> are also great.] Whoever wears <i>tefillin</i> regularly will live long, as [implied by Isaiah 38:16]: "God is upon them, they shall live."

Chapter 5

How is a <i>mezuzah</i> written?<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sefer HaMitzvot</i> (Positive Commandment 15) and <i>Sefer HaChinuch</i> (Mitzvah 423) consider the mitzvah of <i>mezuzah</i> to be one of the 613 mitzvot of the Torah.</i> The two portions, <i>Shema</i> and <i>V'hayah im shamo'a</i>, are written on one piece of parchment in a single column.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">The Jerusalem Talmud (<i>Megillah</i> 1:9) states that unlike a Torah scroll or <i>tefillin</i>, a <i>mezuzah</i> should be written in a single column.</i> Approximately half a fingernail of space<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">approximately one centimeter</i> should be left above and below [the writing].<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Also, a small amount of parchment should be left on the right side for the <i>mezuzah</i> to be rolled closed (<i>Shulchan Aruch, Yoreh De'ah</i> 288:1).</i><br>Should one write [a <i>mezuzah</i>] in two or three columns, it is acceptable,<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">although this is not the desired form</i> as long as it not written tail-shaped,<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., wider above than below, the top lines being longer than the bottom ones</i> in a circle,<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">This word is not found in our text of <i>Menachot</i> 31b, the source for this halachah.</i> or tent-shaped.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., wider below than above, the bottom lines being longer than the top ones.</i> If it was written using any of these forms, it is not acceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Menachot</i> 31b.</i><br>If it was not written in order<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">e.g., one wrote the passage [V'hayah im shamo'a] before the passage [Shema] - it is not acceptable. - Furthermore, if even one letter from a <i>mezuzah</i> was not written in order, the <i>mezuzah</i> is unacceptable (<i>Shulchan Aruch, Yoreh De'ah</i> 288:3).<br>The <i>Ginat Veradim</i> suggests that the Rambam requires only that the order of the two passages not be reversed, but is not concerned with the order of the letters within the passages themselves. This perspective, however, is not accepted by other authorities, who explain that surely, the Rambam requires order within the passages. He gave the above example only because he felt that it was more important to emphasize that even if the passages themselves were written in order, if their order was reversed, the <i>mezuzah</i> is not acceptable.</i> - e.g., one wrote the passage [<i>V'hayah im shamo'a</i>] before the passage [<i>Shema</i>] - it is not acceptable. If one writes a <i>mezuzah</i> on two different parchments, it is not acceptable even if they were sewn together [later].<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">In his Responsa (213), Rabbi Akiva Eiger writes that if the parchments were sewn together before the passages were written upon them, the <i>mezuzah</i> is acceptable.</i><br>A <i>mezuzah</i> should not be made from a Torah scroll<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., one may not cut the passage, <i>Shema</i>, from a worn Torah scroll and write the passage, <i>V'hayah im shamo'a</i>, on the empty space below it. One may not cut both passages from the Torah scroll since, as explained above, a <i>mezuzah</i> may not be written on two different parchments (<i>Siftei Cohen, Yoreh De'ah</i> 290:1).</i> or <i>tefillin</i> that have become worn,<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">Though it is possible to cut the two passages from the arm <i>tefillin</i>, it is forbidden to do so.</i> nor should a <i>mezuzah</i> be written on the empty parchment from a Torah scroll,<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">the empty parchment left as a border above and on the sides of the Torah scroll. The <i>Or Sameach</i> notes that<i>Shabbat</i> 116a questions whether or not these empty portions of parchment have become sanctified with the holiness of a Torah scroll, and does not resolve the issue. Because of the doubt involved, the Rambam rules that in situations where the question of their holiness is raised, one should always take the more stringent perspective. Therefore, in <i>Hilchot Shabbat</i> 23:27, the Rambam rules that they should not be saved from a fire on the Sabbath if the violation of even a Rabbinic transgression is involved (accepting the possibility that they have not become consecrated). In this halachah, this approach requires accepting the possibility that they have been consecrated.</i> because one should not lower an article from a higher level of holiness<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in Chapter 10, Halachot 2-5 (see also <i>Hilchot Tefillah</i> 11:14), the holiness of a Torah scroll surpasses that of all other articles.</i> to a lesser one.<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">Our Sages have established the principle, "One may ascend to a higher level of holiness, but may not descend to a lower one."</i>
It is a mitzvah to leave a space between the passage <i>Shema</i> and the passage <i>V'hayah im shamo'a</i>, as if it were <i>s'tumah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>S'tumah</i> means "closed." According to the Rambam, it refers to a passage whose first word is always written in the middle of a line in the Torah. See Chapter 8, Halachah 2. In the Torah, <i>V'hayah im shamo'a</i> is <i>s'tumah</i>.</i> If space were left as if it were <i>p'tuchah</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><i>P'tuchah</i> means "open." According to the Rambam, it refers to a passage whose first word is always written at the beginning of a line in the Torah. See Chapter 8, Halachah 1.</i> it is acceptable, since these passages do not follow each other in the Torah.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Note the contrast between this ruling and the Rambam's decision in Chapter 2, Halachah 2, regarding <i>tefillin</i>: "If one wrote a passage which should be <i>s'tumah</i> as <i>p'tuchah</i> or a passage which should be <i>p'tuchah</i> as <i>s'tumah</i>, it is unacceptable."<br>The <i>Beit Yosef</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 32) explains the difference as follows: In <i>tefillin</i>, there are passages which follow directly after each other in the Torah. Therefore, all the passages must be written as they appear in a Torah scroll. In contrast, the two passages contained in a <i>mezuzah</i> do not follow each other in the Torah. Therefore, there is no absolute requirement for the passages to be written as they appear in the Torah.</i><br>One must take care regarding the crowns<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 2, Halachot 8-9 and Chapter 7, Halachot 8-9.</i> [on the letters] in a <i>mezuzah</i>. The following letters should have crowns.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam lists the letters in the following halachah. Significantly, the letters he mentions here are not the same as he mentions in Chapter 2 with regard to <i>tefillin</i>. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 288:7) rules that, in a <i>mezuzah</i>, just as in a Torah, one should place crowns on the letters, שעטנ"ז ג"ץ.</i>
In the first passage, there are seven letters which should each have three <i>zeiynin</i> upon it. They are: The <i>shin</i> and the <i>ayin</i> of [the word] <i>Shema</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">In Chapter 2, Halachah 6, the Rambam writes that, when writing <i>tefillin</i>, the <i>ayin</i> of the word <i>Shema</i> and the <i>dalet</i> of the word <i>echad</i> should be enlarged as in a Torah scroll. It is customary to write these letters in the same manner in <i>mezuzot</i>.</i> the <i>nun</i> of [the word] <i>nafsh'cha</i>, the two <i>zeiynin</i> of [the word] <i>mezuzot</i>, and the two <i>tettin</i> of the word <i>totafot</i>.<br>In the second passage, there are six letters each of which should have three <i>zeiynin</i> upon it. They are: The <i>gimmel</i> of [the word] <i>d'ganecha</i>, the two <i>zeiynin</i> of [the word] <i>mezuzot</i>, the two <i>tettin</i> of the word <i>totafot</i>, and the <i>tzadi</i> of [the word] <i>ha'aretz</i>.<br>If no crowns were made, or one increased or decreased their number, [the <i>mezuzah</i>] is not invalidated.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">As mentioned in the commentary on Chapter 2, Halachah 9, based on Rabbenu Asher's opinion, it is proper to add crowns if they have been omitted from the appropriate letters in a <i>mezuzah</i>.</i> If the <i>mezuzah</i> was not written on ruled [parchment],<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 1, Halachah 12, where this subject is discussed.</i> if [the scribe] was not exact with regard to the use of the full or short form [of the words,<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 2, Halachot 6-7.</i> or if [the scribe] added even a single letter inside<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Making additions on the outside of the <i>mezuzah</i> is discussed in the following halachah.</i> [the <i>mezuzah</i>], it is invalidated.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Similarly, if a single letter is forgotten, the <i>mezuzah</i> is invalid (<i>Shulchan Aruch, Yoreh De'ah</i> 288:3).</i>
It is a common custom to write [God's name,] <i>Shaddai</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">This name serves as an acronym for the Hebrew words, שומר דלתות ישראל, "Guardian of the gates of Israel" (<i>Mishnat Chassidim</i>).</i> on the outside of a <i>mezuzah</i> opposite the empty space left between the two passages.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> cites the <i>Zohar</i> (Vol. III, 266a) which states that <i>Shaddai</i> should be written opposite the word, <i>V'hayah</i>.</i> There is no difficulty in this, since [the addition is made] outside.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">See also the Ramah (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 288:15) who states that it is also common to write the letters, כוזו במוכסז כוזו, opposite the words, י-ה-ו-ה א-להנו י-ה-ו-ה, on the outer side of a <i>mezuzah</i>. These letters are the letters which follow the letters in those names of God - i.e., the כ follows the י, the ז follows the ו.</i><br>Those, however, who write the names of angels, other sacred names, verses, or forms, on the inside [of a <i>mezuzah</i>] are among those who do not have a portion in the world to come.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Hilchot Avodat Kochavim</i> 11:12, the Rambam states:<br>A person who whispers an incantation over a wound and then recites a verse from the Torah, who recites a verse over a child so that he will not become scared, or who places a Torah scroll or <i>tefillin</i> over a baby so that it will sleep, is considered to be a soothsayer or one who casts spells. Furthermore, such people are included among those who deny the Torah, because they relate to the words of the Torah as if they are cures for the body, when, in fact, they are cures for the soul, as [Proverbs 3:22] states: "And they shall be life for your soul."<br>The inclusion of these people among "those who do not have a portion in the world to come" is based on <i>Hilchot Teshuvah</i> 3:8, which makes such a statement about "those who deny the Torah."</i> Not only do these fools nullify the mitzvah,<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">As stated in the previous halachah, any addition made on the inside of the <i>mezuzah</i> invalidates it. (Significantly, the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> 288:15 states that it is forbidden to add to the inside of the <i>mezuzah</i>, but does not explicitly say that the <i>mezuzah</i> becomes invalidated.)</i> but furthermore, they make from a great mitzvah [which reflects] the unity of the name of the Holy One, blessed be He, the love of Him, and the service of Him,<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">These are the subjects discussed in the two passages written within the <i>mezuzah</i>.</i> a talisman for their own benefit. They, in their foolish conception, think that this will help them regarding the vanities of the world.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam's statements have aroused questions from many commentaries who note that, in several places, the Talmud associates a <i>mezuzah</i> with Divine protection - e.g., <i>Menachot</i> 33b, the Jerusalem Talmud, <i>Pe'ah</i> 1:1.<br>The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> resolves this difficulty explaining that, although a <i>mezuzah</i> affords Divine protection, that protection comes, in and of itself, in reward for the fulfillment of the mitzvah. There is no need for any additions on man's part. Indeed, a person who makes additions to the <i>mezuzah</i> in an attempt to increase its influence demonstrates that he is concerned with "his own benefit" and "the vanities of the world" and not with the fulfillment of God's mitzvah. Therefore, he deserves the Rambam's severe words of criticism. See <i>Likkutei Sichot</i>, Vol. 19.1. Our translation follows the standard text of the <i>Mishneh Torah</i>. The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> mentions another version which states "at the beginning or the end of the line." That version parallels the apparent source for the halachah, <i>Menachot</i> 31b.2. This custom is also recorded by Rav Yitzchak Alfasi, and earlier, in the halachot of Rav Yehudai Gaon. It is not clear when this custom was begun.3. As mentioned in Halachah 2, a space is left at the beginning of the line so that the passage, <i>V'hayah im shamo'a</i>, will be written in the <i>s'tumah</i> form.</i>
It is a mitzvah to write <i>al ha'aretz</i> on the final line [of a <i>mezuzah</i>], either at the beginning or in the middle of the line. It has become universally accepted custom for scribes to write [mezuzot] with 22 lines, with <i>al ha'aretz</i> at the beginning of the final line.<br>These are the letters that appear at the beginning of each line in order: <i>shema, י-ה-ו-ה, hadevarim, l'vanecha, uv'shochbicha, beyn, v'hayah, m'tzaveh, b'chol, yoreh, esev, pen, v'hishtachavitem, hashamayim, va'avad'tem, v'samtem, otam, otam, baderech, uvish'arecha, asher, al ha'aretz.</i>
When [a <i>mezuzah</i>] is folded,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">As the Rambam states, the intent is that the <i>mezuzah</i> be rolled. Folding a <i>mezuzah</i> is very undesirable, because it will cause the letters to crack.</i> it should be rolled from the end of the line to its beginning<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Menachot</i> 31b states that a <i>mezuzah</i> should be rolled from<i>echad</i> to <i>Shema</i>. The Rambam uses different terminology because, as stated in the previous halachah, the first line of the <i>mezuzah</i> does not end with <i>echad</i>.</i> so that when a reader rolls it open, he will be able to read from the beginning of the line to the end.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., when one unrolls the <i>mezuzah</i>, the initial word of the line appears first.</i><br>After rolling it, one should place it in a tube made of reed, wood, or any other substance and affix it to the doorpost of one's entrance with a nail.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 289:4) states that one should affix it "with nails." The intent is that the <i>mezuzah</i> should be firmly affixed so that it is not dangling from one side.</i> Alternatively, one should hollow out the doorpost and place the <i>mezuzah</i> within.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">As mentioned in Halachah 8, one should not place it deeper than a handbreadth within the doorpost.</i>
Before affixing it on the doorpost of the entrance, one should recite the blessing:<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">as is done before the fulfillment of a positive commandment. This blessing should also be recited again when affixing a <i>mezuzah</i> which falls from the doorpost. There is a question whether a <i>mezuzah</i> should be recited when affixing it after removing it to have it checked (<i>Pitchei Teshuvah</i> 289:1).</i> "Blessed are You, God, our Lord, King of the universe, who has sanctified us with His commandments and commanded us to affix a <i>mezuzah</i>."<br>One<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Significantly, in <i>Hilchot Berachot</i> 11:12-13, the Rambam states that when one affixes a <i>mezuzah</i> for a colleague, the blessing should conclude, "concerning the affixing of a <i>mezuzah</i>."<br>Although the verses which relate the command to affix a <i>mezuzah</i> state, "And you shall write...."</i> should not recite a blessing when writing [the <i>mezuzah</i>], because affixing it fulfills the mitzvah.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">13 In the listing of the mitzvot at the beginning of these halachot and in <i>Sefer HaMitzvot</i> (positive commandment 15), the Rambam also states that the mitzvah is to "affix a <i>mezuzah</i>."<br>With regard to the blessings, see <i>Hilchot Berachot</i> 11:8, which states:<br>For every mitzvah whose performance fulfills one's obligation, one recites the blessing when one performs it. Whenever a mitzvah has a further commandment involved after its performance, one should not recite a blessing until one performs the latter commandment. For example, when one makes a sukkah, a lulav,... <i>tefillin</i>, or a <i>mezuzah</i>, one does not recite a blessing when one makes them.... When does one recite the blessing? When one dwells in the sukkah, shakes the lulav,... wears the <i>tefillin</i>, or affixes the <i>mezuzah</i>.<br>Note also the comments of the <i>Siftei Cohen</i> 289:1, who writes that the blessing, <i>Shehecheyanu</i>, is not recited before affixing a <i>mezuzah</i>.</i>
If one suspends [a <i>mezuzah</i>] within a pole,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., rather than placing the <i>mezuzah</i> in a tube affixed to the doorpost, one places the <i>mezuzah</i> within a pole which stands next to - but is not permanently affixed to - the doorpost</i> it is unacceptable, because it has not been affixed.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Deuteronomy 6:9 states that a <i>mezuzah</i> must be placed "on your gates." Unless the <i>mezuzah</i> is affixed to the gateway, it does not meet this criteria (<i>Menachot</i> 32b).</i> If one positions it behind the door, it is as if one has done nothing.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Deuteronomy, ibid., states that a <i>mezuzah</i> must be placed "on the doorposts of your houses" and not "within the doorposts" (<i>Kitzur Shulchan Aruch</i> 11:9).<br>The <i>Kitzur Shulchan Aruch</i>, ibid., states that if one fears that the <i>mezuzah</i> will be stolen or defaced, it may be placed within the door. It should, however, be placed on the back of the doorpost, but not on the wall next to the doorpost. (See also Chapter 6, Halachah 12.)</i><br>Should one hollow out the doorpost and place a <i>mezuzah</i> within it horizontally, as the rods were put through the rings [in the Sanctuary],<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">See Exodus 26:26-29.</i> it is unacceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Rather, the <i>mezuzah</i> should stand directly upright. This opinion is quoted by the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 289:6). The Ramah, however, notes the opinion of Rabbenu Tam, who favors the horizontal position, explaining that if it is placed vertically, it is unacceptable.<br>Accordingly, the Ramah suggests affixing the <i>mezuzah</i> at a slant, thereby adhering to both views. This is the common custom at present.</i> Should one place it deeper than a handbreadth [within the doorpost], it is unacceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">This also is not "on the doorposts of your houses."</i><br>Should one cut a reed in half and insert a <i>mezuzah</i> within, and afterwards connect this reed with other reeds, making a doorpost for the house from them,<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Although if the reed with the <i>mezuzah</i> had been affixed to an existing doorpost, it would have been acceptable. In this instance,</i> it is unacceptable, because the affixing of the <i>mezuzah</i> preceded the making of the doorpost of the entrance.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">As the Rambam explains in <i>Hilchot Tzitzit</i> 1:16, Deuteronomy 22:12 states that one should "make fringes" on one's garments. <i>Menachot</i> 40b states that this teaches us that we must make the tzitzit and not use those which are already made.<br><i>Menachot</i> 33b states that the same principle applies here. Since the <i>mezuzah</i> was placed inside the pole before it became part of the doorpost, it is not acceptable.</i>
A <i>mezuzah</i> [placed] on private [property] should be checked<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">at least</i> twice in seven years, and a <i>mezuzah</i> [placed] on public [property]<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">e.g., at the entrance to a courtyard or to a city. (See Chapter 6, Halachah 8.)</i> should be checked twice in fifty years,<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Rashi, <i>Yoma</i> 11a, explains that if more stringent requirements were instituted, it is likely that they would be ignored. Every individual would rationalize that it is somebody else's responsibility.</i> lest a letter have become torn or faded.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">A small crack can render the <i>mezuzah</i> unacceptable.</i> Since it is affixed within a wall,<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">as opposed to <i>tefillin</i>, whose checking is governed by different requirements. (See Chapter 2, Halachah 11.)</i> there is the possibility that it will decay.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">At present, it is customary to check <i>mezuzot</i> more frequently. The ink and parchment we use are different, and there is a greater tendency for letters to fade or crack. Also, there are many scribes whose calligraphy is not professional, and errors which render the <i>mezuzah</i> unacceptable are frequently discovered.<br>[For the above reasons, it is also customary to wrap <i>mezuzot</i> in plastic to prevent the possibility of decay.]</i>
Everyone is obligated [to fulfill the mitzvah of] <i>mezuzah</i>, even women and slaves.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Since <i>mezuzah</i> is a positive commandment whose fulfillment is not limited to a specific time, its fulfillment is incumbent on all Jews.</i> Minors<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">who are exempt from the obligation to perform any mitzvot <i>mid'oraita</i> ("according to Torah law"),</i> should be educated to affix a <i>mezuzah</i> to [the doors of] their homes.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">as part of the Rabbinic command to educate them to perform mitzvot.</i><br>A person who rents a dwelling in the diaspora, and a person who rents<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">It would appear that if one purchases a dwelling - even in the diaspora - one is obligated to affix a <i>mezuzah</i> immediately.</i> a room in a hotel in <i>Eretz Yisrael</i>, are exempt from the obligation [to affix a] <i>mezuzah</i> for thirty days.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in Chapter 6, Halachah 1, a person is obligated to place a <i>mezuzah</i> only on a permanent dwelling. Hence, until this time period has passed, these dwellings are not considered to be permanent.</i> One who rents a house in <i>Eretz Yisrael</i>, however, is obligated [to affix a] <i>mezuzah</i> immediately.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Because of the importance of dwelling in <i>Eretz Yisrael</i>, even a temporary dwelling is of significance (<i>Menachot</i> 44a, <i>Tur, Yoreh De'ah</i> 286).</i>
When a person rents a dwelling to a colleague, the tenant is obligated to obtain a <i>mezuzah</i> and affix it.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Even though the Torah states that a <i>mezuzah</i> must be placed on the entrance to "your house," <i>Bava Metzia</i> 101b states that this refers to the person living within, and not the owner.</i> [This applies] even if he<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">the tenant</i> would pay to have it affixed.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Even if the tenant offers to pay the owner to find a <i>mezuzah</i> and affix it, the owner is not required to accept the offer. The Ramah (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 291:2) adds that even if a person rents a house on the condition that it has <i>mezuzot</i>, and he discovers that they are lacking, he may not withdraw from the rental agreement on the grounds that the agreement was based on a misconception.</i> [The rationale is] that a <i>mezuzah</i> is an obligation incumbent on the person dwelling [in the house], and is not incumbent on the house.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Compare to <i>Hilchot Tzitzit</i> 3:10, which states that tzitzit "are not an obligation on the garment, but on the person who possesses the garment."<br>These statements, however, must be understood in the context of <i>Hilchot Berachot</i> 11:2, which states:<br>There are positive commandments which a person is required to pursue and make every effort to fulfill - e.g., <i>tefillin, sukkah, lulav,</i> and <i>shofar</i>. These are called obligations, since a person is obliged to fulfill them.... There are other mitzvot which are not obligations, but rather are left to the person's volition - for example, a <i>mezuzah</i> and a guardrail. A person is not obligated to live in a house which requires a <i>mezuzah</i> so that he could fulfill the mitzvah of <i>mezuzah</i>. Should he desire, he may dwell in a tent or a ship for his entire life.<br>To summarize, according to the Rambam the mitzvah of <i>mezuzah</i> is incumbent on the person (<i>gavra</i>) and not on the house (<i>cheftza</i>). Nevertheless, it is incumbent upon him only when he lives in a house that requires a <i>mezuzah</i>.</i><br>When [the tenant] leaves [the dwelling, however], he should not take it with him<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">for leaving the house without a <i>mezuzah</i> leaves it open to undesirable influences (<i>Tosafot, Bava Metzia</i>, ibid.). Alternatively, removing a <i>mezuzah</i> may bring harm to the person who removes it (<i>Sefer Chasidim</i>).</i> unless the dwelling belongs to a gentile.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">or will be rented to a gentile. Note the <i>Hagahot Maimoniot</i>, which state that a dwelling which a Jew rents from a gentile does not require a <i>mezuzah</i>. This opinion is not, however, accepted by the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 286:23)</i> In that instance, he should remove it when he leaves.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">lest the gentile desecrate it. Note the comments of the Ramah (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 291:2), who states that it is forbidden to give a <i>mezuzah</i> to a gentile to affix on his door. Nevertheless, if the Jew is frightened that refusing the gentile will generate severe negative feelings, he may leave him the <i>mezuzah</i>.</i>

Chapter 6

There are ten requirements that must be met by a house for the person who dwells within to be obligated to affix a <i>mezuzah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam's choice of phraseology emphasizes the concept mentioned in the previous halachah: that the <i>mezuzah</i> is the obligation of the dweller and not the dwelling.</i> If one of the requirements is lacking, there is no obligation for a <i>mezuzah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">It appears that all these requirements are <i>mid'oraita</i>, postulated by Torah law.</i> They are:<br>a) for the area [of the dwelling] to be four cubits by four cubits or<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">See Halachah 2.</i> more;<br>b) for it<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">the entrance</i> to have two<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Note the <i>Turei Zahav</i> 287:1 and the <i>Kitzur Shulchan Aruch</i> 11:11, which state that if an entrance has a doorpost on the right side and a wall which continues on the left side, a <i>mezuzah</i> should be affixed without a blessing. If the doorpost is on the left side, and the wall continues on the right, there is no need for a <i>mezuzah</i>.</i> doorposts;<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">According to most authorities, the doorpost need not be an addition to the walls of the house. Even if the entrance to the house does not have a frame attached to it, but rather the wall of the house itself serves as the doorpost attached to it, a <i>mezuzah</i> is required. Note the statements of the <i>Turei Zahav</i> 287:1. (See also Halachah 3.)</i><br>c) for it<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">the entrance</i> to have a lintel;<br>d)<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">a beam above the doorposts. According to many authorities, the ceiling of the house is not considered to be a lintel. (See also Halachah 4.)</i> for it<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">the dwelling</i> to have a roof;<br>e)<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">See Halachah 5.</i> for it<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">the entrance</i> to have doors;<br>f)<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">The Ra'avad, Rabbenu Asher, and others differ with the Rambam's opinion. In a responsum attributed to the Rambam, his view is explained as follows: The Torah states that a <i>mezuzah</i> should be placed "on your gates." This expression implies an entrance with doors.<br>The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 286:15) obligates us to place a <i>mezuzah</i> even on entrances which do not have doors. Nevertheless, in deference to the Rambam's opinion, the <i>Siftei Cohen</i> 286:25 states that a person should not recite a blessing when affixing such a <i>mezuzah</i>. (See also Halachah 5.)</i> for the entrance to be at least ten handbreadths<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">If the entrance is not at least this high, it is not fit to be used by adults. (See also Halachah 4.)</i> high;<br>g) for the dwelling not to be consecrated;<br>h)<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">See Halachah 6.</i> for it to be intended for human habitation;<br>i)<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">This principle is not accepted by all authorities. See Halachah 7.</i> for it to be intended to be used for a dignified<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">See Halachah 9.<br><b>j) for it to be a permanent dwelling. -</b> See Halachah 9.</i> dwelling;<br>j) for it to be a permanent dwelling.
A dwelling which is less than four cubits<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">A cubit is approximately 48 centimeters according to <i>Shiurei Torah</i>, and 58 centimeters according to the <i>Chazon Ish</i>.</i> by four cubits does not require a <i>mezuzah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Any smaller area would not be considered fit for a dwelling (Rabbenu Asher).</i> If its area is equal to sixteen square cubits, although it is circular, pentagonal, and needless to say, if it is rectangular,<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Or Sameach</i> explains the advantage of a rectangular shape based on <i>Hilchot Tzara'at</i> 14:6, which states that only a square or rectangular-shaped building is considered to be a house with regard to the impurity of <i>tzara'at</i>.</i> since its area is equal to the above-mentioned figure, it requires a <i>mezuzah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Rabbenu Asher does not accept the Rambam's view. Although the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 286:13) accepts the Rambam's view, in deference to Rabbenu Asher's opinion, the <i>Siftei Cohen</i> 286:23 states that one should not recite a blessing when affixing such a <i>mezuzah</i>.<br>The <i>Merchevat HaMishneh</i> does not agree with the <i>Shulchan Aruch's</i> interpretation of the Rambam's words, and explains that the Rambam is referring to a circular building whose circumference is large enough to contain a square four cubits by four cubits. Compare with <i>Hilchot Sukkah</i> 4:7.<br>As explained in Halachah 8, a <i>mezuzah</i> must be affixed to an entrance which leads to another entrance which requires a <i>mezuzah</i> even if the former entrance would not require a <i>mezuzah</i> in its own right. Accordingly, we must say that this halachah is speaking about an independent structure and not a room in a house.</i>
An excedra, a structure with three walls and a roof,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">An excedra was a very common structure in Greek and Roman architecture that was also frequently employed in Jewish homes. It resembled a porch with three sides enclosed and the fourth side left open. (At times, both the third and fourth sides were left open. See <i>Hilchot Sukkah</i> 4:8,9.)<br>It was covered by a roof which contained an aperture to allow sunlight to enter. Decorative pillars were placed at each of the corners of the aperture.</i> does not require a <i>mezuzah</i> even though it has two pillars on the fourth side.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">According to the <i>Kessef Mishneh</i>, this decision applies even if doors are affixed to the pillars. Though the pillars resemble the doorposts of an entrance, a <i>mezuzah</i> is not required because</i> The pillars are intended as supports for the roof, and not as doorposts.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">When quoting this law, the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 286:6) states that if the excedra has a wall (even if it is low) on the fourth side as well, a <i>mezuzah</i> is required. According to this decision, most porches that have pillars at their entrance require a <i>mezuzah</i>.</i><br>Similarly, a roof without walls<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">If, however, it has four walls, even though large openings are left in them, a <i>mezuzah</i> is required (<i>Shulchan Aruch, Yoreh De'ah</i>, loc. cit.).</i> which stands on pillars, even though shaped like a house, does not require a <i>mezuzah</i>, because it has no doorposts. The pillars are intended<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">primarily, not to serve as doorposts, but</i> to support the roof.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Menachot</i> 33b.</i>
[The following rules apply to] a house which has a doorpost on either side and an arch above the two doorposts instead of a lintel. If the<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">portions of the</i> doorposts<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">which stand straight and are not part of the arch</i> are ten handbreadths high or more, it requires a <i>mezuzah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">since it has both two doorposts of at least ten handbreadths each and a lintel.</i> If they are not ten handbreadths high, [the entrance] does not require [a <i>mezuzah</i>], because it does not have a lintel.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam's phraseology has aroused questions from the commentators. Though all agree that a <i>mezuzah</i> is not required, most maintain that the reason is not that the entrance does not have a lintel - for the arch takes the place of the lintel - but rather because the entrance is not of the required height, ten handbreadths.<br>The <i>Turei Zahav</i> 287:3 explains that were the doorposts to be ten handbreadths high, the archway would be considered as the lintel. Since they are not ten handbreadths high, the archway is considered to be part of the doorposts, and thus, the entrance is considered to be lacking a lintel.</i>
A house that does not have a roof does not require a <i>mezuzah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Although a gate to a courtyard or a city requires a <i>mezuzah</i> even though the area enclosed by its walls is open, a house is different; unless it is covered by a roof, it does not require a <i>mezuzah</i>. (See <i>Yoma</i> 11b.)</i> If a portion of [a building] was covered by a roof and a portion was not, the [following ruling] appears to me<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Throughout the <i>Mishneh Torah</i>, the expression "appears to me" indicates a decision for which the Rambam has no explicit source in the texts of the previous generations.</i> [as appropriate]: If the covered portion is near the entrance,<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">and the covered portion is four cubits by four cubits (<i>Kessef Mishneh; Shulchan Aruch, Yoreh De'ah</i> 286:14). Note, however, the <i>Or Sameach</i>, which does not require the covered portion to be this size.</i> it requires a <i>mezuzah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">See also the <i>Pit'chei Teshuvah</i> 286:13, which states that a part of a house which is customarily built without a roof requires a <i>mezuzah</i>.</i><br>The doors should be attached, and afterwards, a <i>mezuzah</i> affixed.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Though this statement, based on the following passage from <i>Menachot</i> 33a, is accepted by all halachic authorities (See <i>Shulchan Aruch, Yoreh De'ah</i> 289:3), its interpretation has been a source of controversy based on the difference of opinion (see Halachah 1) between the Rambam and other authorities whether an entrance without doors requires a <i>mezuzah</i> or not. The Talmud states:<br>The exilarch built a house. He requested of Rav Nachman: "Affix a <i>mezuzah</i> for me."Rav Nachman told him: "Attach the doors first."<br>The commentaries maintain that, according to the Rambam, Rav Nachman was telling the exilarch that if the doors were not attached before the <i>mezuzah</i> was affixed, it is invalid. Since an entrance without doors does not require a <i>mezuzah</i>, affixing it before the doors would create a problem: as explained in Chapter 5, Halachah 8, the doors must be affixed first. Other authorities explain that Rav Nachman made this statement only because it was necessary to determine the direction the doors would open in order to establish the proper side of the doorway on which to place the <i>mezuzah</i>.</i>
[The gates to] the Temple Mount,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">There was a wall around the complex of the Temple Mount separating it from the remainder of the city of Jerusalem. It had five gates, as described in <i>Hilchot Beit HaBechirah</i> 5:2.</i> its chambers, courtyards,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Within the Temple, there were many different chambers and courtyards; they are described in <i>Hilchot Beit HaBechirah</i>, Chapter 5.</i> and, similarly, entrances to synagogues<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">which are referred to as "sanctuaries in microcosm" (<i>Megillah</i> 29a)</i> and houses of study<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Since students often eat and sleep in a house of study, it is customary to place <i>mezuzot</i> there (<i>Tur, Yoreh De'ah</i> 286). The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (286:10) suggests affixing a <i>mezuzah</i> without reciting a blessing beforehand.</i> which do not have apartments in which people live do not require <i>mezuzot</i>, because they are consecrated.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yoma</i> 11b derives this concept from the exegesis of Deuteronomy 6:9: "And you shall write them on the doorposts of your homes." The Temple is not "your home," a private dwelling, and therefore does not require a <i>mezuzah</i>.<br>The <i>Chatam Sofer</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i>, Responsum 281) asks: Since the Chamber of Parhedrin was the only portion within the Temple Courtyard used as a person's dwelling, why does the Rambam state that these entrances do not require <i>mezuzot</i> because they are consecrated?
