Since they are not used as a dwelling, why would one think they require a <i>mezuzah</i>?<br>The <i>Chatam Sofer</i> explains that the Temple is a dwelling - in fact, the ultimate dwelling, the resting place of the Divine Presence. Nevertheless, since it is not a dwelling for humans, it does not require a <i>mezuzah</i>.</i><br>A synagogue in a village in which guests reside requires a <i>mezuzah</i>. Similarly, a synagogue in a metropolis, if it has an apartment, requires a <i>mezuzah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Nimukei Yosef</i> (<i>Halachot Katanot</i>) explains that synagogues in villages would generally have apartments for guests, because the villagers' homes were usually not large enough to accommodate them. In contrast, in large cities, there were generally enough people willing to invite guests to their homes, and thus it was unnecessary for a synagogue to have a guest apartment.</i><br>All the gates in the Temple complex did not have <i>mezuzot</i>, with the exception of the Gate of Nicanor<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">The central gate to the Temple Courtyard. (See <i>Hilchot Beit HaBechirah</i> 5:5.)<br>The Gate of Nicanor required a <i>mezuzah</i> because it was the gate directly before the Chamber of Parhedrin. As explained in Halachah 8, the gate of a courtyard which leads to a dwelling requires a <i>mezuzah</i>. Based on this rationale, Rav Kapach asks why the gates before the Gate of Nicanor did not require a <i>mezuzah</i>, since they led to a gate which led to a dwelling.</i> and those<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">the other six gates to the Temple Courtyard</i> further within,<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> and others note that the Rambam's text differs from his source (<i>Yoma</i>, loc. cit.), which reads "the Gate of Nicanor and the Chamber of Parhedrin, which was further within." He does explain, however, that the Rambam's version is acceptable: Since all these gates lead to the Chamber of Parhedrin, they therefore require a <i>mezuzah</i>.<br>Rav Kapach supports this interpretation, noting that the Chamber of the Hearth also served as a dwelling for the priests, and hence, the gate to it would require a <i>mezuzah</i>. According to this interpretation, this gate is also included, while according to our text of the Talmud, it is not.</i> and the entrance to the Chamber of Parhedrin,<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">This term means "officer of the king," and was used as a derisive reference to the High Priests of the Second Temple period, who were not righteous and would purchase this position from the ruling authorities for lavish bribes (<i>Yoma</i> 8b).</i> because this chamber served as a dwelling for the High Priest during the seven days<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">Note <i>Yoma</i> 10b, which states that the obligation to place a <i>mezuzah</i> on the gate of Nicanor and the Chamber of Parhedrin applies only during these seven days and not throughout the entire year. Nevertheless, since as stated in Chapter 5, Halachah 11, one should not remove a <i>mezuzah</i> after leaving a dwelling, the <i>mezuzah</i> should remain there (Rav Kapach).</i> when he was separated [from his home in preparation for the Yom Kippur service].<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Yoma</i> 2a, <i>Hilchot Avodat Yom HaKippurim</i> 1:3.</i>
A storage house for straw, a barn for cattle, a woodshed, or [other] storage rooms<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Rav David Arameah notes that there are commentaries who point to a contradiction between these statements and <i>Hilchot Melachim</i> 7:5, where the Rambam states that a soldier who is excused from the battlefield for building a new house (see Deuteronomy 20:5), is released for constructing one of these structures.<br>He explains, however, that the Rambam's phraseology clearly indicates how this difficulty can be resolved. In <i>Hilchot Melachim</i>, the Rambam states that these structures are "fit to dwell in." In this halachah, he states that they are not "set aside for their use."<br>A house requires a <i>mezuzah</i> only when a person dwells in it. Accordingly, since these structures are not used for that purpose, they do not require a <i>mezuzah</i>. To receive an exemption from military service, all that is necessary is to build a house "fit to dwell in." Since it is possible to use these structures for that purpose, the exemption is granted.</i> do not require a <i>mezuzah</i> [as can be inferred from Deuteronomy 6:9, which requires that a <i>mezuzah</i> be placed on] "your homes"<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., a house which is set aside for your use - i.e., for human habitation. This principle is not accepted by all authorities. Based on the opinion of the <i>Tur</i> and others, the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 286:1-2) requires that a <i>mezuzah</i> be affixed to such structures.</i> - i.e., a house which is set aside for your use - thus excluding the above and their like.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">provided they are not also used as dwellings by humans.</i><br>Therefore, [if] a barn [is also used] by women as a dressing room,<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 286:2) emphasizes that if the women stand there naked, it is improper for a <i>mezuzah</i> to be affixed.</i> it requires a <i>mezuzah</i>, since it is used as a dwelling by a human being.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">For this reason, a large walk-in closet requires a <i>mezuzah</i>.</i> A guardhouse, an excedra, a porch, a garden, and a corral do not require a <i>mezuzah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">even if they possess roofs, four walls, and doors. Note the decisions of the <i>Tur</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 286) and other Ashkenazic authorities, which obligate placing a <i>mezuzah</i> on these structures if they conform to all the other necessary requirements.</i> since they are not dwellings.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">for humans.</i> If dwellings which require a <i>mezuzah</i> open up to these structures,<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">Based on the following halachah, this decision would apply even if these structures also lack other requirements a building must have for a <i>mezuzah</i> to be placed upon it.</i> they require a <i>mezuzah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">not only on the door between them and the dwelling, but on an entrance which leads to them from the outside (<i>Shulchan Aruch, Yoreh De'ah</i> 286:8).</i>
Accordingly, gates to courtyards, gates to alleys, and gates to cities<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">e.g., the gates to the old city of Jerusalem</i> and towns,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">even though the areas to which they lead are not covered by roofs and are not dwellings</i> all require a <i>mezuzah</i>, since houses which require a <i>mezuzah</i> open up to them.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yoma</i> 11a relates that the obligation to affix <i>mezuzot</i> on such structures is derived from the inclusion of the word, "And on your gates" (Deuteronomy 6:9 . This implies that even structures which are not themselves "homes" should have <i>mezuzot</i> on their entrances.</i> Even when there are ten structures<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">We have translated the term <i>batim</i> loosely. Manuscript copies of the <i>Mishneh Torah</i> substitute the term "entrances."</i> leading one to each other,<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">For example, a gate to a courtyard opens to a gate to an alleyway, which leads to a further alleyway, which leads to a courtyard....</i> should the innermost one<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">meet the ten conditions mentioned in Halachah 1 and thus</i> require a <i>mezuzah</i>, they all require [mezuzot].<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">because of it.</i> Therefore, [our Sages] stated: A gate which opens up from a garden to a courtyard requires a <i>mezuzah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam rarely mentions references to any source other than the T'nach. In this instance when he does, the definition of the source to which he refers is a matter of question.<br>Most commentaries cite the Rambam's source as <i>Menachot</i> 33b. The passage there, however, speaks of a gate leading from a room to a garden or from a room to a courtyard. The <i>Kiryat Melech</i> cites a reference in Chapter 2 of the tractate of <i>Mezuzah</i>, which describes precisely the situation mentioned by the Rambam.</i>
A toilet, a bathhouse, a mikveh,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">even when used only for the purpose of ritual immersion</i> a tannery,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">which is characterized by a foul smell, since feces are often used in the processing of leather.</i> and the like, do not require a <i>mezuzah</i>, since they do not constitute a dignified dwelling.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yoma</i> 11b explains that this exclusion is implied by the commandment to place a <i>mezuzah</i> on our "homes." Only a dignified dwelling, like a home, requires a <i>mezuzah</i>.</i><br>A sukkah on the holiday of Sukkot,<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">If, however, one dwelled in such a structure for the entire year, a <i>mezuzah</i> would be required.</i> and a house on a ship<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Although the commentaries accept this law in principle, they question the Rambam's source. Among the possibilities offered are the <i>Midrash Tannaim, Parashat Va'etchanan</i>, and the Jerusalem Talmud, <i>Megillah</i> 4:12.<br>The latter source compares <i>tefillin</i> to <i>mezuzah</i> and explains that the mitzvah of <i>tefillin</i> has an advantage, because it is fulfilled by those who travel in the desert or journey upon the sea. From this, one can conclude that the mitzvah of <i>mezuzah</i> is not fulfilled at sea.</i> do not require a <i>mezuzah</i>, for they do not constitute a permanent dwelling.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">The same principle applies to other dwellings of a temporary nature. Based on this principle, the <i>Birchai Yosef</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 286) exempts patients in a hospital or inmates in prison of the obligation to have a <i>mezuzah</i> on their doors.<br>Similarly,</i><br>[With regard to] the two booths of a potter, one inside the other:<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">In Talmudic times, a potter would set up two booths, an inner booth, where he would live and store his belongings, and an outer booth, where he would work and exhibit his wares (<i>Sukkah</i> 8b). The inner booth requires a <i>mezuzah</i>. However,</i> The outer booth does not require a <i>mezuzah</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sukkah</i> (loc. cit.) asks: Although the outer booth is not a dwelling and, therefore, does not, in and of itself, require a <i>mezuzah</i>, perhaps a <i>mezuzah</i> should be affixed because it leads to the inner booth? The Talmud answers</i> because it<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">the outer booth</i> is not a permanent structure.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">and only an entrance of a permanent nature can be considered an "entrance to an entrance" and is required to have a <i>mezuzah</i> (<i>Kessef Mishneh</i>). Note the Ra'avad and Rashi, who interpret the passage from <i>Sukkah</i> differently.</i> Stores in a market place do not require a <i>mezuzah</i> because they are not permanently used as a dwelling.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">Note the <i>Kitzur Shulchan Aruch</i> 11:14, which states that this law applies only to stalls used for business fairs that are left vacant after the fair is over. If, however, merchandise is continually left in a store, a <i>mezuzah</i> is required.</i>
A dwelling<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">or room</i> which has many doorways requires a <i>mezuzah</i> for each and every doorway,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">that meets the ten qualifications mentioned above.</i> even though one generally enters and leaves through only one of them.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Even if a doorway is never used. As long as the potential for using it exists, a <i>mezuzah</i> is required. If, however, the doorway is barred closed so that it will not be used, no <i>mezuzah</i> is necessary.</i><br>A small entrance between a dwelling and a loft requires a <i>mezuzah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">On the surface, this statement, is unnecessary. Clearly, such an entrance would require a <i>mezuzah</i>. Perhaps the Rambam is implying that a <i>mezuzah</i> is required even though the opening lies horizontally in the roof of the house. There is a difference of opinion on this matter, and the <i>Kitzur Shulchan Aruch</i> 11:20 requires a <i>mezuzah</i> only on an entrance that stands upright.</i> When there is a separate room in a house, or even one room which leads to another room,<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">This law reflects how dramatically socio-economic standards have changed. In Talmudic times and in the Rambam's era, a dwelling which had more than one large room was an unusual phenomenon.</i> it is necessary to affix a <i>mezuzah</i> on the doorway to the innermost room, the doorway to the outer room, and the doorway to the house,<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">There is no maximum number of <i>mezuzot</i> in a house.</i> since all of them are used for the purpose of dwelling and are permanent structures.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> states that this law is self-evident, based on the Rambam's statements in Halachah 8. The commentaries explain, however, that the above halachah describes a situation where many entrances require a <i>mezuzah</i> because of another room. In this instance, each of the rooms itself requires a <i>mezuzah</i>.</i>
When a person frequently enters and leaves through an entrance between a synagogue and a house of study<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">which do not require a <i>mezuzah</i>, as mentioned in Halachah 6 above,</i> and his own house, that entrance requires a <i>mezuzah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">because the fact that it is an entrance to one's own house is considered of predominant importance.</i><br>When there is an entrance between two houses,<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">or more particularly, between two rooms in the same house</i> [the position of the <i>mezuzah</i>] is determined by the door-hinge.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">This principle is called <i>heker tzir</i> (<i>Menachot</i> 33a).</i> The <i>mezuzah</i> is placed on the side on which the hinge can be seen.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., the side to which the door opens. This halachah is very significant with regard to contemporary homes, which possess many rooms, and it is necessary to determine the side of the entrance on which the <i>mezuzah</i> should be placed. The Rambam explains that this is determined by the direction to which the door opens: The <i>mezuzah</i> is placed on the right side of the entrance to the room to which the door opens.<br>The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 289:3) quotes the Rambam's decision. The <i>Turei Zahav</i> 289:4 and the <i>Siftei Cohen</i> 289:6 mention two other factors:a) The order in which one enters the rooms from the entrance to the house. The room which is closer to the entrance to the house is considered as leading to the room which is further removed, and the <i>mezuzah</i> is placed on the right side of the entrance to the latter room;<br>b) The importance of the rooms. The room which is less important is considered as leading into the room which is more important, and the <i>mezuzah</i> is placed on the right side of the entrance to the latter room.These principles are also significant if an entrance has no doors or has sliding doors. The above concepts apply only to rooms within a house. If the door leads to the public thoroughfare, the <i>mezuzah</i> is always placed on the right-hand side as one enters the house.</i>
Where is the <i>mezuzah</i> affixed? At the inside of the entrance,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Menachot</i> 32b explains that the Hebrew <i>uvish'arecha</i>, translated as "on your gates," can also be rendered "within your gates." Note the commentary on Chapter 5, Halachah 8, which explains that, when there is no other alternative, the <i>mezuzah</i> may be affixed inside the entrance, on the back of the doorpost.</i> within a handbreadth of the outer edge of the doorpost,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Menachot</i> 33b gives two reasons for this position: a) so that one will encounter God's name as soon as one enters one's home;b) so that the protective influences aroused by the <i>mezuzah</i> will affect a greater portion of the home.</i> at the beginning of the top third of the entrance.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Menachot</i> 33a derives this concept from the fact that the Torah teaches the mitzvah of <i>mezuzah</i> directly after the mitzvah of <i>tefillin</i>. Just as <i>tefillin</i> are placed on the upper portion of one's arm, a <i>mezuzah</i> should be placed on the upper portion of the entrance. (Note the <i>Nekudot HaKessef</i> 289, who objects to this decision.)</i> If it was affixed higher up,<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Note the Ra'avad, who states that when a doorway is very high, the <i>mezuzah</i> should be placed at the height of one's shoulders. (See <i>Siftei Cohen</i> 289:4.)</i> it is acceptable as long as it is at least a handbreadth below the lintel.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Rabbenu Asher and the Ashkenazic authorities maintain that the <i>mezuzah</i> may be placed next to the lintel.</i><br>It must be placed at the right-hand side<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">This law applies even when a house is owned by a left-handed person (Ramah, <i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 289:2).</i> as one enters the house.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yoma</i> 11b explains that the word <i>veitecha</i>, "your house," can also be interpreted, "as you enter," implying that the <i>mezuzah</i> should be positioned as one enters a house or room.</i> If it is placed on the left-hand side, it is invalid.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">It is not considered as if one has fulfilled the mitzvah at all.</i><br>A house belonging to partners requires a <i>mezuzah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yoma</i>, loc. cit., explains that although Deuteronomy 6:9 uses the singular form for "your house," it does not exclude houses belonging jointly to many people.<br>The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> relates that it would have been more appropriate to mention this law at the beginning of the chapter; nevertheless, the Rambam mentions it here because it is derived from the same Talmudic passage as the preceding law.<br>Note the <i>Kitzur Shulchan Aruch</i> 11:19, which states that an entrance which one shares with a gentile does not require a <i>mezuzah</i>.</i>
A person must show great care in [the observance of the mitzvah of] <i>mezuzah</i>, because it is an obligation which is constantly incumbent upon everyone.<br>[Through its observance,] whenever a person enters or leaves [the house], he will encounter the unity of the name of the Holy One, blessed be He, and remember his love for Him. Thus, he will awake from his sleep and his obsession with the vanities of time, and recognize that there is nothing which lasts for eternity except the knowledge of the Creator of the world. This will motivate him to regain full awareness and follow the paths of the upright.<br>Whoever wears <i>tefillin</i> on his head and arm, wears <i>tzitzit</i> on his garment, and has a <i>mezuzah</i> on his entrance, can be assured that he will not sin, because he has many who will remind him. These are the angels, who will prevent him from sinning, as [Psalms 34:8] states: "The angel of God camps around those who fear Him and protects them."<br>Blessed be God who offers assistance.

Chapter 7

It is a positive commandment<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sefer HaMitzvot</i> (Positive Commandment 18) and <i>Sefer HaChinuch</i> (Mitzvah 613) count this mitzvah as one of the 613 mitzvot of the Torah.</i> for each and every Jewish man<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">With this expression, the Rambam excludes women and minors. The <i>Sefer HaChinuch</i> (<i>loc. cit.</i>) explains that although this mitzvah is not associated with a specific time, since women are not obligated to study Torah, they are also not required to fulfill this mitzvah. Note the <i>Sha'agat Arieh</i> (Responsum 35), who objects and obligates women in this mitzvah.</i> to write a Torah scroll for himself, as [implied by the commandment (Deuteronomy 31:19)]: "And now,<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Although this command was addressed to Moses, the fact that the plural form of the word "write" is used indicates that the command was addressed to the entire people.</i> write down this song<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">the song <i>Ha'azinu</i></i> for yourselves,"<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">The above is a quote from <i>Sanhedrin</i> 21b. The Talmud, however, does not explain how this verse serves as a commandment to write a Torah scroll. The following interpretation is the Rambam's.</i> i.e., write down the [entire] Torah which contains this song. [The basis for this interpretation is] that the Torah should not be written passage by passage.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">See Halachah 14.</i><br>Even if a person's ancestors left him a Torah scroll, it is a mitzvah to write one himself.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Sefer HaChinuch</i> (<i>loc. cit.</i>) explains that this was intended so that there would be many Torah scrolls available to allow everyone the opportunity to study. Alternatively, a person will be far more motivated to study in a new scroll which he produced himself.</i> If a person writes the scroll by hand, it is considered as if he received it on Mount Sinai.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">Taking the effort to write the scroll oneself indicates that, had the person lived at the time the Torah was given, he also would have joined the Jews in traveling to Mount Sinai to receive it (<i>Nimukei Yosef, Menachot</i>).</i> If he does not know how to write<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">a scroll according to all the particular halachic requirements</i> himself, [he should have] others write it for him.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., he should hire a scribe or purchase a Torah scroll. <i>Menachot</i> 30a states: "A person who purchases a Torah scroll in the public market is like someone who grabs a mitzvah in the marketplace." Rashi maintains that purchasing a Torah scroll fulfills a mitzvah, but the mitzvah is not as complete as if one had written the scroll oneself. The Ramah, however, states (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 270:1) that if a person merely purchases a Torah scroll without checking it, he does not fulfill the mitzvah at all.<br>Note the statements of the <i>Tevuot Shor</i>, which explain that if another person can perform a mitzvah in a more complete manner than one is able to do oneself, it is preferable to commission him to do so as one's agent. In this instance, since most people cannot write a Torah scroll as attractive and halachically accurate as a professional scribe, it is preferable to hire the latter to write one's scroll.</i><br>Anyone who checks even a single letter of a Torah scroll is considered as if he wrote the entire scroll.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">Unless the scroll is checked, it cannot be used (See Halachah 12). Checking also involves effort.<br>In his <i>Hilchot Sefer Torah</i>, Rabbenu Asher explains that, at present, this mitzvah is also fulfilled by writing <i>chumashim</i>, texts of the Talmud, and other books of Torah law. This concept is also quoted by the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 270:2).<br>The <i>Turei Zahav</i> (270:4) explains that Rabbenu Asher's intention was not that there is no longer a mitzvah in writing a Torah scroll (for it is impossible that a change in circumstance will nullify a Biblical command), but that the original mitzvah has been extended to include these other texts.<br>Since even according to Rabbenu Asher's view, there is a mitzvah for each person to write a Torah scroll for himself, it is difficult to comprehend why we do not see many individuals trying to fulfill this mitzvah. This question is particularly pertinent in light of the Rambam's statements at the conclusion of the list of positive commandments in <i>Sefer HaMitzvot</i>, where he describes the writing of a Torah scroll as a mitzvah which a person is obligated to fulfill.<br>It is possible to explain that since many people are not capable of actually writing a Torah scroll themselves and do not have the financial resources to purchase one and check it, they fulfill this mitzvah through purchasing letters in a Torah scroll written by the community (<i>Pit'chei Teshuvah</i> 270:1; <i>Likkutei Sichot</i>, Vol. 24).</i>
A king is commanded to write another Torah scroll<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sefer HaMitzvot</i> (Positive Commandment 17) and <i>Sefer HaChinuch</i> (Mitzvah 503) count this mitzvah as one of the 613 mitzvot of the Torah.</i> for himself, for the sake of his sovereignty,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">This scroll must be written while the king is in office. If he wrote it beforehand, even if he knew that he would inherit the throne, he does not fulfill this mitzvah.</i> in addition to the scroll which he possessed while a commoner,<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam does not state "which he wrote while a commoner." His choice of phraseology implies a leniency. Were the king to inherit a scroll from his family, he need not write two scrolls (one to fulfill the mitzvah of writing a Torah scroll and one "for the sake of his sovereignty"). Writing a single scroll is sufficient (<i>Kessef Mishneh, Hilchot Melachim</i> 3:1).</i> as [Deuteronomy 17:18] states: "And when he sits on his royal throne, he shall write...."<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Writing this scroll makes the king conscious that there exists an authority above his own (<i>Sefer HaChinuch</i>, <i>loc. cit.</i>).</i> This scroll should be checked against the scroll in the Temple Courtyard<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam's source is the Jerusalem Talmud (<i>Sanhedrin</i> 2:6). A Torah scroll must be checked for accuracy against an existing scroll. There was a scroll kept in the Temple Courtyard for this purpose.</i> by the Supreme Sanhedrin.<br>The one which he possessed while he was a commoner should be placed in his storage chambers,<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">The intent is not that it should be hidden away, but that - in contrast to the scroll he writes as king - it need not accompany him at all times. Rather, like a Torah scroll kept by a common person, it should be kept in a storage closet.</i> and the one that he wrote<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">or had written for him - while he was a king, should be with him at all times. - except in the circumstances mentioned in the following halachah.</i> - or had written for him - while he was a king, should be with him at all times. When he goes out to war, his Torah scroll should be with him. When he returns, it should be with him. When he sits in judgment, it should be with him.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sanhedrin</i> 21b states that the king should "wear the scroll on his arm like an amulet, as it is written, 'I have set God before me at all times. Since He is at my right hand, I will not be budged' (Psalms 16:8 ."</i> When he dines, it should be opposite him,<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">but not with him, lest it become soiled by food.</i> as [Deuteronomy 17:19] states: "And it shall be with him and he shall read it<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">He alone. This scroll is reserved for the king's personal use. None of his subjects may study from it (<i>Tosefta, Sanhedrin</i> 4:4).</i> all the days of his life."
If a king did not possess a Torah scroll before he became king, he must write two Torah scrolls after he ascends the throne: one to place in his storage chambers,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">thus fulfilling the mitzvah incumbent upon every Jew</i> and the other to accompany him at all times,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">fulfilling the mitzvah incumbent upon him as king. Deuteronomy 17:18 explicitly states that the scroll associated with his royal position should be his "second Torah scroll."</i> never leaving his presence except at night,<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Or Sameach</i> suggests amending the text based on the <i>Sifre</i>, which requires the king to have his Torah scroll with him at night (except when sleeping). As support for this change, the <i>Tzafnat Pane'ach</i> quotes <i>Hilchot Melachim</i> 3:5:<br>He should be involved in Torah study and the needs of Israel by day and by night, as it is said: "It should accompany him and he should read it all the days of his life."</i> when he enters the bathhouse, the toilet, or when he sleeps.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">even during the day. Deuteronomy 17:19 states, "It should accompany him and he should read it." <i>Sanhedrin</i> 21b concludes: Where he can read it, it should accompany him; excluding places like those mentioned above, where it is not permitted to recite words of Torah. (See <i>Hilchot Melachim</i> 3:1.)</i>
A Torah scroll which was written on unruled [parchment]<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 1, Halachah 12.</i> or which was written with portions on <i>g'vil</i> and portions on <i>k'laf</i><sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 1, Halachah 7, for a definition of these terms.</i> is invalid.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">because the portions appear as two different scrolls.</i> It must be written either entirely on <i>g'vil</i> or entirely on <i>k'laf</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">As mentioned in Chapter 1, Halachah 8, it is preferable to write a scroll on <i>g'vil</i>. Nevertheless, as explained in the commentary, at present, it is customary to write on <i>k'laf</i>.</i><br>How should a Torah scroll be written? One should write with very careful and attractive calligraphy,<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Shabbat</i> 133b interprets Exodus 15:2: "This is my God and I will glorify Him," to mean "perform mitzvot before Him in a beautiful manner... make a beautiful Torah scroll... with beautiful ink, a beautiful pen, and a skilled scribe."</i> leaving the space the size of a small letter<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">a <i>yud</i></i> between each word<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">On one hand, the words (and similarly, the letters mentioned below) should not be too close to each other lest one be unable to differentiate between them. Conversely, leaving too large a gap between them is not attractive.</i> and a hairbreadth's space between each letter.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">See the conclusion of Chapter 8.</i> The space of a line should be left between each line.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Leaving this space between the lines makes the text easier to read.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">The length of each line should be thirty letters, so that one can write the word למשפחושיכם, the longest word in the Torah. The Rambam and similarly, <i>Menachot</i> 30a, write this world in a full form, containing a <i>vav</i>, and thus containing ten letters. Nevertheless, according to our tradition, the word never appears with a <i>vav</i> in the Torah and thus contains only nine letters.</i><br> The length of each line should be thirty letters so that one can write the word למשפחותיכם three times.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Hagahot Maimoniot</i> states that this is approximately a handbreadth.</i> This should be the width of every column.<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Siftei Cohen</i> (272:3) states, however, that this figure is not a hard and fast rule, and everything depends on the penmanship of the particular scribe. (See also <i>Tosafot, Menachot</i> 30a.)</i> A line should not be shorter than this, lest the column appear like a note;<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam's phraseology differs slightly from his source, <i>Menachot</i>, <i>loc. cit.</i>, which states: "One should not write [a scroll] with many columns [i.e., with short columns], lest it appear like a letter."</i> nor wider than this, so that one's eyes will not wander through the text.<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., a person will become confused which line he is on (<i>Menachot</i>, <i>loc. cit.</i>).</i>
One should not reduce the size of a letter<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., write it narrower than usual</i> in order to leave the proper amount of space between one passage and another.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the following situation. A passage ends in the middle of the line and the next passage is <i>s'tumah</i> (see Chapter 8, Halachah 2). Thus, space for nine letters must be left between the two passages. The scribe should not write the letters narrower than usual to allow him to fit them in the space which is left. Instead, at the outset, he should plan the scroll in a manner in which such difficulties will not arise.<br>The <i>Turei Zahav</i> (273:2) writes that if the scribe does not plan properly and is forced to write narrower letters, the scroll is not disqualified.</i><br>Should [a scribe] have to write a word with five letters [at the end of a line, and there not be sufficient space for them all], he should not write two within the column and three beyond its margins.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Note the <i>K'nesset HaGedolah</i>, which questions whether the scroll is disqualified if written in this manner. From the Rambam's phraseology here and in Halachah 9, it appears that he considers this a preference, but not an absolute requirement. This view is stated in the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 273:5).</i> Rather, he should write three within the column, and two beyond its margins.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Thus, the majority of the word is within the margins.</i> If there is no room on the line to write [at least] three letters, he should leave an empty space and continue at the beginning of the [next] line.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 273:3) articulates the Rambam's thoughts, explaining that the scribes should leave empty spaces rather than elongate the letters (for doing so distorts their shape). At present, the latter practice has, nevertheless, become common.</i>
Should [a scribe] have to write a two-letter word [after completing a line], he should not write it between the columns.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Although one is allowed to write two letters of a five-letter word outside a column's margins, it is not proper to write an entire word there even if it consists of only two letters (<i>Kessef Mishneh</i>).</i> Instead, he should write it at the beginning of the [following] line.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">even though it will cause him some difficulty in spacing out the following line.</i><br>[The following rules apply] if one had to write a word<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">In one of his responsa, the Rambam states that this should not be done with God's name. See <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 276:8).</i> of ten<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">As stated above, according to our tradition, the longest word in the Torah has only nine letters.</i> - or more<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Though there are no words with more than nine letters in the Torah, the <i>megillah</i> contains one eleven-letter word. The same rules that apply to writing a Torah scroll apply in its composition.</i> or fewer<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">letters in the middle of a line, and less space than necessary remained within the column: If it is possible to write half of the word within the column, with [only] half extending beyond the margin, he should. - Though the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 273:4) quotes the Rambam's decision as halachah, the<i>Siftei Cohen</i> 273:4 (based on the opinion of Rabbenu Asher) maintains that one should not write more than two letters outside a column's margins. Significantly, in the laws of <i>tefillin</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 32:33), the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> also quotes Rabbenu Asher's view.</i> - letters in the middle of a line, and less space than necessary remained within the column: If it is possible to write half of the word within the column, with [only] half extending beyond the margin, he should. If that is not possible, he should leave an empty space and continue at the beginning of the next line.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Note the commentary at the conclusion of the previous halachah.</i>
One should leave four empty lines between each of the books of the Torah, neither more, nor less, starting the next book at the beginning of the following line.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Note the <i>Pitchei Teshuvah</i> (273:1), which states that one should not conclude any of the first four books of the Torah on the final line of a column.</i><br>One should complete the entire Torah in the middle of the line at the bottom of the column. If many lines remain in the column, he should write shorter lines,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">The Ramah (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 272:4) states that one may also write the words with taller letters which take up several lines each.</i> beginning at the beginning of the line, but not completing it,<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">This differs from Rashi's interpretation of <i>Menachot</i> 30a, which maintains that one should write the lines in pyramid form.</i> so that the words לעיני כל ישראל<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">These are the final words of the Torah. Concluding in the middle of the line is a clear indication that these are the Torah's final words (<i>Turei Zahav</i> 272:6).</i> are in the middle of the line at the bottom of the column.
One should be careful regarding the oversized letters, the miniature letters,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">According to tradition, each letter appears once in the <i>Tanach</i> in a form smaller than all the other letters, and once in a form larger than all the other letters.</i> the letters that are dotted,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">At times, shapes resembling asterisks are place above letters in the Torah. Each time letters are written in such a manner, several exegetical interpretations are offered explaining the deviation from the norm.<br>The Hebrew word <i>nekudot</i> is also used to refer to the signs which serve as vowels in the Hebrew language. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 274:7) disqualifies the use of a text which includes these signs.</i> the letters that have abnormal shapes<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">e.g.,the pe'in that are bent over, and the crooked letters - See the <i>Chatam Sofer</i>, Responsum 265.</i> - e.g., the <i>pe'in</i> that are bent over - and the crooked letters that the scribes have copied from each other in a chain of tradition.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Rarely are these factors mentioned in the Talmud or the early codes. Rather, traditions regarding these letters were handed down from scribe to scribe.</i><br>[Similarly,] care should be taken regarding the crowns<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., on which letter to place a crown(s)</i> and the number [of crowns placed on a letter].<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">The number of crowns is not uniform</i> There are some letters that have [only] one crown, and others that have seven crowns.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Menachot</i> 29b states that when Moses ascended to heaven, he found God attaching crowns to the letters of the Torah. When he questioned God concerning their purpose, God told him that, in the future, there would be a man (Rabbi Akiva) who would derive mountains upon mountains of laws from each particular crown.<br>There is a serious difference of opinion between the Rambam and Rabbenu Asher regarding the crowns. Rabbenu Asher maintains that crowns should be placed on the letters שעטנ"ז ג"ץ. The Rambam maintains that the letters on which the crowns are placed is a matter of tradition extending back to Moses. In practice, today, it is customary to place crowns on the letters שעטנ"ז ג"ץ at all times, and to place crowns on certain other letters depending on tradition.</i> All these crowns are shaped like<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">tiny</i> <i>zeiynin</i>. They should be as thin as a hair.
All the above matters were mentioned only because this is the most perfect way of performing the mitzvah. Should one, however, alter the structure [of a scroll from that] mentioned above or not be precise regarding the placement of the crowns, [the scroll is acceptable]<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">There is a difference of opinion in this regard between the Rambam and Rabbenu Asher, who maintains that a Torah scroll lacking crowns is disqualified. As explained in the commentary on Chapter 2, Halachah 9, the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> advises adding all the necessary crowns before using the scroll.</i> if all the letters were written as they should be.<br>[Similarly,] if one wrote the lines closer together, separated them further, lengthened them, or shortened them,<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">See Halachah 6 regarding these three factors.</i> the scroll is acceptable, provided one letter does not touch another,<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">See the conclusion of Chapter 8. If the letters touch, they must be separated before the Torah scroll may be used (<i>Shulchan Aruch, Yoreh De'ah</i> 274:4).</i> no letters are omitted, extra letters are not added,<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">See Halachot 11-13.</i> the shape of even a single letter is not altered, and the [form of the passages, whether] <i>p'tuchah</i> or <i>s'tumah</i>, is not changed.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">See Halachah 11 and Chapter 8, Halachah 3.</i>
There are other practices which, although they are not mentioned in the Talmud, have been followed by scribes as tradition, transferred from generation to generation. They include that:<br>a) the number of lines in each column not be less than 48 nor greater than 60;<br>b) there is a space of approximately nine letters left empty between each passage, so that one could write the word אשר three times;<br> c) that the five lines above the song recited at the Red Sea begin with the words: <i>haba'im, bayabashah,</i> י-ה-ו-ה and <i>b'Mitzrayim</i>, and that the five lines below that song begin with the words: <i>vatikach, achareha, sus, vayetz'u,</i> and <i>vayavo'u</i>.<br>d) that the six lines above the song, <i>Ha'azinu</i> begin with the words: <i>v'a'idah, acharei, haderech, b'acharit, l'hach'iso,</i> and <i>k'hal,</i> and that the five lines below that song begin with the words: <i>vayavo, l'daber, asher, hazot, asher</i>.
All the above matters [were mentioned]<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">in the previous halachah</i> only because this is the most perfect way of performing the mitzvah. If one deviated from them, [the scroll] is not disqualified.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">only because this is the most perfect way of performing the mitzvah. If one deviated from them, [the scroll] is not disqualified.</i><br> In contrast,<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">There are more serious scribal errors that can render a scroll unfit for use.</i> if one wrote the short form of a word that should be spelled using a long form, or the long form of one that should be spelled using a short form,<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">As mentioned in the commentary on Chapter 2, Halachah 3, there are times when the Hebrew vowels <i>cholam</i> and <i>shuruk</i> are written with a letter <i>vav</i>, and times when that letter is omitted. Similarly, there are times when the vowel <i>chirik</i> is written with a <i>yud</i>, and times when that letter is omitted.<br>The expression <i>malei</i>, rendered as "full form," refers to the form that includes the extra letter. <i>Chaseir</i>, rendered as "short form," refers to the form that lacks the extra letter.</i> [the scroll] is disqualified.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Although in its present condition, the scroll cannot be used for a public Torah reading, as explained in the following two halachot, the scroll is not necessarily totally disqualified. In certain circumstances, it can be corrected and then used.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">[The same ruling applies if, in circumstances where one word is written in the Torah scroll and a different word is read] There are several instances when, although one word is written in the Torah scroll, a different word is recited when the Torah is read publicly. Both the written text of the Torah and the traditional way in which it is read have their source in the revelation at Sinai (<i>Nedarim</i> 37b).</i><br> [The same ruling applies if, in circumstances where one word is written in the Torah scroll and a different word is read] - e.g.,<i>yishkavenah</i> is read instead of <i>yishgalenah</i> (Deuteronomy 28:30), and <i>uvat'chorim</i> is read instead of <i>uva'folim</i> (Deuteronomy<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">In these instances, both the words share approximately the same meaning; however, the term that is read in public is slightly less harsh than the term actually written in the Torah.</i> 28:27  - one writes the word that is read [instead of the word that is written].<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">See the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 275:6).</i><br>Similarly, if one wrote a passage that should be <i>p'tuchah</i> as <i>s'tumah</i>, or one that should be <i>s'tumah</i> as <i>p'tuchah</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 8, Halachot 1 and 2.</i> or if one wrote another passage from the Torah<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">other than the songs</i> in the form of one of the songs,<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., the song of celebration after the crossing of the Red Sea or the song <i>Ha'azinu</i></i> or wrote one of the songs in the form of another passage,<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">See the conclusion of Chapter 8.</i> [the scroll is disqualified].<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">See the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 275:1-5).</i> It does not have the holiness of a Torah scroll and, instead, is considered as one of the <i>chumashim</i> from which children are taught.<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">In Talmudic times, even children would learn from scrolls. These scrolls, however, could not be used for the communal Torah readings; they have the same level of holiness as sacred texts that are printed today. Compare to Halachah 14.</i>
A Torah scroll<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">The Ramah (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 279:1) quotes the ruling of the <i>Hagahot Maimoniot</i>, who applies this principle to the other books of the <i>Tanach</i> and, also, to other sacred texts, such as the Talmud. (See also Rashi, <i>Ketubot</i> 19b.)</i> that is uncorrected should not be left [unattended to]<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">lest the error it contains cause a person to err regarding a law or Torah concept.</i> for more than thirty days.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">As apparent from <i>Bava Metzia</i> 118a, this is a period that our Sages generally granted to correct various problems.</i> Rather, it should either be corrected<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">as mentioned in the following halachah</i> or entombed.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">as mentioned in Chapter 10, Halachah 3.</i><br>A Torah scroll that has three<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">or fewer</i> errors in each column should be corrected. If it has four, it should be entombed.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">For a scroll with more corrections than this will not be attractive (<i>Menachot</i> 29b).</i> Should the majority of a scroll have been checked to be accurate<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> interprets this to mean that the majority of the letters of the scroll are written properly, even if there are three or more errors in most of the columns. This interpretation is quoted as halachah by the <i>Siftei Cohen</i> 279:4. The <i>Ziv Mishneh</i> differs, and interprets this as meaning that the majority of the columns of the scroll are written properly.</i> and there are four<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">or more</i> errors in each column of the remainder of the scroll, the scroll should be corrected, provided there is at least one column of the defective portion that has fewer than four errors.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Tosafot, Menachot</i>, <i>loc. cit.</i>, emphasizes that this leniency is granted only when the column was written correctly at the outset. If this column also had been corrected, it may not serve as the basis for the correction of the entire scroll.</i>
When does the above apply? When one wrote the short form of a word instead of the long form, and one will thus be forced to insert the [extra] letters between the lines.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">If so many words are written between the lines, the scroll will not be attractive and it is therefore disqualified.</i> If, however, one wrote the long form of a word instead of the short form, one may correct the scroll even if there are many errors on each page. In such an instance, one removes a letter instead of inserting it.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">The Ramah (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 279:4) suggests rewriting the other words on the line with a slightly elongated script, so that an empty space will not be left.</i>
It is permitted to write a scroll containing each of the five books of the Torah individually.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Ketubot</i> 103b, which relates how Rabbi Chiyya wrote five Torah scrolls and gave each one to a different child to learn from, in order to preserve Torah study among the Jewish people.</i> These scrolls do not have the sanctity of a Torah scroll.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">As mentioned in <i>Hilchot Tefillah</i> 12:23, these scrolls may not be used for the communal Torah readings, nor must they be awarded the same degree of respect as a kosher Torah scroll.</i><br>One should not write<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">In one of his responsa, the Rambam explains that this prohibition also applies to embroidering or engraving passages from the Torah.</i> a scroll that contains several passages,<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">See Halachah 1 and commentary which use this law as the basis for the derivation of the mitzvah to write a Torah scroll.</i> nor should one write a scroll<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">containing verses from the Torah</i> for a child to learn from.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Surely, writing passages from the Torah for other purposes is forbidden. In the above-mentioned response, the Rambam criticizes people who write passages from the Torah as amulets or for other similar purposes.</i> This is, nevertheless, permitted if one [ultimately] intends to complete an entire book of the Torah.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ketubot</i> 103b relates that Rabbi Chiya wrote five scrolls, each containing one of the books of the Torah and gave them to five different children to study.<br>Rabbenu Yitzchak Alfasi (in his notes on <i>Gittin</i> 60a) differs, and allows passages from the Torah to be written for instruction. Even though this is forbidden by the letter of Torah law, the Rabbis allowed such scrolls to be written to enable people who could not afford to have an entire book of the Torah written for them to teach their children.<br>The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 283:2) quotes the Rambam's decision, while the <i>Siftei Cohen</i> (283:3) accepts Rabbenu Yitzchak Alfasi's position.</i> It is permitted to write a scroll with [verses from the Torah] when one writes three words in a line spaced out disjointedly.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">Our translation is based on the responsum mentioned above. The Rambam's intent is that each line contains only three words and that no line is positioned directly below (or in any other organized pattern), so that the passage will not appear as a single entity.<br>The source for this law is <i>Gittin</i> 60a, which relates that Queen Heleni had a golden tablet made on which was inscribed the passage for a <i>sotah</i>, so that the priests would not have to copy it from a Torah scroll on every occasion. Because of the prohibition against writing passages from a Torah scroll separately, this tablet was written in the above manner. (Note a somewhat different description of this tablet in the Rambam's Commentary on the Mishnah, <i>Sotah</i> 2:4.)</i>
It is permitted to include [all the books of] the Torah, the Prophets, and the Holy Scriptures in a single scroll.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">See, however, the following halachah, which describes the status of such a scroll.</i><br>Four empty lines should be left between each book of the Torah,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">as stated above, Halachah 7</i> and three empty lines between each book of the Prophets.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Our text of <i>Bava Batra</i>, 13b, which serves as the source for this halachah, appears to indicate that four lines should be left between each book of the Prophets as well. Note, however, <i>Soferim</i> 2:4, which, like the Rambam, mentions leaving only three lines. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 283:1) quotes the Rambam's decision.</i> One should also leave three lines between each book of the twelve [minor] prophets,<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Although they are considered in their entirety as a single book of the Bible, this distinction between the works of each prophet should be made.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Although some of the minor prophets - e.g., Hoshea and Amos (see the Rambam's introduction to the <i>Mishneh Torah</i>) - chronologically preceded Isaiah, because of the size of their books they were included as a unit (<i>Bava Batra</i>, <i>loc. cit.</i>).</i> so that should one desire to cut, he may do so.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Based on <i>Bava Batra</i>, <i>loc. cit.</i>, the <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> maintains that there is an error in the published version of the text and that it should read as follows:<br>One should also leave three lines between each book of the twelve [minor] prophets. [Alternatively, within the works of the prophets,] one may complete [a book] at the end [of a column] and start [the following one] at the beginning [of the following column], so that should one desire to cut, he may do so.<br>Rav David Arameah sees no reason to amend the text, and explains that the space is left between the books, "so that should one desire to cut, he may do so."</i><br>This is the order of the Prophets: Joshua, Judges, Samuel, Kings,<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">The division of Samuel and Kings into two books was first introduced by the Vulgate, the Church's translation of the Bible into Latin.</i> Jeremiah, Ezekiel, Isaiah,<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">Although chronologically, Isaiah preceded Jeremiah and Ezekiel, because of thematic connection, <i>Bava Batra</i> 14b favors the order quoted by the Rambam:<br>The Book of Kings ends with a description of the destruction of the First Temple. This is also the theme of the majority of Jeremiah's prophecies. The Book of Ezekiel begins with the theme of destruction and exile, but concludes with visions of Mashiach's coming. Afterward, it is followed by the Book of Isaiah, which focuses primarily on the Messianic redemption.</i> the Twelve [Minor Prophets].<br>This is the order of the Holy Scriptures: Ruth, Psalms, Job,<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">These three books are arranged in chronological order, according to the opinion that maintains that Job lived in the time of King Solomon.</i> Proverbs, Ecclesiastes, the Song of Songs,<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Rashi, <i>Bava Batra</i>, <i>loc. cit.</i>, explains that King Solomon wrote these three books in this order, completing the Song of Songs in his old age.</i> Lamentations, Daniel, Esther, Ezra,<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">These texts are also in chronological order. According to the Talmud, the books of Ezra and Nechemiah are a single text. (See also <i>Sanhedrin</i> 93b.)</i> Chronicles.<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">which was written by Ezra in Babylon (<i>Bava Batra</i> 15a).</i>
All sacred texts may be written only on a ruled [surface]. [This applies] even if they are written on paper. One may write three words without ruling [the surface on which they are written]. Writing any more than that is forbidden.<br>A scroll that includes the Torah, the Prophets, and the Holy Scriptures does not possess the same degree of holiness as a Torah scroll. Rather, it is like a scroll containing one of the books of the Torah, because the addition [of a book in the scroll] is equivalent to having omitted one.

Chapter 8

There are two forms for a passage<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">The Torah is divided into 669 passages, each containing one or more verses. There are two structures with which they are written: <i>p'tuchah</i> (described in this halachah) and <i>s'tumah</i> (described in the following halachah).</i> which is written as <i>p'tuchah</i>:<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><i>P'tuchah</i> literally means "open." This name is given because an empty space is left on the preceding line.</i> [One form is used] when one completes [the previous passage] in the midst of the line.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">as explained below, leaving an empty space large enough to contain nine letters.</i> Then, one should leave the remainder of the line empty and begin the passage that is <i>p'tuchah</i> at the beginning of the following line.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">All the halachic authorities accept the use of this form for a passage written as <i>p'tuchah</i>.</i><br>When is [this form] used? When the empty space is large enough to contain nine letters.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">In Chapter 7, Halachah 10, the Rambam defines this measure as enough space to write the word רשא three times. In this context, the <i>Beit Yosef</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 275) states that, after the fact, a scroll is not disqualified if a space necessary to write nine <i>yuddim</i> is left open.</i> If, however, the empty space is very small or one concludes [the previous passage] at the end of the line, one should leave one line totally empty and begin the passage that is <i>p'tuchah</i> at the beginning of the third line.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Rabbenu Asher differs, and maintains that, in such an instance, the passage written as <i>p'tuchah</i> should be begun on the second line, but a space large enough to contain nine letters should be left at the beginning of the line.<br>Because of this difference of opinion, the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 275:2) suggests that the scribes structure their text so that the passage that precedes a passage which is <i>p'tuchah</i> always ends in the middle of the line, leaving a space large enough to contain nine letters and one word of the new passage. All authorities agree that this form is <i>setumah</i>. The Ramah adds, however, that if that is not possible, one should write the passage according to the Rambam's decision.</i>
There are three forms for a passage that is written as <i>s'tumah</i>:<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>S'tumah</i> means "closed."</i> [One form is used] when one completes [the previous passage] in the midst of the line. Then, one should leave the above-mentioned amount of empty space<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">The space must be large enough to contain nine letters.</i> and begin writing at least one word of the passage written as <i>s'tumah</i> at the end of the line. Thus, there will be a space in the middle [of the line].<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Rabbenu Asher also accepts this as a proper form for a <i>s'tumah</i> passage.</i><br>When there is not enough room left on the line to leave this amount of space open and write at least one word at the end of the line, one should leave the entire space empty, then leave an empty space at the beginning of the second line,<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">There is a debate among the authorities (See <i>Turei Zahav</i> 32:25; <i>Shulchan Aruch HaRav</i> 32:51) if it is necessary that the space of nine letters be left open on a single line or if it is sufficient that the sum total of the space at the end of the previous line and the beginning of the new line be equal to this measure.</i> and begin writing the passage to be written as <i>s'tumah</i> in the middle of the second line.<br>When one completes the previous passage at the end of the line, one should leave a space of the above-mentioned size at the beginning of the second line and begin writing the passage to be written as <i>s'tumah</i> in the middle of the line.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Rabbenu Asher does not accept the Rambam's opinion on this, and requires different forms in these instances for a passage to be written as <i>s'tumah</i>.<br>The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 275:2) maintains that a scribe should structure his text so that all the passages that precede a passage to be written as <i>s'tumah</i> end at the beginning of a line, so that one can leave the required space and fit in one word of the new passage. The Ramah adds, however, that if that is not possible, one should write the passage according to the Rambam's decisions.</i><br>Thus, a passage written as <i>p'tuchah</i> always begins at the beginning of the line, and a passage written as <i>s'tumah</i> always begins in the middle of the line.
A scroll that has errors regarding the long and short form of letters can be corrected and checked as explained above.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 1, Halachah 16; Chapter 7, Halachot 12-13.</i> In contrast, if:<br>one erred with regard to the space between passages and wrote a passage that should be written as <i>p'tuchah</i> as <i>s'tumah</i>, or one that should be written as <i>s'tumah</i> as <i>p'tuchah</i>;<br>one left an empty space where a [new] passage does not [begin];<br>one continued writing in the normal manner without leaving a space between passages; or<br>one changed the form of the songs,<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam is referring to the form of the song sung after the crossing of the Red Sea, and the song <i>Ha'azinu</i>. He describes their proper form at the conclusion of the following halachah.<br>Significantly, the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 275:3) rules that, although a Torah scroll is disqualified if one writes either of these two songs in the same form as the rest of the Torah, if one writes them in a way that differs both from the usual form of the Torah and the manner in which these songs are customarily written, the scroll is not necessarily disqualified.</i> the scroll is disqualified and may never be corrected.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">In one of his responsa, Rabbenu Asher explains that this is not an absolute statement. The scribe may correct the text by erasing the passage that has been written improperly. Nevertheless, this will not generally be done, because:<br>a) if the passage contains God's name, it is forbidden to erase it; or b) leaving the required space between passages may cause the space for the passages themselves to be too cramped.<br>The Ramah (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 275:1) quotes Rabbenu Asher's opinion.</i> Instead, one must remove the entire column on which it is written.
Since I have seen great confusion about these matters in all the scrolls I have seen, and similarly, the masters of the tradition who have written down and composed [texts] to make it known [which passages] are <i>p'tuchot</i> and which are <i>s'tumot</i> are divided with regard to the scrolls on which to rely, I saw fit to write down the entire list of all the passages in the Torah that are <i>s'tumot</i> and <i>p'tuchot</i>, and also the form of the songs. In this manner, all the scrolls can be corrected and checked against these [principles].<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">In his introduction to the <i>Mishneh Torah</i>, the Rambam outlines the goals he had in composing the text:<br>to compose a work that clarifies... the entire Oral Law, setting it in order without question or difficulty... revealing all the laws to the great and the small regarding each and every mitzvah.<br>The goal of presenting the Oral Law in a form that could be put in practice by every Jew is clearly expressed in these halachot that give precise instructions enabling each individual to compose a kosher Torah scroll.</i><br>The scroll on which I relied on for [clarification of] these matters was a scroll renowned in Egypt, which includes all the 24 books [of the Bible]. It was kept in Jerusalem for many years so that scrolls could be checked from it. Everyone relies upon it because it was corrected by ben Asher,<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">This appears to refer to the scribe Aharon ben Moshe, from the tribe of Asher, who lived in Tiberias in the generation following Rav Sa'adiah Gaon and was renowned for his knowledge of grammar (<i>Shalshelet HaKabbalah</i>).</i>who spent many years writing it precisely, and [afterward] checked it many times.I relied [on this scroll] when I wrote a Torah scroll according to law. The Book of <span class="glossary_item">Genesis</span>יהי רקיע יקוו המים יהי מאורות ישרצו המים תוצא הארץ ויכלו אלה תולדות השמים כולן פתוחות והן שבע פרשיות אל האשה אמר ולאדם אמר שתיהן סתומות ויאמר יי' אלהים פתוחה והאדם ידע זה ספר ויחי שת ויחי אנוש ויחי קינן ויחי מהללאל ויחי ירד ויחי חנוך ויחי מתושלח ויחי למך ויחי נח אחת עשרה פרשיות אלו כולן סתומות וירא יי' אלה תולדת נח שתיהן פתוחות ויאמר אלהים לנח וידבר אלהים אל נח ויאמר אלהים אל נח שלשתן סתומות ויהיו בני נח ואלה תולדת בני נח שתיהן פתוחות וכנען ילד ולשם ילד שתיהן סתומות ויהי כל הארץ שפה אחת אלה תולדת שם שתיהן פתוחות וארפכשד חי ושלח חי ויחי עבר ויחי פלג ויחי רעו ויחי שרוג ויחי נחור ויחי תרח כולן סתומות השמונה פרשיות ויאמר יי' אל אברם ויהי רעב ויהי בימי אמרפל שלשתן פתוחות אחר הדברים ושרי אשת אברם ויהי אברם ויאמר אלהים אל אברהם ארבעתן סתומות וירא אליו פתוחה ויסע משם ויי' פקד את שרה שתיהן סתומות ויהי בעת ההוא ויהי אחר ויהי אחרי הדברים ויהיו חיי שרה ארבעתן פתוחות ואברהם זקן סתומה ויסף אברהם ואלה תלדת ישמעאל ואלה תולדת יצחק ויהי רעב ארבעתן פתוחות ויהי עשו ויהי כי זקן יצחק ויצא יעקב שלשתן סתומות וישלח יעקב פתוחה ויבא יעקב ותצא דינה שתיהן סתומות ויאמר אלהים וירא אלהים ויהיו בני יעקב ואלה תלדות עשו ארבעתן פתוחות אלה בני שעיר סתומה ואלה המלכים וישב יעקב ויהי בעת שלשתן פתוחות ויוסף הורד מצרימה סתומה ויהי אחר הדברים ויהי מקץ שתיהן פתוחות: ויגש אליו ואלה שמות ואת יהודה שלשתן סתומות ויהי אחרי הדברים ויקרא יעקב שמעון ולוי יהודה זבולן יששכר כולן פתוחות והן שש דן גד מאשר נפתלי בן פרת יוסף חמשתן סתומות בנימין פתוחה There are 43 passages that are <i>p'tuchot</i> and 48 passages that are <i>s'tumot</i>, 91 passages in their entirety.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">The first passage in each book of the Torah is not mentioned, since it is governed by different rules (<i>Kessef Mishneh</i>). There is some debate among the commentaries concerning the exact text of the <i>Mishneh Torah</i>. Also, there are different traditions regarding several of these passages. Accordingly, today, a scribe should write a scroll based on a Torah scroll that is accepted as correct, and not from this list.</i>

Chapter 9

A Torah scroll should not be written in a way which causes its length to exceed its circumference, or its circumference to exceed its length.<br>What is the appropriate length? When the scroll is written on <i>g'vil</i>, six handbreadths - i.e., 24 thumbbreadths. When the scroll is written on <i>k'laf</i>, it may be more or less, provided that the length is equal to the circumference. If one wrote a scroll on <i>g'vil</i> less than six handbreadths long and concentrated one's writing, or [wrote a scroll] more than six handbreadths long and spread out one's writing, if the length is equal to the circumference, it has been written in the proper manner.
The following margins should be left [on each column]: Below the column: four thumbbreadths; above the column: three thumbbreadths; and between each column: two thumbbreadths.<br>[To allow for these margins,] one should leave an additional thumbbreadth at the beginning and the end of each portion of parchment and room to sew the parchments together. Thus, when one sews all the portions of parchment together, there will be two thumbbreadths between each column throughout the entire scroll.<br>One should also leave an extra portion of parchment at the beginning and the end of the scroll, to wind around its staves.<br>All these measures are part of [performing] the mitzvah [in the optimum manner]. If one decreased or increased any of them, [the scroll] is not disqualified.
How should a person structure the scroll [he is writing] so that its length will be equal to its circumference? He should begin by making equal portions of parchment, each having a standard width of six handbreadths.<br>Afterwards, he should wind the parchments, each in the same manner, making one tight coil. He should continue adding to the coil, winding the parchments tightly until the circumference of the coil is six handbreadths, the width of the parchment. He should measure with a red cord that [is long enough] to surround the entire coil.
Afterwards, one should make a measuring rod, forty or fifty thumbbreadths long. Each thumbbreadth on the rod should be divided into halves, thirds, and quarters, so that it will be possible to have exact measurements, including even half and quarter thumbbreadths.<br>One should measure each piece of parchment with this rod to determine its length in thumbbreadths. In this manner, one can calculate the length in thumbbreadths of the entire coil.
Afterwards, one should take two or three other parchments [as an experiment] to check the size of one's writing. One should write a [sample] column.<br>It is obvious that the length of the column [used for the writing] will be seventeen thumbbreadths, since three thumbbreadths are left for a margin above the column and four thumbbreadths are left for a margin below. The width of the column, however, varies according to the thickness of one's writing. Similarly, the number of lines within each column varies according to the writing. The space of a line should be left between each two lines.
After one writes the experimental column as one desires, one should measure the column with the rod. Then one should add the two thumbbreadths to be left between columns and calculate the number of columns one will have in the entire coil [should one continue] using the same [size] writing.<br>Once one knows the number of columns [for which there is space in the coil], one should calculate according to the scroll from which one is writing whether the entire Torah will be able to be contained in the number of columns there are in the coil based on this [size] writing.<br>If the entire Torah can be contained within this number of columns, [the scroll] will be [written in the] desired [fashion]. If, according to one's calculations, there are more columns than necessary to contain the Torah, one should write with a broader script, so that fewer columns will be included. One should write another [experimental] column [and recalculate until the calculations are resolved].<br>If, according to one's calculations, there are fewer columns than necessary to contain the Torah, one should write with a thinner script, so that more columns will be included. One should write another [experimental] column and recalculate until the calculations are resolved.
After one knows the width of the column and the measure of one's writing, one takes the coil [of parchment] and divides each parchment into columns according to the [size of the] column with which one experimented and made the [above] calculation, ruling each column. When more than three or four fingerbreadths remain after the final column [which fits onto] the portion of parchment, one should leave only a thumbbreadth and the space necessary to sew it and cut off the rest.<br>One need not worry that, ultimately, additional parchments will have to be added to the coil to compensate for the portions which were cut off. This is not a factor of circumstance, because the writing will cause [the size of the scroll] to be extended [only] according to the number of columns [originally calculated].
Similarly, if one desires to make the width of the scroll more than six [thumbbreadths] or less than six [thumbbreadths], one should follow similar calculations. Thus, the length of the scroll will be equal to the circumference, neither less nor more, provided one does not err in his calculations.
The thumbbreadth mentioned in all these calculations - and in all other Torah measurements - refers to the width of a normal person's thumb. We have calculated this measure precisely and found it to be equal to the width of seven average barley-corns when placed next to each other in a cramped manner. This is equivalent to the length of two barley-corns amply spaced apart.<br>Whenever the term "handbreadth" is mentioned, it refers to four of these thumbbreadths. Whenever the term cubit is mentioned, it refers to six handbreadths.
In the Torah scroll which I wrote, the width of each column was four thumbbreadths [with the exception of the] columns on which the Song of the Red Sea and the song <i>Ha'azinu</i> were written; they were six thumbbreadths wide. There were 51 lines in each column and 226 columns in the entire scroll. [In its entirety,] the scroll was approximately 1366 thumbbreadths long.
The six thumbbreadths beyond the number that one would arrive at by calculation were used for the margin of the scroll left at the beginning and the end. I wrote the scroll from parchment made from ram skin.<br>Should one desire to write a scroll using these measurements or deviating from them only slightly - i.e., adding or subtracting two or three columns, there is no necessity to trouble oneself with calculations; without any difficulty one will have a scroll whose length is equal to its circumference.
One should not write fewer than three columns on a piece of parchment, nor [should one write] more than eight columns. If one has a piece of parchment large enough to contain nine columns, one should divide it in two, one portion containing five columns, and one portion four columns.<br>When does the above apply? At the beginning of the scroll or in the middle. At the end of the scroll, however, even if there is one verse in one column, that column may be written on a separate piece of parchment and sewn together with the other parchments.
When one sews the parchments together, one should use only sinews from a kosher species of animal or beast. [Sinews taken from] animals which died without being ritually slaughtered or which were killed by wild beasts [are, nevertheless, acceptable].<br>This is a halachah transmitted to Moses on Mount Sinai. Therefore, if one did not sew them with sinews, or used sinews from a non-kosher animal, the scroll is unacceptable until one removes the threads and sews them again.
When sewing all the pieces of parchment together, one should not sew the entire length of the parchment.
